BEFORE THE MIRROR
By “JEANNETTE.’'
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
air of us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women, and while perhaps a few—the mere
stoical among us—can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we
may all, with a little care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results
of worry from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet matters, and may be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of face and figure which is the birthright of
every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present, not
generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Some Hints about the Hair. —An Excellent
Shampoo.—Few women realise liow all-impor-
tant a really good shampoo is if the hair is
to he kept in perfect condition. To shampoo
the hair properly, slnrke it down, comb it out,
then dip the lingers into a little pure olive or
almond oil, and thoroughly massage the
scalp. Then mix a teaspoonful- of stallax
granules in a cup of warm water. Fill a basin
with warm water, into which the ends of the
hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax mix-
ture into the roots in the usual way. Rinse;
and dry by fanning, or in tlie open air, if the
weather permits. Should the hair be excep-
tionally greasy, the oil massage of the scalp
should be omitted. Stallax can .be bought at
any chemist’s in quarter-pound sealed pack-
ages. It. keeps indefinitely, and one package
is sufficient for 23 or 30 shampoos.
A Tonic for Thin or Falling Hair.—When the
hair falls unduly, or lias become thin and im-
poverished, a tonic should he applied every
night for six or eight weeks. To make a
simple and inexpensive but most efficacious
lotion, at home, take a quarter of a piut of
hay rum and add to it one ounce of boranium.
Shake thoroughly, and leave for 30 minutes;
then strain, and add sufficient water to make
half a pint. Every night massage the scalp
for 30 minutes, going over the entire head,
then dab the mixture into the. roots with a
soft sponge. Regular brushing for a few
minutes every morning will also help to im-
prove the condition of the hair.
A Grcasv Skin and Jllackheads. —A greasy,
oilv skin, with blackheads and enlarged
pores, is a sore trial, and many a girl goes
through life with her appearance entirely
spoiled with these unsightly blemishes, which
may now be so quickly and effectively removed
with but little trouble. To remove blackhead*
and the dull, dirty look of. the skin which
accompanies them, dissolve one sty mol tablet
in a glass of hot water. Allow the efferves-
cence to subside, then dip a small soft sponge
into (lie liquid and bathe the face. Leave o»
the skin for a few minutes, then dry with a
towel, and the blackheads will come right off.
For a greasy skin, bathe the face three times
a week with stymol and spray with cold water
every morning, using a vulcanite throat spray.
The Secret of a Good Complexion.—The secret
of a good complexion is so simple that, were
it only more widely known, there would be
no such thing as a rough, blotchy skin.
Nature has decreed that the outer cuticle,
when it. shrivels and dies, shall lie removed;
but so many women fail to realise this. To
remove, by absorption, the dead outer skin,
and preserve the complexion in all its pris-
tine beauty, mercolized wax is recommended,
ordinary creams and lotions being quite in-
effectual for this purpose. Smear the wax
over the face and neck, rut) it gently into the
skin, and leave for the night. In the morning
wash it off, using a good soap, such as
Pilenta. and all the dead skin will be removed
with (lie wax. Then apply a lotion to re-
move any, trace of greasiness and brace up
tbe skin, making it firm and white. The lotion
can bo made at home quite easily, by mixing
one ounce of elominite in four tablespoonfule
of hot water. Bottle, and. when cool, apply
to the face with a soft sponge, or the tips of
the lingers, allowing it lo dry on the skin.
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
berries will reduce your weight.
They are harmless, and no alteration need be
made in your diet. They have also one great
advantage over other flesh - red u cers: they re-
duce so gradually that there is no fear of
loose, flabby skin and wrinkles.
ZULA.—- No, I am afraid I cannot tell you how
to prevent yourself from biting your nails.
You must exercise your will power.
CONSTANCE.—To remove tbe yellowness
from your skin, get a tin of mercolized wax
and apply it every night, nibbing it well into
the skin. Leave it on till morning; then wash
it off with warm water and a good soap. (2)
To give a color to the cheeks, get a little col-
liandum and rub some on a leather. Apply tins
to the cheeks. It gives a perfectly _ natural-
looking flush to the face, and is quite unde-
tectable. (3) To remove the blackheads, take
a stymol tablet and dissolve it in warm water.
When the effervescence has subsided mop the
lotion over the face, allow to remain on f»r
a few minutes, (lien wipe off, and the black-
heads will disappear.
(iEOB<*IE.—=I tun not surprised to hear that
the electrolysis treatments were not succe*-
ful. They seldom are. Get an ounce of phe-
minol and a tube of tekko paste. Use them in
conjunction, and you will soon be rid of the
troublesome superfluous hair.
SHEILA. —To restore your hair to its original
color, get two ounces of tammalito and mix it
with three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair every night or morning. In a couple of
weeks there will be no sign of the greyness.
Tammalito is not a dye, but a natural re-
storer.
ELSIE.-- To remove the roughness from your
skin, use a lotion made by mixing cleminite
with warm water. Allow the lotion to cool,
I hen bottle, and use after washing the face
every morning. U

The Red Page
Our Reptile Contemporary
I have before me a copy of our reptile
contemporary, the N.S.W. Government
Gazette, a struggling publication which
comes out occasionally. It has little to
recommend it. The style is formal, dry and
discursive. There is nothing continuous or
consistent about its argument. The lack of
lire, imagination, sentiment, high resolve and
poetic feeling is absolute. In fact, there is
no poetry whatsoever. No soldier could read
it in the; trenches and feel better for its peru-
sal. It isn't calculated to soothe anybody’s
declining years. It fails even to * awake
healthy indignation. The illustrations, con-
sisting of two animals on their hind legs, are
commonplace.
There is a great waste of pomp ami paper
and circumstance about our loathsome con-
temporary. These are not times for paper-
wasting or pomp-wasting. The two animals
should be cut out. The heading in four big
lines, “Government' Gazette of the State of
New South Wales, Published by Authority,”
should be condensed to “New South Wales
Government Gazette” in one line. If any-
one wants to say that N. S. Wales isn’t*a
State, it is up to him to prove it in the High
or Petty Debts Court. Or if anyone doubts
that the Gazette isn’t published by author-
ity, he can always get counsel’s opinion at
his own expense provided he approaches coun-
sel properly through a solicitor.
Next to its non-committal policy on the
great questions of the hour and its lack of
cablegrams, the feature of the effete and
time-serving contemporary is its circumlocu-
tion about persons in high places. One short
notice re the shop-closing hour on Wednes-
days and Saturdays in a town of ”8”> people
contains these superfluities :
PROCLAMATION.
New South I By his Excellency, Sir Walter
Wales J- Edward Davidson, Knight Comman-
to wit. J der of the Most Distinguished Order
, of Saint M-ichael and Saint George,
Governor of the State of New South
IV. 13. Davidson, Wales and its Dependencies, in the
Governor. Commonwealth of Australia.
Whereas under Section 4 of the Early Closing
(Amendment) Act, 1900 ....Now therefore, I Sir
Walter Edward Davidson, the Governor aforesaid,
with the advice of the Executive Council. . . .the
first day of August, in the year of our Lord One
thousand nine hundred and eighteen....
Given under my hand and the seal of the State
this twentieth day of June, in the year of our
Lord One thousand nine hundred and eighteen, and
in the ninth year of his Majesty’s reign.
By his Excellency's Command,
G. S. Bbeby.
GOO SAVE THE KING,
And it could all be put like this :
PROCLAMATION.
The Shadow of the King says:—
Under Section 4, Early Closing Amendment Act,
1900,... .August 1, 1918.
20/6/1918. G. S. Beery,
Twelve times in one issue God is asked to
save the King, once in a matter relating to
garbage, once re fish, once re a public holiday
at Gulargambone, and nine times over other
affairs. It seems to me that the profane and
degraded contemporary shouldn't throw the
name of the Almighty about in this silly and
profuse fashion, nor should it imply that the
King requires such a lot of saving. It might
be taken for granted that the King isn’t lost,
and that N. S. Wales is still in the Common-
wealth of Australia until someone sees it
stealing away, and then the business-like
idea is to run after it and drag it back by the
hair. .Just here it may be mentioned that it
is a meutally-deficient sort of contemporary
which declares three dozen times that N. S.
Wales is a State and then describes its
Treasurer as “Colonial.”
The perpetual “Advice of the Executive
Council” is sheer hypocrisy. Only a worm-
like contemporary would descend to such a
subterfuge. Often half the Council isn’t
there, and half of what is there doesn’t take
the trouble to open its mouth or its ear about
the fixing of a half-holiday at Mumble-bone.
Put truthfully, it might read
... ..With the advice of such of the Executive
Council as happened to be present and was taking
notice of the proceedings, one member having gone
to England at the public expense, one leaving all
his time occupied doing a recovery! one having been
seen leaving town in a , motor-car with a minor
actress, and a member without portfolio, who
doesn’t really matter, not having been seen at all
for two days.,,.
My idea is that in the interests of truth,
peace and economy the “Executive Council”
formality should be dropped altogether.
This derelict contemporary is, as already
hinted, without religion (apart from its hoarse
demand that God wilhsave the King), with-
out visible political principles, without art
or poesy or even the Higher Criticism. To
it a primrose by the river’s brim is merely a
vegetable growing on portions 81 and S 2 in
the parish of Bulalgee, licensing district of
Bobundera and Cowwallop. It never wastes
a chance to make three soulless lines serve
where one would do by spelling out dates to
the bitter end. and mentioning the county
with the longest name as often as possible, by
sometimes calling Fitzpatrick “Minister for
Mines” and sometimes “Secretary for Mines”
and sometimes not calling Ball anything at:
all. And the chances it misses are pathetic.
Even in its own dull rut It might brighten
things up when it advertises the new sheep
or bull reserve at say Jemalong, if it threw
in a brief summary re scenery, if any, which
there probably isn’t in a place with
a name like that; also, re noted bush-
rangers, mixed criminals, suicides and
ghosts thereabouts, local hangings, and
the illegitimate birth-rate of the surround-
ing districts. And the temporary appoint-
ment of Jones vice Smith and Brown vice
Robinson; also the resignation of Sclimidt-
etein and Boffin and .Jobb would be made
interesting if it ‘was explained that Smith
was away waiting till things blew over : that
Robinson was lying low on the original
Crown grant of Briggs, in the parish of
Hyandra, land district of Dubbo and Molong.
on account of a divorce summons; that it
had required three policemen to intern
Sclmiidtstein and one of them was badly
bitten ; that Boffin had fled . with the office
cash and another officer’s wife; while Jobb
(Continued on vet-ffe 26.)

CHALLENGE WINDMILLS
THE MAN ON THE LAND has OFTEN to
LEAVE the FARM WORK he is doing to RE-
PLENISH the HOUSEHOLD WATER supply. The
ACTUAL operation of PUMPING daily from a
WELL to the HOUSE TANK does not take up a
GREAT DEAL of tifne, but in the AGGREGATE it
represents HUNDREDS of WASTED HOURS
YEARLY. He should install a CHALLENGE
WINDMILL to do the pumping for him. The TIME
SAVED would be REFLECTED in the MONETARY
RETURN from his farm. The UP-TO-DATE MAN
uses a CHALLENGE WINDMILL and CONTINU-
ALLY SOUNDS its PRAISES.
The CHALLENGE CONSTRUCTION is the
EMBODIMENT of SUPERIOR MECHANICAL
ELEMENTS. BROUGHT TOGETHER for the first
time in ONE UNEQUALLED COMBINATION;
and PRODUCING A WINDMILL of EXCEP-
TIONAL MERIT.
Briefly stated, these elements consist of an
INTERNAL GEAR, CENTRE ONE-PIECE
CRANK. ROCKER ARM, SPLIT BOXES, with
detachable, reversible BABBITT BEARINGS,
interchangeable with graphite. SIDE VANE and
MULTIPLYING SPRING GOVERNOR.
The internal type of gear is recognised by
mechanical engineers as superior to an external gear
in at least two respects. First, twice as many cogs
in mesh. Second, noiseless. These features secure
strength, long life and satisfaction—all that is obtain-
able in gear transmission.

RELIABLE OHIO ENSILAGE COTTERS
The OHIO No. 11 CUTTER and BLOWER, as shown, will DEAL with ALL
KINDS of DRY FEED, as well as ENSILAGE, at the RATE of from 3 to 5
TONS per HOUR. The model shown, the plain-table type, although a POWER-
FUL machine, requires but sor 6 h.p. to drive it. The OHIO No. 11 is STRONG
and DURABLY built, and is fitted with two knives of high carbon steel which retain
their cutting qualities. OHIO BLOWERS ELEVATE MORE ensilage per
horse-power than any other blower. The reason is plain. The same shaft that
receives the power from the engine carries the cutting knives and the blower fan.
No. 11 Cutter and Blower, with two 11-inch knives, cuts £, \ and 1 inch. £2B

with Blower and Hood.

The OHIO No. 13 STANDARD FEED and ENSILAGE CUTTER is also
a NECESSITY on an UP-TO-DATE FARR. The good points which a farmer
looks for in such a machine are these—STRENGTH, DURABILITY and SATIS-
FACTORY OPERATION. The OHIO No. 13 contains all the latest IMPROVE-
MENTS, and is in EVERY WAY RELIABLE. From 4 to 6 tons of ensilage or
from 2500 to 3500 pounds of dry feed can be cut per hour. By substituting
shredder blades for the cutting knives, perfect shredding may be' accomplished.
With two knives it cut i, 1, 11, and 2 inch, and }, 1. | and 1 inch with four
knives. The speed is from 450 to 600 revolutions per minute. The train of
feed-gear pinions, clearly shown in picture, vibrate as the feed roller raises and
lowers, yet their movable bearings maintain the respective distances to each other
and keep the gears properly in mesh without friction, which gives a uniform motion
to the roller. The backwardly-inclined motion of the upper roller, as it raises,
assists in taking hold of the feed, and this, combined with other
good points, makes these machines strong feeders. Price ...

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring Song—Variation.
There’s a voice in my heart whispering low,
There’s a wound in my soul that won’t heal;
There’s a something gone from me, a
preciousness sped,
A perfect soft music I miss as I go
With the other old dogs that are lonely and
feel
Regret for the days that are dead.
English lanes where the cowslips grew,
Dairymaids with plump cheeks a-bloom,
Pleasures of springtime and blossomy May,
Woodland, walks with the imp, Nell Grey
(Sweet to look at, but tricky to woo),
Cosy suppers in Grandmamma's room:
Nell again (a good memory this.'),
Tremulous under a boy's first kiss.
There’s a stir of Spring in the air,
A lilt of new mirth in the sea,
And out of my heart that is haunted by care,
From depths of my heart that are barren
and bare,
A delicate music comes back to me
And sings- of the days that were.
Tanjong Katong where skies are blue
And palms lisp down to the shining strand,
Ripples of green on the China Sea,
Java nights when youth stayed with me,
Pines that keep guard over Khatmandu.
Srinagar gemming a, wonderland,
Kinchinjvnga aloof and cold,
Old Rangoon with its mosque of gold.
So what does it matter at last?
Why can’t one grow old and be gay?
There are plenty of things for a codger to do
As the jolly old world winks past.
There are lips ’twixt the cup and the slip,
they say,
And one may live though the Fates boo-hoo/
George-street brave on a Saturday noon,
And Pitt-street frothing with frills and lace.
Red-lipped girls with the deep, deep eyes
That stir old age to new enterprise.
Pan who lingers to chuckle and croon
From, the dimple sweet of a roguish face.
Spring in the pageant and Spring in the air:
And Spring in my heart.... So what do I
care!
N.S.AV.
FRANK MORTON.
Getting Through.
I lay in sleepy wonder looking round
One day when Spring had decked the world
with green ;
It seemed as though all Nature’s voice had
been
Merged into one low-swelling, throbbing
sound
(The noise of bees, of insects on the ground,
The drowsy songs of birds singing unseen),
When suddenly a lark’s pure song serene
Filled all the air with ecstasy profound.
And so the drone of prayer clings to the
earth
In one low cadence' like the murm’ring sea
Which undulates beneath a summer sky,
Until some soul gives travail-maddened birth
Unto a concentrated prayer that he
Sends, like that lark’s song, to the Power on
high.
VICTORIA.
A. B. C.
Prayer and Curse.
Plumed and black through the golden noon
Goes a hearse to the burial-place;
Step for step with nodding heads
Sable horses slowly pace.
To and fro the people go,
Ceasing not from talk and task;
Who goes there and how he died
No one cares enough to ask.
Women on the pavement stand.
Watching while he rides in state ;
Women twain, with eyes that shine,
One with love and one with hate.
“Take my hatred,” mutters one;
“You have earned it, earned it well.
God be praised ! When life is done
There is Hell!”
Plumed and black through the golden noon
Hearse and horses onward move;
Neither do they bolt for hate,
Neither shall they stay for love.
To and fro the people go
Giving death no thought at all;
Women twain, of all that host,
Watch the noonday funeral.
As it passes, murmurs one:
“Take my love....’tis freely given.
God be praised ! When life is done
There is Heaven!”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Good-bye.
There can be nothing new in our Good-bye.
The platform with its testy, jostling crowd ;
Impetuosity in Custom’s shroud
Severely straiked and bound —just you and I.
Convention, shuttering Love’s lantern gleam ;
Fate, like a porter, shrieking “terminus!”
For the swift golden journey of a dream
And.... just a stumbling in the dark for us.
There shall be nothing new in our Good-bye—
A little quieter, belike, than most;
For we must part in silence, you and I,
Lest whispering near graves should raise a
ghost.
You to go north, and I to stay behind
And wave adieu, and flaunt a careless brow
(Some memory roses in with rue to bind),-
And wonder why all roads are empty now.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 10s. per annum, you can have Tiie Bulletin
sent regularly to you every week for a year, to any
address. in Australasia, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be- understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 5, 1918.
THE GENERATIONS THAT WERE
BORN IN HELL.
It is a common boast among the strongly
pro-Hun elements of the Australian Labor
party that the party has sent a larger pro-
portion of its members to the war than any
other political crowd. If it is so, then the
proportion which didn’t go to the war is
smaller, and less representative, and less
entitled to speak for the body in general
than any other stay-at-home remnant. This
possibly accounts for the transformation of
Labor, if it can be judged by the talkers at
the top of things, from a strongly patriotic
body with a national spirit into_ a virulently
pro-Hun institution with an international
and Bolshevik and I.W.W. craze. It is a
case of the smallest tail here usurping the
place of the largest dog that is temporarily
absent. Anyhow it .has been decided that
this is a good time for a referendum. The
proposal is that the party should give up
recruiting (which its leaders have done
already) unless the British _ Government
asks the Hun for peace, promising him all
his colonies back, and no fines on account of
the £2,000,000,000 or so of expense that his
wickedness has caused. He may be called
upon to repair actual breakage of property,
but that isn’t certain; what is proposed is
that he certainly won’t be called upon to
pay any more than that. It being a friendly
arrangement on equal terms, the German, the
Austrian, the Bulgarian and the Turk will,
of course, sit at the council table and help
to assess the breakage. The Hun’s new
client kingdoms—Poland, Finland, Courland,
etc. —will doubtless also claim to be repre-
sented. On the other hand, such small out-
lying Allies as Bolivia, Guatemala, Ecuador,
Haiti, etc. —will presumably be ruled out
because they only contributed their good-
will. Brazil sent a fleet: and Cuba is pre-
paring an army, so they may, or may not,
have a voice. Anyhow, if the Australian
Government interferes in any way with the
holding of the referendum (the leaders will
decide what is interference) the said leaders
will declare the vote carried. They appar-
ently have the right to do this even if the
interference doesn’t alter the result of the vote
but merely creates annoyance (to them).
They already interfere by printing on the
ballot papers a recommendation to vote
against recruiting. Perhaps this is meant to
arouse the Government to action under the
law. As the thing is being done in rather a
hurry, a large section of the Laborites who
showed themselves in favor of recruiting by
going to the war—especially those in hospi-
tals or Hun prisons—will be disqualified.
It won’t make much difference to the
nations in the battle-zone —those that have
been through Hell. They know a thousand
times more about the affair than even our
men at the Front. Every man and woman
aged under 48 who was born in France or
Italy or Belgium or Russia or Servia was
born in Hell and grew up there. Their lives
have been a race of armaments, a carnival of
expense, and a terror of war. And always
the Hun set the pace. There were great
armies in previous days, of course. When
Napoleon I. had a sort of madness coming
upon him he raised nearly 600.000 men for
his very greatest effort, though he drew them
from at least half a dozen nations. Wallen-
stein, who was a sort of inspired madman,
collected over 100,000. In the bitterest days
of the U.S. Civil War Lincoln called for
300,000 new soldiers in one act. Britain
once, in the days of the great struggle with
France, had 300,000 under arms at once.
Even the idea of a permanently-armed nation
was familiar before the Hun arose. It was
recognised that though a big nation might be
content to arm and train one capable man
in 100, yet a very, very small one like Monte-
negro must train every man, for every possi-
ble enemy was at least 20 times its size.
But the Hun invented the idea of keeping big
nations permanently and wholly under arms.
Also, lie invented the idea of keeping weapons
always up to date. It had been a spasmodic
business before his time. The new Macedo-
nian spear, with the phalanx to wield it
and good leadership to direct it, made an
empire. The English long-bow (as distin-
guished from the previous short-bow and
cross-bow) made its debut on a big scale at
Crecy (1346), and so slow was the re-
arming process that until Flodden (1513) it
was the decider of battles. Only wonderful
commanders like Robert Bruce and Ber-
trand du Guesclin could argue against it.
Europe was just thinking, about 1864, of
scrapping the friendly old muzzle-loader of
Waterloo and before Waterloo. A practic-
able breech-loader had been invented by an
Englishman in 1859, but the Prussian
snatched the idea, and with it he
swept away the Danes in 1864 and
the Austrians in 1866, and put the fear
of death into Britain and France. France
hurriedly bought a breech-loader and tried to
turn its easy-going, mostly-voluntary army
into a big compulsory force. But it was
wrecked in 1870, when it was in the middle
of the stream changing from an old horse
that was worn out to a young one that was
not yet trained.
After that life became a life-and-death
race for every nation that was within the
Hun’s reach and which had anything to
lose. Two million soldiers were wanted
where 100.000 formerly looked sufficient. Two
million expensive rifles had to be scrapped
every few years to make place for better ones,
where 100,000 cheap blunderbusses were for-
merly melted down once in a half century
or so. As much money was spent on new
artillery in a year as would formerly have
sufficed for. a century. Things moved so
fast that in the Cuban war of 1898 three
generations of firearms met. The bulk of
the Spaniards, armed with the rifle of the'
day before yesterday, went down like grass
before the U.S. soldiers with the rifle of
yesterday. - But at El Caney, a mere few
hundred Spaniards armed with the wmapon
of to-day held a poor and hastily-entrenched
position through many long hours against
an overwhelming force, and only caved in,
after doing vast damage, when every man
was either killed or wounded. That was life
put in a paragraph. The European nations
shot away more ammunition in a year’s
training than would have previously sufficed
for a campaign. And yet every year, while
young men went away to spend their best
young days in training, and as the armies
grew bigger and the costs became more
impossible, the feeling of insecurity grew.
The forces of the Hun were always ahead in
numbers, unless the half-armed or wholly-
unarmed section of Russia’s paper reserves
was counted. They were always ahead in
weapons and training, for the -Hun was arm-
ing joyfully for an attack out of which he
hoped to make great profit, while the.
Frenchman and the Russian and the Belgian
and the Serb, 'and even the Switzer and
Dutchman and Roumanian were arming
reluctantly for a defence out of which the
best they hoped was that they might not
make ruinous loss. The initiative—the choice
of time and place—was left to the Hun. No
one cared to arouse the sleeping dog. Every-
body hoped that he might remain asleep or
bite somebody else when he woke.
The generation which was born in Hell
and grew up in Hell has no use for any
peace which won’t disarm the Hun so that
it may disarm also. And it must be such a
peace as will enable them to search him for
hidden weapons and look under his bed for
a concealed gun. The Prussian has been an
aggressor ever since the first king of Prussia
was crowned in 1701. and even before that.
It was the gibe thrown at the present
Kaiser before this war started that he was
the first of his line who hadn’t stolen some
territory, and the statement was nearly true.
Under Frederick the Great Prussia raided
Austria without any declaration of war, and
plundered it of its richest province. It de-
vised the partition of Poland. It invaded
France in 1792, and its generals published
fanciful bulletins about the capture and loot-
ing of Paris, the destruction of many towns,
and the wholesale execution of French poli-
ticians with torture thrown in. It went to
war again in 1806, and- was defeated and
disarmed and its army reduced to about
40.000 men. But within seven years it was
found that the nation had a gun hidden
under the bed. and lb at the whole able-
bodied male population had been drilDd and
armed when nobody was looking. Prussia
has had two spells at being the military
dictator of Europe—-in a moderate way as a
kingdom from 1740 to 1786: in a large wav
as head of an empire from 1871 to .1914 and
onward. And now the nations intend to get
out of Hell—if there is any door.
Any peace which doesn't disarm Prussia
effectually—and there is 217 years of his-
tory to show that that can only be done by
force, not by negotiation—will only mean
starting the old dreadful race of armament
afresh bv land and sea. The race will be
resumed with fewer resources, for Russia,
Belgium, Serbia, Roumania and Northern
France have been laid desolate; with a
crushing load of debt, and with the hopeless
feeling that Hell is to last for ever. If that
kind of pacification is established Australia
will almost certainly be called on to bear its
share of the burden of keeping the world in
arms, and that will multiply its defence
expenditure fourfold. .The old easy-going
days will be over. Every affluent white-man
dependency must sooner or later be invited
either to share with Britain, proportionately
to its population, in defending the empire, or
else to get out of the empire and defend itself
against the wolves as best it may. The
defence that a population of two persons to
the square mile could put up. especially after
the Hun had been re-established at its door,
partly by its own act. can be guessed at.
The case is one in which necessity knows
no tradition and not very much law. Assum-
ing that an arrangement is made which
doesn't effectively disarm the nation that
started the race of armaments, then Britain
will be facing the future with a war-interest
and war-pensions hill of anywhere between
£400,000,000' and £500,000,000 a year, and a
military and naval peace bill of perhaps
£100.000.000. Provided the defence bill
could be reduced almost to nothing by a
real disarming process, the situation would
be eased. Otherwise it may be necessary to
disclaim all responsibility for far-out places
which don’t help much financially, and which
can’t, like India or the Soudan, be forced to
help or even to give commercial preference;
and gather the British means of defence
closer together. The idea is unusual, but
then everything is unusual just now. Eng-
land itself was once turned adrift in the
same fashion —not put out of an empire, but
told that the centre of the emoire had to
draw in its horns because the Hun was in
the ofling. England was_ conquered shortly
afterwards, and the nation wiped out by
Germans save for some remnants in Wales
and other craggy parts.
THE WAR PROFITS BUNGLE.
When the Government can spare time to
have a lucid moment it might devote a
gleam of thought to its War Profits tax.
That affair was copied from an English law
regardless of the widely-different circum-
stances, just as anybody at Cooktown or
Darwin might have a fire at Christmas
because they have Christmas fires in Eng-
land. The British Government badly
wanted to prevent people who made or sup-
plied arms and munitions of war from reap-
ing huge profits from the public calamity,
but Australia doesn’t make these things —at
least not in any dizzy fashion. Canada made
and sold to the Allies over. £70.000,000 worth
of cartridges, explosives, steel plates, steel
goods, vessels of one sort and another in a
single year, but. we only had a strike in
our lonesome arms factory and didn’t export
any of these things. Then Britain desired
to prevent holders of food supplies cornering
the market, and British shipowners holding
up the people with ruinous freights—all to
their own profit. But Australia has so
much of the really essential foodstuffs —bread
and meat —on hand that would nearly take
a billionaire to corner the market, though an
artificial scarcity has been created by the
borrowing of immense sums to buy up and
store foodstuffs, for delivery to the Allies
when Noah’s Ark calls for another cargo.
As for looting the public with exorbitant
freights we have mighty few ships to loot
with, though we are looted ourselves badly
enough by outside shipowners, many of whom
are outside British jurisdiction. As for
restricting the rapacious manufacturer we
haven’t got much of a manufacturer to re-
strict, though we have importers (agents for
outside manufacturers) in plenty. Last year
the British War Profits tax was estimated to
yield £180,000.000. The Australian tax,
being on much the same scale, should
have yielded, population considered, about
£18.000,000. It really yielded £6Bo,ooo—less
than one-twentieth. The simple reason is
that there are no war profits, this being the
one big white man’s country that has missed
the ’bus. Importers have made a good deal.
Trading concerns have made a lot. Big
cattle and sheep men are a doubtful quantity.
Farmers have become very poor. Manufac-
turers on the whole have done badly. Cff
course w r hen Brown makes £lOOO more than
he was making before the war and Smith
makes £l2OO less, and Brown is taxed £750.
and Smith gets nothing, there are £lOOO of
war profits to show, but there is in the aggre-
gate a war loss of £250.
* * * *
The wretched return of the War
Profits impost suggests that there is
a big loss in the aggregate. A mere
shifting about of the old incomes would
far more than account for the miserable sum
collected. It is time to consider if the War
Profits tax isn’t really a traitorous device to
kill Australian manufacturers for the
importer’s benefit. If a manufacturer starts
a new industry—and this country wants them
badly enough—his profits are all war profits
and liable to tax. And whether he starts a
new industry or extends an old one he
requires to buy machinery at war prices and
thus tie himself and his capital down, with a
fairly good assurance that he will lose the
lot through cheap foreign competition when
the war is over, and the imports begin to pour
in again under the old fool tariff. Naturally
he is not in any haste. To buy say £5OOO
worth of new machinery ; and to make say
£lOOO a year out of it—the latest returns
show that the manufacturers’ earnings don’t
bear even that relation to the money spent on
new plant—except when there is a strike on.
and to pay £750 of that away in war tax, to
do this with no guarantee that the tax will
stop when the war ends, and with every pros-
pect that the oM importing competition will be
as fierce as ever when the war ends, and that
his machinery may become mere junk—-well.
.it isn’t an entrancing prospect. The. importer
can expand on the old lines or begin to deal
in new ones, without buying any machinery.
Nobody starts a strike in his business.
No Laborite inquires what wages are paid to
the people who make his goods. His trade is
only limited by the scarcity of tonnage and
the manufacturer is almost as badly handi-
capped, for he wants many imported raw
materials. A War Profits tax that charged
the manufacturer 25 per cent.- and the dealer
in foreign goods 75 per cent, might have
advantages, but our politicians were mere
cortvists who couldn’t understand that cir-
cumstances alter cases. Certainly they never
copied the British idea of not borrowing
abroad and of steadily reducing the public
debt in peace time, but these are rare hursts
of originality.
THE “S. M. HERALD’S” POOR JOKE
AT OUR DISTRESSES.
It is apparent to everyone that if the people
as a whole are to enjoy the same prosperity
and high standard of comfort the country must pay
for its share in the greatest war in history hv a
more efficient national economy, particularly in the
direction of encouraging production and INCREAS-
ING TRADE. .. .seeking new products and NEW
MARKETS develop the State’s official organisa-
tion for the stimulation of TRADE. The field is
unlimited. The markets of the world clamor for our
staple products.... new opportunities in the develop-
ment of our natural resources.
—S. M. Herald, August 30,
One of these clays the heartless old rag
which “pokes borak” at this country’s mis-
fortunes may find that it has gone too far.
Australia may lose patience with the insult-
ing stepmother in Hunter-street that talks
to it perpetually about “trade.” Just before
the war started to disarrange calculations
this country had more trade in pro-
portion to population than Great Britain,
Canada, the United States, France, Argen-
tina, Austria, Germany, Brazil, Denmark.
Italy, China, Russia, Spain, Japan, Sweden
or Uruquay—all, barring China, Russia and
Spain, really energetic countries. In fact it
came near to being the boss trader of the
world —in other country’s ships—the two
rivals whom it was impossible to beat being
Holland, which possesses the mouth of the
great river which carries the commerce of
Germanv and Belgium and counts that as
Dutch trade in passing; and Switzerland,
which carries the trade between Germany
and Italy, and counts that in passing. For
trade of its own —carried in other nations
ships, for it didn’t manufacture ships—
Australia bossed all the world, bar Maon-
land. It has so much trade even now
that it lately imported a consignment of
wattle-bark —which used to be an Australian
monopoly—from South Africa in a Swedish
vessel. And to pay for this accursed trade
.—carried at great cost and now at utterly
ruinous cost in other people’s ships, because
we don’t manufacture ships—Australia in-
curred the biggest foreign peace debt on
earth. It didn’t get a penny of money for
its debt, only goods that it avoided making
itself in order to deprive its people of work
—in other words it got a great deal ot
blighted trade. # *
Trade is a good, honest, desirable thing
when it pays; but. that is when a country
pays for its imports and generally when the
trading country has its own ships, and
makes the profits attached to carrying the
trade. When trade lands a country with a
debt of £243.735,171 due to outside creditors,
all acquired in the course of one reasonably
long lifetime, it is not a sign of health. It
is the hectic flush of disease on the counte-
nance of a bankrupt.
* * f *
As the jeering Foreign-trade publication
puts it, “the markets of the world clamor
for our staple products.” But we can’t eat
clamor or drink clamor .or meet our foreign
liabilities with clamor. Sheol clamors for
water all the time, and North Queensland
has the natural product for sale in cubic
miles, but there is no business doing. The
present position is that the wheat harvest of
1915-1916 has been sold, but not all deli-
vered ; that the harvest of 1916-17 has been
mostly but not all sold and not all delivered :
that the crop of 1917-18 is on hand unsold :
and that' the crop of 1918-19, which is
already in view doesn’t seem likely to sell at
all. unless ships turn up from somewhere,
and there is little prospect of them while the
war lasts, or unless we manufacture them
ourselves. It is rumored that Prime Minis-
ter Hughes is offering, or will offer, these
two crops to the clamorous in England at a
reduced rate, but what they want is raw
materials, and they don’t enthuse much about
paying even 4s. 6d. per bushel, for 'the news
that ihe stuff is inaccessible at the far end
of the world and likely to remain there.
About £14.000.000 have been borrowed—and
the interest on £14.000,000 is something
considerable —in order to give the farmers
whose wheat has been bought a bare living
wage to go on with. Unless the borrowing
can go on, which it can’t do for ever; or the
war ends and the outside nations that manu-
facture ships come to our rescue at their own
price, or we manufacture ships ourselves
much faster than we are doing, a great
natural industry seems likely to close up-
This isn’t a time for any fat old journal to
preach dependence on the great natural re-
sources. Its policy has been tested for the
first time and it has burst. Australia, wholly
enclosed by sea. further away from the
world’s big markets than any other great
raw-product country, and more dependent
than any other big country on imported
sroods, gambled at the instance of the S. M.
Herald school on two things—that somebody
beyond our control would always supply
vessels and that somebody beyond our con-
trol would always renew the loan. The first
proposition has practically broken down,
though we still get. a little freight at dis-
astrous expense. The real breakage may be
yet to come. The second proposition hasn t
broken down yet. though it may; meanwhile
we pay a hair-raising price for renewals.
* * • * *
The collapse of the shoddy snuffling gospel
of dependence on the great natural resources
is so bad that we begin to find a difficulty
in supplying ourselves with our own rav
material. The people of Australia are actu-
ally beginning to clamor for raw material.
Coal, bullocks and salt are raw enough, but
a country that hasn’t learned to manufacture
trains and ships finds them a great deal less


accessible than they were, and consequently
dearer. The two other big white-man coun-
fries —the United States and Canada —that
produce raw material on a large scale as
we do, and are in the war but outside the
devastation zone, like ourselves,_ are making
wealth these days, and even lending money to
the combatants. But they long since burned
the S. M. Herald’s Foreign-trade doctrine.
They combined a high tariff and great second-
ary industries with their wheat and cow and
hide and ore business. Being able to manu-
facture trains and ships, they were able to
furnish goods—not only raw material, but
manufactured goods—when the markets ot
the world clamored and offered high prices
for prompt delivery. The making of the
means of conveyance is a complex affair. It
requires iron mines in working order —not
mere undeveloped deposits or great natural
resources like those at Blythe river; huge
smelting works, steel mills of various kinds,
the capacity for making engines, boilers,
cordage of hemp and wire, complex machinery
of minor sorts, timber fittings of all sorts,
brass fittings, nickel fittings, bedding (unless
crew and passengers are to sleep in their
clothes), rails and bridges and signalling
apparatus, many kinds of tools, and' many
kinds of sundries. A country that can turn
out and fully equip a train or a big steam-
ship knows how to create almost anything.
A country that can’t do this sort of work,
especially when it is' the biggest sea-girt
country on the map and the one most remote
from the world’s big markets, finds itself at
the first emergency with millions of tons of
mouldering raw materials on its hands (the
smell of some of them is dreadful), with
decaying industries, impossible overdrafts
and an outlook that is very poor, and may
become worse. Above all. it finds itself
minus the most essential thing of all—the
habit of self-reliance.
* * * #■
Will the fat and complacent Foreign-trade
paper that publishes its out-of-date theories
in an out-of-date office where three crooked
streets meet state what is the merit of a
policy which collapses at the first real emer-
gency? What is the advantage of a gun that
will only shoot in time of peace, or an
umbrella that will only keep off the rain in
drought time?
THE CLOSED ROOM, 1918.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After the North a Port Phillip summer looks
like autumn ; and if there were nothing else
to mark the change, there is the sap-green of
the caressing wave above the white sand of
Hampton—kindly to the eye after the dark
reflections of the sea-floor of Moreton Bay.
That sea, under another name, yet part of
the great ocean that washes around the
world, strikes oh some shore on the Atlantic
where are my son and all his old mates of
Hobson’s Bay. The haze towards Geelong ;
the nearer lucent sea ; the red, brown and
yellow of the cliffs; the mass of pines that
mark the old house inland ; even the kindly,
animated mat which is my old unforgetting
dog—these are here apparently changeless
as we are changeable.
Around the old house the black susurrent
pines; the feathers of the pampas-grass mov-
ing gently as to a breath; and because I
know where to look, the agapanthus bloom-
ing almost secretly in the long grass—the
flower of love shining in the wilderness. The
falling trellis dragging the rose to earth;
the stumps of the frees I felled three years
ago falling to dust; the great pines, strong
and unchanging, and a sleepy bird talking in
a nest. And night is here, and silence, where
there were sons and daughters singing ; some-
where in France the boys are waiting to
attack or defend. I was fond enough of this
old house once, and now I care nothing if I
see it never again.
In nly study, closed now a year and more,
all the onee-familiar seems foreign. Norman
Lindsay’s Bacchanal; the Islimael Club’s
.Toss; spears'", Solomon Island wasliie-washies
and arrows tipped with dead men’s bones;
limepots, and all the scraps of wanderings;
Dante and a Coolgardie miner’s-right and
other things as incongruous. All I want to
do is to get away from them. We never come
back; not the prize-fighter nor the actor, nor
the politician; not even the sentimentalist,
who finds the green grapes of his youth have
set the teeth of his maturity on edge. Yester-
day is a closed room, and no matter how
you desire you shall not return to it.
Poor, old human nature is fighting always
against that inevitability; yet is it mostly
glad when it fails. The dead rose asks you
to remember that once it had perfume ; the
widow is singing to her second husband in
the waltz-song fashion of last century : “Love,
I will love you ever; love, I will leave you
never” —a promise which that second hus-
band may yet wish to be withdrawn. It is
all change, endless and unchangeable.
Yet most of these changes are more appa-
rent than real; seeming to be changes more
radical than they are, because we have our-
selves awakened from the lies and illusions
crammed into us in our early miseducatlon.
I was taught some madly-impossible ideals
of life as if it weffe a Avorld of copy-books ;
and from life I expected too much. These
false lights .are most of the illumination of
the stodgy English home that did not admit
the general careless veniality of the world :
that taught of some cheap “royalty” as if it
were human; that had no prejudices but
against an easy freedom of living, and no
hatreds but of other “religions.” It pretended
that women are all angels, and all men
whales for virginities, and that ladies have
not legs.
Personally the most misleading lesson was
the most continually reiterated, that I must
“love my enemies.” This is surely the most
ridiculous and impracticable of advice. Apart
from the fact that such of our enemies as are
worthy of the necessary attention should be
hated like Hell, and waited for —revenge
being never well done in a hurry—apart from
these considerations, I say. if a man loves
his enemies, what has he left for his friends?
I was fertile soil for such a story—unself-
ish, open-handed, and ready to run a mile t~
win a smile. And that made me careless to
the fact that from the day a boy goes alone
into the world all his healthy desires for
pleasure aid the world in its job of dirtying
him, and if he have a temperament he helps
it with both hands. Then it is human nature
to be a saint and forgive the world, or to turn
upon it; and in such ways the pigeon
becomes the father of the hawk.
It is the false education which is to blame;
and of all education the book-learning which
is esteemed as being nearly all is the least.
For book-learning doesn’t make character;
habit does it, and then character makes that
settled plan, whose results the mentallv lazy
call “Destiny.” See the training consequent
on the English acceptation of the position of
woman in the human family. It was a con-
cept based on smug hypocrisy and impudent
refusal to accept facts. It resulted in the
working-class women being overfecund slaves,
and the leisured-class women sterile toys.
The good things of the past are matesliip,
and love while it lasts, and children while
yet you are all the universe to them; and as
we would both love and hate the past if we
could go back to it, it is just as well we can’t
go back. A lucky man is he who has drunk
the world with wine and spent it like water;
and Time shuts the successive gates of his
changing paradises behind him. The closed
door is a great and noble institution.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The War of Jaw and Votes.
It would be an easy and pleasant task to make
out a case in defence of a proposal that any
person found guilty of sedition, in so far as
the offence might have direct bearing upon
Australia’s war effort, should be prevented
from standing for election to the Federal
Parliament, but it wouldn’t be a simple job
to defend a project for, on the same ground,
taking away a man’s rights as a State elec-
tor, prohibiting him from sitting in a State
Parliament or being a member of a munici-
pal or shire Council while leaving him all his
national franchise rights. Premier Holman
appears to have the same idea. At any
rate, the case he made out for the Sedition
Bill he, introduced into N. S. Wales Assem-
bly last week omitted to give the document
a leg to stand on. The main objections to
this measure of his are: (1) That the
penalties for ‘sedition are already ample;
(2) that the exclusion of persons convicted
of sedition, or of discouraging recruiting or
of attempting to create disaffection, from the
State Legislature and the electoral roll and
the municipal and shire council gives no
more assistance towards winning the war
than would a measure compelling Holman
to become a vegetarian; and (3) that the
spectacle of a citizen being eligible for elec-
tion to or, if already elected, being able to
keep his seat in, the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment, which has charge of the nation’s war
business and yet being excluded from outfits
having nothing to do with the war and
mainly concerned about sewers and foot-
paths and vagrant cows would be more or
less absurd, not to say grotesque. Further-
more, there is the consideration that “sedi-
tion” is nowadays being interpreted in a
way that would have caused Charles I. to
give three cheers. This Bill is obviously
drafted for political purposes. It may be true
enough that people who want to avoid the
consequences of sedition should not be sedi-
tious. This is Holman’s main point in
reply to criticism of his Bill. It is a point
that can be made in justification of any
penalty for any offence, and has been made
a thousand times in defence of the grossest
outrages upon public liberty. Holman’s
yarn about the connection of this Bill with
the war is a mere gag. The truth is that
he is using the war to work party dodges,
and the measure has been designed to help
the game along. It is not because this paper
has sympathy with the particular individuals
Holman is trying to torpedo that it looks
with disfavor on this Bill, but, among other
reasons, because it loathes every such tricky
attempt to surreptitiously score points off
the patriotic sentiments of the people. Any-
how, in trying to repress a number of indi-
viduals who could easily be attended to under
the War Precautions regulations, practically
all reasonable discussion about the war has
been prohibited and the doper and muddler
and trickster allowed to do their worst. The
injury done to Australia by all the alleged
seditionists in the land has not been a cir-
cumstance compared with the harm per-
petrated by shirking, incompetent politicians
and reckless custodians of the public purse.

Making Another Name for Himself.
“Garnishor” : Not many a man is pleased
by the receipt of the writ which makes him
defendant in an action at law, but if the
suit against W. M. Hughes proceeds to
judgment it will insure the little man an
unforgettable place in “case law,” The point
to be litigated in the action of Mertons
Ltd. v. Hughes is one of the few remaining
points in English law which is still practi-
cally “in the air.” It turns on the power
of a company to take an action in respect
of an alleged libel. There are only three
cases, and one of them is small and unim-
portant and another is not quite relevant.
In the first place, a company is a legal,
and not a physical, entity. As Chief Baron
Manwood put it: “Companies are invisible,
immortal, having no conscience or soul.” The
rule is that a company may sue for a libel
affecting its property, but not for one merely
affecting personal reputation. Hughes has
republished the allegeu defamation since the
issue of the writ, and, according to the
cables of 17/S/’lB, he says that Merton’s
Ltd. is “as much German as are the legions
of Germany on the field, and much more
dangerous.” In a judgment which still holds
it was laid down by Chief Baron Pollock
that a corporation “could not sue in respect
of an imputation of murder, or incest, or
adultery, because it could not commit those
crimes.” The reason why the Corporation
could not commit the crimes is that the law
assumes that it can only do those things
which its articles empower it to do. Apply-
ing the analogy to the allegation against
Merton’s Ltd., and assuming that Pol-
lock’s law is sound, it would imply mere hot
air to speak of the co. as German since it is
a registered British legal entity, and cannot.
in the nature of things, be anything other
than British. Pollock's judgment (deliv-
ered in 1859) was confirmed by two High
Court Judges— Day and Lawrance —in
1891. In this latter case the defendant had
published that bribery and corruption ex-
isted in the Manchester City Council. Day
said the question was whether such an action
would lie. He went on to say that it was
altogether unprecedented, and —
there is no principle on which it could be founded.
The limits of a corporation s right of action for libel
are those suggested by Pollock, C. 8., in the case
which has been referred to. A corporation may sue
for a libel affecting property, not for one merely
affecting personal reputation. The present case falls
within the latter class. There must therefore be
judgment for the defendantJ
The remaining case, not directly to this point
however, was decided by the Court of
Appeal in 1893. A newspaper had said of
the South Hetton Coal Co. Ltd. that the
homes provided by the co. for its workmen
were unfit and insanitary. As it was part of
the co.’s business to run such homes the
action lay. Tie main interest of the decision
in the South Hetton case is that the Court
confirmed the decision of Pollock as quoted
above. Although the law is at present as
stated, it by no means follows that the
Judges will not alter it when (and if) they
deliver judgment in Mertons Ltd. v.
Hughes. There is one pretty good indica-
tion that they will. One of the best authori-
ties in England on company law is G.
Spencer Bower, K.C., and he is also pro-
bably the best authority on libel. In his
Code of the Law of Actionable Defamation
he says he thinks
the dictum and decision of Chief Baron Pollock
(quoted above) are wrong, and that, as soon as they
come under review by the Court of Appeal, it will
bo declared to be so.
In his opinion the Court of Appeal would say
that a juridical entity can sue in respect of
an imputation of any conduct whatsoever of
which its agents, and therefore itself by its
agents, can be guilty. His argument is that
to contend the contrary is to ignore the
entire law of principal and agent, and its
main doctrine that every principle, whether
a natural or juridical person is liable civilly
for every illegal or wrongful act done by his
or its authority. He says that Pollock's is
an amazing argument because it assumes that an
artificial person cannot have a corporate or collective
reputation in respect of any acts or conduct except
those for which it could be punished in the same
way as a natural person could, and that, because a
corporation cannot be hanged, it is not actionable to
publish of it that it employs its members or agents
to perpetrate crimes for which they could be hanged.
If such things were said of a natural princi-
pal. employing such agents they would be
actionable. Why are they less so when said
of an artificial principal? It does not s g-
nify whether the natural principal does not
do the act himself because he is incapable of ,
doing it. Whether he is personally capable
or not, he is equally liable. If Bower’s
view is held to be the right one, then the
name, of W. M. Hughes will be permanently
associated with a very important alteration
in the law of libel. If, on the other hand,
Pollock’s judgment is upheld, there will be
permanent association with an equally im-
portant confirmation of the existing law. In
either event Billy’s name will become a
familiar one to students of case law.
The One Big Union.
From Arthur Rae, Organiser of the Aus-
tralian Labor Party:—
In your sub-leader (B. 22/8/18), “A Kingdom
inside a Commonwealth,” you say that the under-
taking is no less than “supplying the nation with a
new Parliament, a new Government and a new Con-
stitution.” Even so, what is the matter with that
beyond the size of the contract? A synopsis of
The Bulletin’s editorials of the last 10 or 15
years would justify a similar demand. Now, how-
ever, The Bulletin has taken to barking up the
same tree as the S. M. Herald and Telegraph and
other like organs that at one time it would not have
been found dead in the same paddock with. That
the scheme to unionise the whole of the industrialists
of Australia into one organisation is open to criticism
no one will deny, and probably no stronger critics
will be found than the officers and members of the
existing unions which will be asked to join. Their
criticisms, and the safeguards they demand, will
go as far to keep the imaginary “oligarchy” under
proper control as can be applied to any human form
of Government. So far as the principle of’ one
single organisation of industrialists is concerned,
why should The Bulletin lash itself into a fury of
scorn, while accepting without protest the One Big
Union of Capitalists which calls itself the Employers’
Federation in the industrial field and the National
party in the field of politics ? On another page of
tfie same issue, in commenting upon an utterance
of Judge Gurlewis, The Bulletin says: “Every
worker (who is neither a sponger nor the pliant tool
of the boss) knows that without his union he would
bo powerless to hold his own in the economic
struggle.” This sentence gives the reason and the
justification for unionism. The same economic rea-
son which compels the intelligent workmen to join
the union of his trade now forces on a union of
unions. The old craft union has done a vast
amount of useful work in its day, but with the
steady increase of big industries, which by reason
of the multiplication of wonderful mechanical de-
vices are constantly dispensing wdth skilled mechanics,
the craft union has become a clumsy and obsolete
institution, vastly better than none, admittedly, but
no more fitted to compete with modern industrial
problems than a bark canoe is fitted to carry ocean
commerce. Capitalism in its organised control of
trade and finance is not merely a kingdom wdthin a
Commonwealth, it is an empire dominating humanity.
The Trade Union Congress which passed the One
Big Union scheme is severely blamed for meeting
with closed doors. I am not aware that the
Employers’ Federation took the public into its con-
fidence in forming its constitution' nor on any
subsequent occasion, yet The Bulletin expresses no
alarm at the consequences which may arise from
this perennial secrecy. With the Labor Congress
there were specially good reasons why the press
should not be present. Only those parts of delegates
speeches which could be construed into hostility to
the scheme, or the chance remark of an indiscreet
speaker, would stand any chance of being published.
On the other hand the present fashion of “national”
Governments applying all the most despicable
methods of European autocracies in their administra-
tion of the laws, such as spies, detectives, a'gents-
provocateur, shorthand note-takers, etc., compels all
Labor gatherings to be more than ordinarily careful
whom they admit to their confidence. The “300
officiab” in the One Big Union and the average
wage of £3OO per annum, are simply guesses in
the dark, . and have no foundation. _ Tfie actual
■ number of officials, over the essential minimum,
■ will be entirely in members’ own hands to determine,
but in any case will be fewer than the aggregate
number of secretaries now employed. The Congress
did not touch the question of salaries. Further-
more, the principles of the Initiative Referendum and
Recall are embodied in every grade of the adminis-
tration so as to keen the union as democratic as
human ingenuity can devise. Of course the success
of the scheme and its advantages to the community
must in the last analysis depend upon the wisdom,
tolerance and honesty of the average membership, and,
like all man-made institutions, its powers may be
abused; but on the whole the advantages, not merely
to the present-day unionists, but to the community,
are calculated to far outweigh its possible dangers.
It would be as idle to advocate the abolition of
machinery, because steam-boilers occasionally burst,
and railway trains sometimes collide and thereby
destroy life and property. As to the objection to the
One Big Union because it may tread on somebody's
corns, nothing big can be done, or attempted without
taking risks, and the One Big Union is far less likely
to permit foolish strikes to take place, dragging
in others without rhyme or reason, than is the case
at present.
The mattet of a “Worker’s Bank” had nothing
whatever to do with the scheme, but was simply a
subject listed on the Congress agenda for discussion.
As for the Commonwealth Bank, the only adverse
criticism was that it had not begun to live up to
its possibilities in financing the Commonwealth, and
was avowedly a friend and backer of the private
banks ( vide Denison Miller’s recent speech in
London) instead of wiping them out of existence,
as it one day must do. As for the £220,000,000
sunk in railways making them “the property of
everybody,” (Jiat is all bunkum. Nominally, they
are “our railways”; actually, they belong to the
investors of the capital, mostly foreign investors at
that, and freights and fares are constantly going up
to meet tin growing interest bill.
The alleged breaking up of large estates is no
refutation of the statement in our preamble that
industries are concentrating in fewer and fewer
hands. Bank amalgamations are the order of the
day, and trusts, mergers, combines, “gentlemen’s
agreements” and “honorable understandings” are
all forms by which Capital not only functions
nationally, but has reached out internationally, and
when the present armed conflict has ceased will
become world-wide in its operations.
The One Big Union was not adopted by men who
“hate the politicians,” but in order to guide and
supplement political action by the united voice and
influence of organised industrialism.
The reply to which is: —
(1) The statement that The Bulletin barks up the
same tree with the Herald and Telegraph is a
falsehood. The Bulletin differs from them diametri-
cally in the biggest things—honest finance, stoppage
of borrowing for public works, reduction of the
public debt, real decentralisation by opening the
ports with, such railway facilities as Sydney
possesses, the establishment of cities there (granted
the facilities that made Sydney other cities like
Sydney are possible), with the State as sole ground
landlord. The Labor party stood for some such
policy once till it went into the same camp of
rotten finance and truckling to the money-lending
capitalist and privately-owned congested cities as
the Herald and Telegraph.
(2) The people who run the Employers’ Federation
and the National party don’t use their organisations
to advocate the restoration in the Pacific of Hun
power, with its militarism and its autocratic
monarchy. They aren’t obviously the agents of
the biggest military capitalist on earth. If they
were The Bulletin would object to them as much as
it does to the Big Union, or more.
(3) If everybody who keeps an eye and an ear
open for glaring treason and shameless advocacy of
the cause of our most dangerous enemy is a “spy,
detective, agent-provocateur and shorthand note-
taker,” then the Australian Governments do employ
them.
(4) Mr. Rae objects to the Commonwealth Bank
because “it hasn’t begun to live up to its possibilities
in financing the Commonwealth” and is “a friend and
backer of the private banks instead of wiping them
out of existence.” How would Mr. Rae have financed the
Commonwealth? If he knows a better way and
doesn’t publish it —in detail, not in mysterious bunkum
and generalities—he is a mean creature, and is neg-
lecting his country’s interests. But don’t let him
propose the old, oft-tried, oft-exploded schoolboy-
device of an irredeemable and unlimited paper cur-
rency. Better financiers have discovered that trick-
before and thought they had dropped on something-
new.
(5) For the rest the Commonwealth Bank has;
financed all the big war loans, and saved the country-
over £3,000,000 by doing it below the old joint--
stock bank rates. It has accumulated about
£25,000,000 of deposit business (apart from Savings
Bank deposits) that would otherwise have gone to
the private banker. It accumulated in its first 54
years over £760,000 of reserves, all belonging to the
people. It hasn’t cost the people a penny. And it,
is growing faster than ever now that it has got
over its preliminary expenses and other troubles.
It has put up probably a world’s record for progress.
But it didn’t ask Mr. Rae’s advice. And it didn’t
rush out and club the other banks on the first day
of its existence, and scatter their deposits and
securities in the street, and start a picnic which
would have closed up half the industry of the
country. It is a Bank of the People, not a gorilla
or a loutish anarchist with an axe, or an incendiary.
The Bulletin admires it as a really successful
attempt at Public Ownership. If it isn’t the right
kind of bank let Mr. Rae get his party to start
the right kind, with himself as manager, and show
how to do it.
(6) Mr. Rae is right in saying that the railways
are not really the property of the pe pie. They
belong to the money-lenders. So far as Mr. Rae’s
State is concerned they might have been making
progress towards being realty the people’s property
had not Mr, Rae’s party been such a dashed liar
and traitor, and gone into the S. M. Herald’s loan
camp. It once advanced a policy of reduced bor-
rowing—and borrowing was then down to about
£1,500,000 a year; no borrowing except for works
that would pay full interest ar.d sinking fund; and
its leader even promised a general sinking fund.
What does Mr. Rae propose by way of repairing this
carnival of waste, treachery, falsehood, fraud, truck-
ing to the money-lending capitalist and Judas
IscAßioTism? Does he propose that the party should
swear off now, and be honest? Or would he have
it pile a swindle on top of a lie and repudiate? Or
would he have it remain in the old ditch of sin?
(7) Bank amalgamations don’t necessarily mean
the concentration of capital in fewer hands. Joint 1
stock banks, large or small —-there are no private
banks in . Australia now that The Bulletin knows
of, and have been none for quite a while —are the
property of any and every Big Unionist who buy-s
shares. * Of course they don’t give away shares, for
they have none to give. The bank itself owns less
than an old age pensioner—the co-operative pro-
prietors own everything. They generally 7 favor the
small man, for the owner of lOOJHIO shares doesn't
