While Wilhelm Backs His Cart.
When last heard from Billjim had advanced
20 miles from the place he started from, and
had penetrated right into the heart of the
stricken country over which the harrow of
war is passing for the fourth time. What
that countryside is like now only Heaven
and the toiling, weary soldiers thereabouts
can tell. Broken up into shell holes,
trenches and graves, the towns and villages
mere heaps of dust, it is probably such a
locality as the world has never known before.
And while Billjim is fighting his way on,
the problem of who is going to take up
his gun when he drops out of the fray is
more acute than ever. He has the consolation
at any rate of knowing that a vote is being
taken by the Australian Labor party
which, if carried, would be a proclamation
of local quit and abandonment. That
the Australian army should be allowed to
waste away would be bad enough, and such
a tragedy is the not improbable result of
the mess the Commonwealth has been plunged
into by political tricksters and moral
shirkers; but that the party which stood,
at least, for the maintenance of the nation’s
battalions by the voluntary method should at
this hour deliberately desert not only this
principle and all it meant, but openly betray
the men it sent away and the cause for
which every democracy in the world stands
to arms would be a revelation of baseness
almost without historical parallel. Never
did men light in a more holy war than these
lads of ours, for they are defending the very
foundations of human liberty and national
freedom. The whole future of mankind
turns on whether victory shall go to a
boweriess autocracy or to them who stand
across its pathway in defence of Right. And
no mistake need be made about this victory
going to be an easy, simple thing because
the tide is running our way to-day. Baulked
though they have been, and by the grace of
Uncle Sam will continue to be, the German
armies are still a powerful instrument for
evil, and every man that the Allies can feed
and equip is wanted to see the last round
out. Meanwhile the German on the western
front goes steadily on the reverse gear,
bound apparently for the powerful line of
entrenchments he left in April, there to
spend the winter. To winter where he is
now would be an experience full of horror
and misery, even if it were possible.
Whether lie will be able to fall into the old
line and stay there remains to be seen.
Foch will probably be heard from even more
emphatically in a few days than during the
last week or two. Of the adventures going
on in Siberia the tidings are about as full
of illumination as- the inside of a well.
General Frost is due to descend on the
situation thereabouts in a few weeks. As
the German and Austrian troops are without
supplies and the Czecho-Slovaks are in the
same fix, and the Bolsheviks are alleged to
be living on vodka, and everything generally
is up in the air, the situation when the ice
and snow move off the landscape next yeai
ought to make a fairly interesting yarn. The
only definite news from Russia is that
Lenin has followed the Czar across the
Styx a local Charlotte Corday having put
a bullet in him. Perhaps he was not very
sorry. Russia seems a good place to be out
of just now.
The Case of Some Drab Insects.
When Premier Holman learned that the
teachers in N. S. Wales were dissatisfied
with their lot (or their scrap) and intended
getting, if possible, under the shelter or the
Arbitration and wage-fixing laws, lie was st
disappointed Treasurer. And one at least of
his colleagues spoke as if the teachers weie
exhibiting base ingratitude, and as if he had
been bitten by his pet, serpent. One woVitd
think As arbitration, in place of strikes, is a
notable feature of the .creed on which Hol-
man was brought up,, that he would be
glad, in this time of industrial upheaval
and veiled insurrection, that one large body
of men intended to' follow orderly and law-
abiding ways. Also, that these men m-
tended to adopt a principle that he has
preached ever since his youthful and well-
groomed head first appeared above the waters
of obscurity. Yet he wasn’t glad worth a
cent. He was mad. The, teachers say, among
other things, that there isn’t one of them
to-day whose salary is larger than that paid
for the same class of work 37 years ago ;
and that there were all these years ago over
30 teachers receiving £SOO a year while now
there are only nine. Tlie cost of living has
gone up. Wages in almost every line of
industry have gone up. sometimes more than
100 per cent. But the only difference to the
teachers has been that the prizes of the pro-
fession —very poor prizes at the best —have
become fewer. By reason of their calling
they are a scattered crowd that flocks separ-
ately, and they can’t in any single electorate
wield a vote worth considering. They aren’t
eligible for the Shearers’ Unjon or the Boiler-
makers’ Association. And because of these
disadvantages, the most valuable. kind o.
civil servant ever invented has received little
save applause, and not too much of. that.
Certainly it must be admitted in fairness
that Premier Holman disputed some of the
assertions of the pedagogues. He alleged in
a broken voice that £27,000 was made avail-
able last year-—not by way of a general in-
crease, for among about 8000 men and
women it would have been a mockery of
70s. per head every 12 months, but to help
individual cases. And this year £39,000 was
made available, part to increase the salaries
of the 8000 and the rest for something, or
other. The figures don’t seem much seeing
that the educational folk represent nearly
half the public service in point of numbers;
that last year the rise of salary for the other
half was about £200.000; and that toe
wealthy University which has no claim at all
was lately promised £300,000 for the mere
asking. But the Ministry is a creature of
habit. It gives in to demands from any big
organisation with a solid vote because it
loves ease and hasn’t the lower jaw of a
fighter. It gaily spends public money on a
banquet to an opera co., or a reception to the
Prime Minister’s wife, or on opening the
bear and laying the coping-stone. of the lion
at the new Zoo, or on a great children’s fete
at the same Zoo so that the kids may view
the crocodile, because it loves to be kind and
liberal and to make speeches and bask in
applause. And it takes little interest in the
dreary case of a body of useful men and
women who have no block vote to sell and to
whom it would be absurd to make speeches
—it feels this way because it has no time for
plain business. The teachers will be well-
advised to hang on to the Arbitration (’onto
and save the butterfly Premier the trouble of
considering the case of mere drab insects
such as they. He will be snared much hes -
itation and responsibility when the Arbitra-
tion Judge decides matters for him. Thru
he will only have the money to find, and with
all the profits pouring in from between
£50.000.000 and £60.000.000 of loan works
that he has helped to construct that should be
easy.
The Family at Australia House.
“Fax” : Some of the charges of waste and
extravagance which Melb. Age brings against
Andrew BTsiier seem rather ill-informed,
whereas one glaring fault in the administra-
tion of the High Commissioner’s office is not
mentioned at all. Most of the increased ex-
penditure is due to the establishment of a
branch to deal with war pensions, munition
workers, transports and war records. Also
the little army of temporary employees lias
gone up in wages owing to increased cost of
living: for instance, typists originally
engaged at 255. are now getting £2 a week.
There’s nothing riotously extravagant in a
payment of £2 a week to typists. As to the
fact that George Reid offered to continue
High Commissionering without salary when
his term of office expired, it is certain that
such an arrangement couldn’t have been
adopted. Australia wants its representative
in London to be a laborer worthy of his
hire. But a clear waste of £lOOO a year or
more is evident in the pensioning off
of two of George Reid’s principal offi-
cers, both of whom could have been
retained. A previous oflicial secretary and
assistant ditto are drawing pensions total-
ling £1132. The present official sec. at £lOOO
a year went to London as private sec. to
Andrew Fisher. Reid's personal secretary
had received £360, but the man at Fisher’s
elbow was given £5OO, in a partly camou-
flaged-form, Ilis salary was stated at £3lO.
and the other £l9O was called “allowance.”
There’s also a glaring fault in the annoint-
ment of clerks in the Hiah Commiss oner s
Office. A great proportion are . Engbsh-
men who have never been in Australia, whilst
several of the-born-Australians in Australia
House (outside of the public service! are
cadets of the ignoble Stinking Fish family.
Hiah Commissioner Andrew Ftsher has
made no effort to staff Australia House with
good Australians. As a man who is
unquestionably lacking in administrative
ability he leaves too much of the manage-
ment to his officers. The work of public-
service officers at Austraha House has never
been properly investigated on behalf of the
Public Service Comnrssioner. nor has there
been any careful grading of officers. The
system that was loose under the compara-
tively inexpensive, control of George Retd
has remained loose since Andrew Fisher
took over the. stout gentleman’s job. It
needs tightening.
Spring Fashions in Art.
The Royal Art Society of N. S. Wales has
opened its consignment of Spring goods at
the New Gallery, Department of Public In-
struction (where the powers-that-be raise
the teacher's salary), Loftus-street, Sydney.
Exhibits are in number 279, and the aver-
age is above- that of some recent years.
There are few masterpieces, but on the other
hand there is only one item which really
calls for the execution of the artist and his
burial at midnight where four roads meet. Lest
fie should feel lonesome his name is withheld.
The general tendency of the show is brown
or yellow with a leaning towards grey.
Green is scarce; and the waters are not
aggressively blue. Very few artists break
out in red, though
R. S. Waltelin does
something in that
way. His roofs are
of plain definite
colors, severely
accurate, and not
v e r y inspiring.
Still it is seldom
that a roof is in-
spiring.
Girl is, of course,
a predominant fea-
ture. She always
is in art, for a
girl’s skirts make
graceful curves
while a man’s pants
J. S. Watkins.
aon’t. Her hair is an inspiration, while a man’s
hair is mostly a bald spot. Also her blouse
is a work of art, while a man’s waistcoat is
merely a screen in front of his lunch. The
collection includes fair, dark, long, short and
medium girls; flapper girls, one of whom has
just arrived and is waiting with her bag for
the door to be opened; girls reading, girls
knitting, girls perpendicular or partially
recumbent, horizontal girls, and girls who
are just busy being girl. Likewise there is
a girl \yeeping with her head on the table
and a newspaper with no print on it drop-
ping from her hand. It is the work of James
R. Jackson, and calls for kindly mention.
Something to the same effect may be said
about J. S. Watkins’ girls who are looking
at a Buddha and other works of art. Tempe
Manning’s partially dressed and just-washed
girl, accompanied by a stove or bath-heating
apparatus, is only open to objection by
reason of insufficient flesh. Still that doesn't
imply that she is inartistic, for some girls
are built that way and fatten later on. The
flapper girl who has just arrived with the
bag is a water-color by Dattilo-Rubbo, and
would be worth buying even if the child had
lost her luggage. Joseph Wolinski’s girl in
Japanese clothes is a pleasant color effect
and a compliment to an ally, also he has
two other girls, one of whom is engrossed
in literature and looks as if she could spell
all the words,
The nudes, of
which there are
not so many as
usual, call for little
remark. The nudes
used to be mostly
lying down. Now
they are mostly
erect, having risen
up to make muni-
tions, perhaps. A
sitting nude gives
him the impression
of having sat down
too hard and flat-
tened a portion of
Joe Wolinski.

Possibly there is rather much Sydney
Harbor, and the Heads, and the coast near
Newport. Sydney Harbor at daybreak, when
the water is dead calm, and the sun hasn’t
yet dragged himself out of bed and resumed
the struggle for existence, and the mosquito
curtain of haze is still hanging from the
bedpost of the sky, and an indefinite sort of
vessel—a brooner or schrig or full-rigged
auxiliary punt—is lazing on the surface, has
points about it, and so has .the adjacent
coast. Still they grow painfully familiar,
Dattilo-Rubbo, Lister Lister, James R. Jack-
son, J. Muir Auld, J. W. Tristram do justice
to the old subjects, and Alfred Coffey spends
much on them —time which isn’t wholly
wasted.
Will Ashton is largely represented. His
scenes are mostly
laid in England,
Scotland of France,
nrd his “Cornish

is especially worth

all his work is

other river of the

speed in the same
kind of flat coun-
try, but when he

Also he is massive and picturesque in his
“Pont Philippe IV., Paris.” R. de Mestre
is architectural but not massive in a street
scene of old Sydney, and looking at it one
feels so well pleased that the lack of massive-
ness is forgiven. In fact one is glad to do
without it. A. J. Daplyn supplies half-a-
dozen good things, including a church (218)
and a pub next door (219). They ai'e both
old, especially the church, and both rather
silent, for customers are scarce in each case.
Likewise they both look dry. Mabel Bar-
ling’s "Quick Sketches of Cows” suggests
that if she drew cow’s a little more slowly
she would draw very good cows. There is
no excuse for drawing cows quickly. They
are waitful beasts. D. S. Cohen is to be
specially commended for the head of a
Hebrew, whose honest old face suggests

Black, by J. S. Watkins, stands out

“Trot”: Holman's Sedition Bill excludes
from political disqualification the person
convicted of the crime of sedition where such
“crime” consists “only of intention to pro-
mote feelings of ill-will and hostility be-
tween different classes of his Majesty’s sub-
jects.” As there is no caste system in Aus-
tralia yet, nor an official aristocracy, this
term “different classes” has great elasticity.
Is it sedition to promote ill-feeling between
the class addicted to malt and the class that
is pledged to lemonade ; to stir up the Ryan-
ites in Bananaland to hostility against the
other crowd; to tell the people who object
to unduly expensive chops that those respon-
sible are brigands; to speak disrespectfully
of the Wattites and the Oookites and sew
the seeds of discord between the men that go
to toil per boot and those who go merely to
the races by motor-car? If these disturb-
ances are not sedition within the meaning of
the Bill, what are they?
The Government, in its wisdom, has deco-
rated the military police with insignia of
red, white and blue, so that they may be
seen and dodged afar off. A policeman, like
a soldier, requires a uniform as a guarantee
that he is the real article and not a burglar
or confidence-man in disguise. But lie
doesn’t want a uniform like a bush-fire.
Once the British soldier was described as a
man who was taught to dress himself in
scarlet, wave a flag, give three cheers, and
then creep unseen on his enemy, but he has
got over that. He even uses smokeless
powder now in. order to avoid publicity.
Once the London detective or Bow-street
runner wore a red waistcoat so that he
might collect evidence by stealth. Now the
only way one can tell a detective is by his
obtrusive unobtrusiveness, and his effort to
look more like other people than they do
themselves. There is onfl improvement that
might be suggested in the matter of the red.
white and blue military police. They should
be taught to bray at short intervals when
their suspicions are aroused. They might
blow on the bagpipes while following a clue,
but the bagpipes might obscure the view of
the clue.
An English soldier who seems to have had
more than his share of bad luck is a lad
named Probert, of Tredegar, Whales. As a
boy of 16, under the name of Williams,
against the wishes of his foster parents, he
enlisted, and was sent to France. Not many
months later he was wounded and received a
commission. Then came the battle of Loos,
and in this he received a shock which has
caused all the trouble. He was sent to
Harefield Hospital, England, suffering from
loss of memory. By mistake lie was next
forwarded to Australia. At different times
out here he has answered to the names of
Gordon McDonald, Lewis, Probert. Williams
and Davis. Not being able to explain satis-
factorily to the authorities wffio he was, and
using at times the name of another man.
Gordon McDonald, 13 A.L.H.. who was still
on service, he was placed in Long Bay gaol
as an impostor. However Cffirke, of the
Vic. Prisoners’ Aid Association, has been at
work, and with the assistance of Boyd.
M.H.R., has succeeded in straightening things
out. Robert is now to return to England
and the hospital treatment that he needs.

Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.

serve their teeth as long. as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Itough powders, such as cuttle-
fish. crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

“Gran’dad Kudd” reached the Palace (Syd-
ney) during the week. Dad, the human
volcano of “On Our Selection,” has now got
to the stage of life when he mostly dodders
and growls at ho in 5 in bedroom slippers
while grey-haired Dave acts as his. faithful
manager, coming in at intervals from the
paddocks to say “By cripes” and growl a
protest when the old man has made things a
bit hotter than usual. The busy helpful
Mum as a dramatic entity has faded away
into Gran’ma, a sort of pathetic .lay figure,
who, file anxious Rudd family think, should
be taken away for a change of air. It
wouldn't do the play much harm if she were
taken away and lost in the ranges. Young
Dave, son of old Dad’s first lieutenant, re-
presents the third generation at the cheeky,
leggy stage, and grand-daughter Nell is the
charming and (apparently) inevitable heroine
who drags hero and villain after her and
causes au artificial storm of melodrama to
rage round the hero’s potato-harvester. Nell
is very sweet and weak; but neither she nor
her suitors matter in the least, and the lurid
detective who finally harvests the unclean
potato villain on his wedding morn, and so
allows the meek hero to take Nell to the
church deserves imprisonment for life in
“East Lynne.” The strength and charm of
the play is in its Australian atmosphere and
the delightful characterisation. Daniel
Damascus Rudd, the family prodigal, comes
back early in the first act in a full set of
black bushman’s whiskers and adds himself to
the old portrait gallery. Fortunately, in
spite of family gibes, he doesn’t trim his
splendid beard or shave, but boldly wears
his face-hair, and so remains a gallant and
striking figure to the end. Finding there is
money in her family, he woos the local wow-
ser's smirking daughter, and the first linger-
ing embrace is slyly refereed as a wrestling-
match by Gran’dad, who sheds a lot of his
first-act senility as the action of the play
quickens. At one stage he even comes home
drunk on a horse with the well-corned wowser
double-banking behind him. The play moves
on merrily from incident to incident with
the stock melodramatic figures occas’onally
intruding unconvincingly on the familiar
bush types.
* * * *
Bert Bailey is completely satisfying as
Grah’dad, and Fred Macdonald, the other ex-
perienced character-builder, makes Dave

grown older a loveable character. William
Evans stutters as a' more mature Joe Rudd.
Louis Maehilton is the young player wdio
adds Daniel Damascus Rudd to the family
circle. He supplies a careful study of a
flash, prancing bushman, so that Dan looks
real and is always interesting. Edmund
Duggan is natural as a poor, plausible neigh-
bor. Willie Driscoll, as the good Mr. Banks,
is a scared rabbit of a man married to a
shrill Cold Tea fanatic played by Queenie
Sefton. Amelia Banks, their daughter, is
the grinning, drawling bush belle who makes
eyes at Dan. It’s a rich comedy part, and Yetty
Landau doesn’t waste many of its chances.
Lilias Adeson, as Lily (Dave’s wife), and
Alfreda Bevan, as Madge (wife of Joe), are
convincing as the strident, hardworking bush-
women. Grade Dorran, as Nell, is a charm-
ing feminine figure; but she and the other
melodramatic inventions walk about outside
the real and highly-diverting play of charac-
ter. Everybody should go to see that.

“So Long, Letty,” ‘revived for six nights
only at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Satur-
day, found a big house waiting for it. Its
music is brisk and joyous, and in the sunny
first act the eight famous dancing girls, with
arms affectionately interlocked, describe a
complete circle in line, each dancer using one
unstockinged leg to hop on while the other
rosy limb is waggled tirelessly in the air.
Heaven knows who invented the strange
evolution; but the Sydney audience goes
crazy with delight as soon as it beg.ns, and
the well-trained girls have to continue doing
it until they and singer Connie Ediss are
tired out. The anthem to which it is per-
formed is “Here Come the Married Men.”
Maude Fane is dainty as Letty Bobbins, but
“fools” too much. The habit is growing on
Maude of late.
Some parts need
all the tricks an
actress has; but
there is no profit
in working them
overtime when the
librettist has done
his bit. Connie
Ediss is thoroughly
at home as fretful
Grace Miller, “the

the sporty, garden-

who comes home

Sydney James serves the last of his
“Pierrot Pie” at the Playhouse (Sydney)
to-night. Beginning Friday is “Varieties”
and the policy is new artists and pro-
gramme every fortnight. Amongst the
new goods will be a romantic beilowdrama
ent.tled “The Fireman,” with comedian Joe
Brennan in the lead.
“S’nice” is keeping producer Espinosa
smiling at the T.voli (Sydney;, where lead-
ing lady Eve Kelland grows sentimental in
song over her young man at Kiama —not
that there are not equally nice youths further
inland, but because K ama, the N. S. Wales
coastal town, has a lilting name that tickles
the song-writer’s ear. By the way, if there
isn’t a coon song entitled “Take Me Back to
Old Pyjama,” it’s time somebody wrote it.
Pyjama is a nice, restful name, and could
easily be attached to an imaginary tropic isle
with palm-trees growing on it and a star-eyed
girl on the beach waiting to be encored seven
times.

The Strand (Sydney) tells the story of
yet another of Alice Brady’s jaunts to New
York. This time she takes the step from
a New Jersey fishing village. The* inevitable
artist discovers her beauty and offers her
love minus matrimony; but Alice pauses.
The pause gave a chance to a wealthy
rancher, who butted in with the offer of a
ring and a bank balance. That was where
Alice ceased to pause. She took the cash in
hand and let the artist The Lyceum has a
b'g attraction in the De Saulles sensation.
Miriam Cooner as Blanquette, the dishonored
wife and distraught mother, is an outstanding
cino. actress. The Piccadilly this week has
another “Made In Australia” film —this time
from South Australia. It is broad comedy
with an English new-chum (not so very
new) as the victim of most of the jokes.
“Algie’s Romance” is the title, and it was
going well when The Bulletin started on its
wiy to the parsonage. “The Triumph of the
Weak” is the Crystal Palace feature, witn
Alice Joyce tne beauty in distress.

Leila Doubleday, back from years of
education and successful achievement in
Europe, drew a large audience to the
Auditorium (Melb.) last Saturday and was
disappointing only to those who had expected
a more marked individuality in the accom-
plished artist. It is a pleasure to hear Miss
Doubleday play anything on her sweetest of
violins. She gets a wonderfully pure tone,
and charmingly melodious effects, in an
always free and easy way, but as a facile
and strikingly tuneful executant she lacks
force of character. She hasn’t the unruffled
calm, the air of suppressed feelipg, of, say,
Marie Hall. A London critic once declared
that Maud McCarthy’s virginal treatment of
a passionate theme was peculiarly interest-
ing. Neither the personality nor the in-
terpretive performance of this clever' Aus-
tralian girl is peculiarly interesting. It is
as though the tuition she received from an
Austrian master had been uncongenial to
her temperament. Her time might have
been better spent at Brussels. Miss Leila
is neither cold nor sensuous in her artistry—
just sweet and pleasing all the time. Of a
dozen things she played on Saturday the
Spanish Symphony (Lalo) was the most
important, and Tempo di Menuetto( Pugnani-
Ivreisler) the most happily expressed. At
the end of the concert oile felt that, in the
full and soulful sense of the term, the gifted
violinist has yet to “arrive.” As a rule she
would be more appealing if her utterances
were less rapid. Her broadest tones need
further broadening; her lightest are not
feathery enough. But she does everything
with an ease which promises well for the
future. Cacialli, whose voice is a basso-
baritone blend, sang two or three numbers
like the artist he is. and Harold Elvins sup-
plied a couple of little pianoforte solos as
well as the accompaniments. To-night
(Thursday) the performers will be the same
but the programme will be different.
Miss Emilie Polini is getting much credit
in “The Invisible Foe.” at the King’s
(Melb.), for her impersonation of the
daughter whose dead daddy makes frantic
efforts to put her in touch with the tell-tale
document. .Miss Polini is the life and soul
of the Invisible Ghost, story. The
sincerity of the actress lifts the
rather ridiculous into the almost sublime;
she introduces a leaven of crude super-
naturalism into the lump of stodgy
melodramatic narrative. The public that
declines to take the villain seriously, or feel
very sorry for his acquiline-noSed brother,
is nevertheless disposed to think of tearful
Emilie with gentle compassion. It’s a sad
thing for a young woman to be haunted by
the perturbed spirit of daddy, anyhow. There
are other sad things in “The Invisible Foe,”
but the predicament of the haunted spinster
is the saddest.
One more week of “The Thirteenth Chair”
will be*the last of that dark, but not entirely
dismal, drama at the Royal (Melb.). Mean-
while suspicion continues to be hurled by
the artful dramatist at several people who
; didn’t murder Spencer Lee, and enterprising
■ sweep promoters persevere in the game of
i putting the names of all the characters into
a hat and selling the tickets at a shilling
each, and deducting commission from the
one who draws the murderer.
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the end of
“Katinka’’ is fixed for Friday of this week.
To-morrow brings “Oh! Oh!*! Delphine!!!"
for the first time in Australia. The pro-
fusion in notes of exclamation suggest that
Delphine is a daughter of Ginger.
** * *
The production of Bucalossi’s comic opera,
“The Black Cloaks,’’ by the Vic. Opera Co.,
at the Palace (Melb.), on Saturday, was
passable enough in chorus and orchestral
work, but failed to establish any individual
reputation. The most attractive performer
was Phyllis Ashley, as Girola, who acted
and sang with a pardonable consciousness
of superiority. The show began agree-
ably enough under the vigorous baton
of Edward Hanstein, but occasionally yawn-
ing cavities in the dialogue through the
missing of cues and general slackness stirred
the gods to ribald comment, which quickly
changed into a gale of laughter when Don
Phillip, or Aragon, strode into view. Thk
Bulletin must say that the Don was the
nearest approach to a human hatpin it lias
ever beheld.
** * *
“Iloni Soit,” at the Tivoli (Melb.), lacks
the briskness and brevity of “Time, Please,’’
and employs the plagiarist more freely than
any previous revue. There are three or four
jokes that enjoy a blessed resurrection, but
great efforts have been made to conceal
their antiquity and give them tin 1
savor of wit fresh from the brain.
The laundry episode, however, has not been
retouched, and is the same undisguised
classic that tickled the ribs of the Pharaohs
in the long ago. The original humor in the
entertainment tends more than ever to dissi-
pated energy by Barry Lupino, quaintly sup-
ported by the miniature Rego, though John
Junior and Hugh Steynt occasionally lend
a hand. Junior is mostly a furtive frivoller
revealing all the creased profiles of a tailor’s
dummy, except in the tabloid drama with
Beatrice Holloway and John de Lacey,
where lie plays the misleading juvenile in a
strong, exultant fashion. Miss Holloway is
the same plump and composed exponent of
artless heartaches who engages the combined
genius of several dressmakers to add to her
beguiling arts. Marie Eaton’s part is song-
ful, but why she appears as an incredible
swaggie shrilling banal sentiments about
an old bush track isn’t clear. Noel Geddes
and Ethel Bennetto are nice girls, who seem
inconspicuous by their presence beside the
other humorists. The music is thin and
tinkly and the dressing and scenery rarely
fill tiie eye. For the most part the revue is
Barry Lupino and might be fined for loiter-
ing while he holds the stage.
The Bijou (Melb.) management lias
installed one or two new singers in the
first part, but otherwise the bill is about the
same as before.
Hoyt’s (Melb.) has secured Lina Cavalieri
for a week’s run in “The Eternal Temptress.”
Scenes are laid in Venice and Rome, but
all the glittering vistas Those cities can
offer are not to be compared *with the weird
glory of Lina in her full war-paint as a
temotress. “The Rise of Jennie Cushing."
which follows, is the fall of Elsie Ferguson,
who tumbles from her high pedestal as a
movie actress by giving overweight in the
emotional scenes. The other current Hoyt
stars are Jewel Carmen and Julian Eltinge.
Jewel lends her intoxicating glance to “The
Girl with the Champagne Eyes.” while
Julian buries his sex in feminine garments
in a comedy drama, “The Widow’s Might.”
* * *
The Majestic (Melb.) has Clara Kimball
Young in “The Reason Why” this week.
The film is from the yarn of Elinor Glyn.
and is full of the splendidly reckless people
who try to be giddy with discretion and
make dull vice a fashionable, conceit. The
one redeeming feature in the production is
Clara Kimball Young, who lavishes her art
and her dressmaker on an artistically puny
part. The follow-on is the Triangle feature.
“Station Content,” a romantic railroad
tussle in which Gloria Swanson is the cause
of the unpleasantness.
Sydney Telegraph’s latest Foreign-trade
gag is that this country wants ships to
come here to carry away our wool and meat
and wheat and lead, and if we shut out
imports by Protection these ships won’t
come because they will have no cargo to
bring. Which is a sad outlook, for the
things we sell are cheap and heavy and
the things we buy are expensive and light.
If we have to buy a ton of ladies’ foreign
clobber, brought in a foreign ship, every
time we want to send away a ton of lead or
beef, it would be cheaper to buy the dashed
ship.

If the 3i p.c. p.a. on buildings and plant
which the N. S. Wales Gas (Amended) Act
now permits cos. to appropriate from revenue
for their "Special Purposes and Deprecia-
tion Fund’’ is a proper allowance, then
the increased profit shown for the
past half-year lias not been earned.
Chairman George Judah Cohen put the
matter this way to shareholders at
the recent meeting: “The profits would not
permit of the payment of the new standard
rate of dividend or provide the full con-
tribution to tin* Special Purposes and De-
preciation Fund.” The profit of £60,286 is
arrived at this way :—-
June, 17. Dec..’l7. June, 18.
Total revenue ..£440,319 £479,189 £499,948
Working expenditure 330,589 354,399 366,641
Working profit. . £109,780 £124,790 £133,307 _
Less net interest . . 85,541 38,193 36,713
Less written off . . 34,744 60,256 36,308
Profit .. .. £39,495 £26,341 £60,286
It was recently shown in Court that the
amount of depreciation in 1017 on this 3A p.c.
basis would have been £95,043, almost the
amount shown as written off in the above
table. Further, Supreme Court Judge Fer-
guson reported that “it appeared from the
evidence that approximately the same
amounts [£50,558 in 1912 and £95,043 in
1917 —calculated.on the 34 p.c. basis] would
be arrived at by adopting the accepted stan-
dard of allowances for depreciation in works
of this kind.” However, what the directors
have not written off for the past half will
probably come out of the profits of the
current term, which will be considerably
increased by the new proclaimed selling
prices. The result of not making the full
allowance as permitted by the Act —though
note that the amount Written off is practi-
cally the same as the directors usually allow
for each June half—is that the dividend ,on
old and new capital has been lifted to 7 p.c.
again.
Two important events marked the first
half of this year: (1) The amendment of
1912 Gas Act. and (2) the Supreme Court
inquiry into the cost of production and dis-
tribution of gas. Under the original Gas
Act the dividend on new capital raised was
limited to 7 p.c. The Amending Act in-
creased this rate to 8 p.c. A year ago the
directors claimed that it was “not possible
to obtain further necessary capital,” and
now it stated that this 8 p.c. “should attract
investors, and thus again place the co. in a
position to carry out the extensions re-
quired.” To be quite candid, it is not due
to the fact that the co. is now allowed to
pay 8 p.c. on new capital that investors will
rush any new issue. What counts is the
co.’s ability to earn and pay dividends at
something approaching that rate. It could
undoubtedly have got capital at the old 7
p.c. rate if it had been able to pay 7 p.c.
Actually for the years 1915 and 1916 it
paid 6 p.c. and for 1917 only 5 p.c. What
the investor considered a fair return is
shown by the , dealings in the “C” shares,
as limited to a 7 p.c. dividend. In June,
1914, when the full rate was being paid, the
£6 shares were sold as high as £8 each, and
on the average prices the investor was quite
satisfied with a return at the rate of 5 5-8
p.c. At June, 1916, when the div. had
dropped to 6 p.c. the shares were bought at
a price yielding 6| p.c. In December last,
when the dividend had got down to only 5
p.c., and the future was uncertain (and the
latter is a big factor), shares were bought
at a price yielding 7 1-8 p.c. Finally, dur-
ing last month, when the 7 p.c. dividend had
been restored and there was the promise of
an 8 p.c. in the future, the shares were
being bought at a price which returned
0 7-8 p.c. Not even in the darkest patch
did the investor demand 8 p.c. When this
increased rate is being paid it will be inter-
esting to see exactly what the market con-
siders a fair return. The result of the
Supreme Court investigation was the finding
that since 1912, when the Gas Act was
enforced, the cost of production had in-
creased by over Is. 2d. per 1000 ft.
The cost of coal and oil had ad-
vanced 50 p.c. in the five years. So the
standard price to the consumer was raised
from 3s. 6d; per 1000 ft. to 4s. Bd.. but the
Government stepped in and clipped off 3d.,
this “concession being made by the Board in
view of the very heavy burdens already being
borne by the community.”
A further glance at the balance-sheet
figures shows a very satisfactory increase in
the sale of residuals:—

year.

been raised in debentures, while the co. is
depending on temporary loans to the extent
of £342,366.

The Sydney Perpetual has just done what
The Bulletin for some time has been sug-
gesting some of the trustees' might advan-
tageously do; strengthen their positions by
getting in a little of their uncalled capital
and investing it in War Doan. This co.’s
nominal capital is one million (100,000
shares of £lO each) and its paid capital
£50,000 (= 10s. per share). Now a further
ss. per share has been called up, and the
£25,000 thus raised goes into the new War
Loan. During the past four years, after
paying the 10 p.c. dividend (£5000) there
has been left an average of £7950 per annum
to add to reserves. So there should still
be a fine margin between net profits and a
similar rate of dividend on the increased
capital. Besides, the co.’s business is grow-
ing very nicely, the value of trusts adminis-
tered having expanded in this way
1898. . £3,367,255 I 1916. . £12,604,680
1908.. 6,739,048 | 1917.. 13,102,290
1912.. 9,791,590 | 1918.. 14,057,149
The co. shows its net profits in a somewhat
different way to the table at the head of
these remarks. The net profits for each of
the past four years is given as: 1915,
£10,970; 1916, £9490; 1917, £12,902, and.
1918, £14,031. The additions in the table,
besides including the amounts written off pre-
mises, are made up of appropriations to what
is termed a “Commission reserved account.”
A trustee co. gets its commissions in two
ways. The Perpetual, for instance, charges
from Ito p.c. on the corpus or capital value ;
2| p.c. on the first £50,000; 1| p.c. on the
second £50,000; and 1 p.c. on anything in
excess of £lOO,OO0 —a charge which on a
£50,000 estate administered for 20 years
works out at only one-eighth per cent, per
annum. Then on all income it charges from
21 to 5 p.c. Instead of crediting to one
particular year the whole of the corpus
commission on an estate entrusted to its
care, it reserves portion in many cases, and
draws on this reserved amount from time to
time as the estate is being wound up. This
reserved commission account now stands at
£45,446.
The balance-sheet figures have moved this
way in 20 years:—
1898. 1908. 1918.
Capital.. ..£25,000 £25,000 £59,^25
Liabilities .. 30 196 345
Liquid assets . . 23,067 24,622 66,289
Mortgages, etc.. . 8,031 45,084 17,642
Premises . . 10,050 16,050 114,000
The shareholders’, funds are thus snugly
placed. From the clients’ point of view the
position is equally comforting. The Per-
petual has a very fine record of 31 years
behind it; and besides the co.’s own funds
there is still £925,000 of capital callable to
protect their interests, in the remote possi-
bility of anything going wrong.

The Bank oe Queensland: —

This is the third half-year of the amalga-
mated Royal of Queensland and the Bank
of North Queensland. The new bank seems
to be gradually getting into its stride, and its
disclosed gross profits steadily increase. Of
course, it is yet too early for the full advan-
tages of the amalgamation to show up to any.
extent. One very satisfactory feature of
the Bank of Queensland is that the cus-
tomers of the two old banks have complete
confidence in the amalgamated bank’s man-
agement, which is shown by the fact that
the new bank has .apparently lost practically
none of the business of both the old shows.
As in the case of the proposed amalgamation
of the National and Colonial, this confidence
is due not only to a belief in .the ability of
the management, but also to the fact that
prior to amalgamating each management
had gone thoroughly into the other’s affairs
and carefully valued every asset. In this
way shareholders and customers had the
guarantee of both managements that the
amalgamated bank’s assets were worth all
they were claimed to be.
Nominal capital is £1,000,000 and
£450,000 has been paid up. Visible reserves,
after paying the dividend, amount to £9142.
Deposits total £2,928,451, and other liabili-
ties, £230,962; total. £3,165,41.4. So the
bank holds about £6 18s. of the public’s
money for every £1 of the shareholders’
funds. Of the assets £1,244,530 is held in
liquid form, equal to over 39 p.c. of the total
liabilities. The position from the customers’
point of view is thus quite comfortable.
Advances, etc., a year ago stood at
£2,109,892, £1,930,220 six months ago, and
£2.230,094 as at July 31 last. Practically
the. whole of the increase represents ad-
vances to customers under the last War
Loan scheme. • Premises are still included
at £157,500, and apparently the management
has not yet realised on its unnecessary pro-
perties. As to the future, the Bank of
Queensland is operating in a field of very
big possibilities, and as Queensland expands
so will this bank’s business.

The co. does not seem to be at all concerned
about the future of the beer industry.
Since the war began it has continued acquir-
ing pub properties and swelling its bank
overdraft. Towards the end of last year
the directors purchased one. of Fremantle’s
most central properties, and when the
accounts were made, up shareholders didn’t
get a taste. Now it is announced that
Westralia’s thirst for the co.’s bottled ales
and stout has got ahead of the plant, and
that the directors have decided to make
some additions estimated to cost about £5OOO.
To help finance this plant extension the
shareholders are again asked to forego a
dividend. Trading profit has increased, but
the cost of manufacturing and other charges
have been making swifter pace, as will be
seen from a comparison of-recent June half-
years : —-
Prolits from
Sales. Bents. Expenses.
1913 .. .. £12,888 £B4l £7,808
1914 .. .. 11,807 ■ 852 8,010
1916 .. .. 13,738 736 9,353
1917 . . . . 12,641 883 9.477
1918 .. .. 16,073 1328 12,366
However, the management lately set out to
even things up a little by increasing its
prices.
The purchase of properties has naturally
weakened the cash position. Five years ago
the co. owed the bank only £B2 and sundry
creditors £14,794. The bank overdraft now
stands at £18,856 and other liabilities amount
to £7746. The co. thus owes £11,776 more
than it did five years ago. In the same
period more or less liquid assets have moved
front__ £17.040 to £23,687 —an increase of
£6647. Thus the cash position has become
£5129 poorer than it was five years . ago.
At June 30 last liabilities exceeded liquid
assets by £2915. In the five years the mixed
item of “freehold and leasehold properties,
plant and machinery, rolling-stock, goodwill,
water supply, etc.,” has grown from £104,856,
against which there was it depreciation re-
serve of £14,567, to £136,268, with a reserve
,of £17,255. That gives a net increase of
£28,724 in the book value of fixed assets.
So, provided ample allowance has been
made for depreciation, and after allowing
for the £lO,OOO of new capital got in during
1914, the co. is £13,595 better off than it
was five years ago. Whether going in for
more pubs was good business, only the
future can tell —and the future of beer is
very uncertain. The Cold Tea party is very
energetic nowadays, and there’s certainly
going to be a big scrap over the liquor ques-
tion when Wilhelm’s little affair is over and
the six o’clock war-time closing legislation
lapses.

The latest copper-smelting plant to be erected in
Australia is that of the Mount Cuthbert Company
in North Queensland. It consists of buildings, power-
plant with boilers, generating sets and blowers,
blast furnace, copper converters and converter re-
fining plant, blister gathering and cleaning shed,
also steel bins for ore and coke. Wherever possible
the plant is electrically driven. The whole of the
plant was supplied by the Austral Otis Engineering
Co. of Melbourne, who have made numerous smelting
plants working in Australia at present.— Advt. [2

The 42-years-old Adelaide Steam Tug Co.
lias voted itself out of existence. At the
meeting which carried a motion for liquida-
tion chairman ,1. R. Corpe explained that
the Outer Harbor gave the eo. its first bad
knock, by enabling the largest ocean
steamers to tie up right alongside a railway
nne, instead of lying off several miles outside
imrgs and letting their passengers be con-
veyed to the jetty and their cargo lightered
op the Port Iliver. Since then the deepen-
ing of Port Pirie has further cut down the
need for lighters and tugs, and the co. has
’been reduced practically to the towing of
its own boats.” So it has arranged for the
Adelaide Steamship Co. to take over all its
little fleet and fixings for £55,000 in cash.
At .Tune 30 the co. bad 22 vessels of a book
value of £39,976; plant and stores, £7241;
freeholds, £4562, and net cash assets of
£30,849.
* * * *
A lot of attention has been given lately
to some of the older goldfields in the N. S.
Hales central west. The old Stuart Town
held has come into the limelight again, as
a result of an argument which is proceeding
over the possession of the old German Jack
mine, held by H. A. Hannelle.y, of Sydney,
since 1916, after being idle for 20 years.
Good stone was taken out of the show in
the old days, but the water was afterwards
allowed to pour in, and a lot of cash will
have to be spent before the picks can reach
the reefs _ again. Nevertheless there is
enough faith in the old place to cause a
syndicate to turn eyes towards the mine. 11.
Keith, of Dubbo, has accordingly moved that
Hennelle.v be no longer heard on the leases
for his failure to comply with labor con-
ditions. Tne issue has become complicated
by the fact that Ilennelley claims that liis
sop with the A.I.F. has an interest in the
mine, which brings into operation the
Department’s policy of excusing all such
absentees. Anyway, the Minister is to decide
the mattei\ The syndicate mentioned has
already taken an option over Hunt’s leases,
covering the old German Jack Extended, on
condition that the lessee unwaters the shaft
and exposes the reef, from which the syndi-
cate will extract a crushing and pay a lump
sum for the interest in the show if the gold
comes out. On the same field a mild rush
has taken place for the Post Office claims,
which were worked extensively a few years
ago, when a fine battery was busy. Better
methods of treatment have aroused the new
hopes in this area.
* * * *
The old Sunny Corner mine (N.S.W.),
which paid away £94,000 in dividends before
it closed down 20 years ago when copper
was about £4B a ton and silver at 2s. 2d. an
ounce, has got it,s wind again and is now
paying well. Wilkinson and party are
getting out a big body of the stone,
about 100 tons per week being treated.
The average ton contains 20oz. silver, 11
p.c lead, 14 p.c. zinc, 24 p.c. copper, and
4dwt. of gold. The present party has had
the mine 14 months and the returns
have paid from the start. Good offers have
come from old hands who know the geology
of the field, but Wilkinson isn’t selling.
A casual survey of the mine shows that
there are several years’ supply of ore in
sight on the 25 acres. With more labor on
the show the syndicate will erect a larger
treatment plant to avoid the greater part
of the £5 6s. a ton for transport to the coast
and treatment charges. The stuff can be
mined for 11s. a ton and treated on the sur-
face for about 28s.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) made a net profit
of £37,433 for 1917, and £32,500 was distri-
buted in dividends. The ore treated totalled
159.397 tons and the gold yield realised
£232,550. Ore reserves are estimated at
nearly four years’ suppiy at tne present rate
of extraction. With a production valued at
millions sterling to its credit the Gwalia
is one of the few remaining of the 90-odd
Westralian gold-mines that have maintained
dividends to date. Like most other mines
that can show payable ore and good pros-
pects the Gwalia did not at time of flotation
come before the public in the ordinary way.
It had a large capital, and a big sum was
set aside for working capital. But it was
all subscribed privately, and the shares only
appeared in the market at 100 per cent, pre-
mium. The reason for this was that a big
tonnage of payable stone was in sight, and
the outlook for good profits was very favor-
able. Dividends panl by the co. to date
amount to well over one million sterling.
What with cyanide vats wanted and poor
labor supply, Ghilde Harold, Laverton
W.A.), isn’t yet on the dividend list. Still
manager Ditchburn has not a doubt that
once he gets the plant complete and can
work full time things will so hum as to
ensure repayment of what has gone into the
mine and towards machinery. The debt
under those heads is £19,000. with £l9OO as
a contingent liability to vendors, and £l3OO
owing on current debts. Funds to square all
these items are to come from a modest
reserve of 10,000 tons opened up above the
200 ft. level, and the stuff to be opened up in
the future. Ditchburn has driven 355 ft.
at No. 2 level on a reef 7ft. wide and assay-
ing 245. With costs as they are in Westralia
and mine supplies at abnormal prices, there
can’t be much margin out of stuff of
that grade. Ditchburn has a shallow shaft
600 ft. south of the main shaft, and he is
itching to dive into the reef there, as he has
been picking out some Bdwt. prospects from
an Bft. formation. A shoot of that kind
would sweeten up the grade all round. That
is what the Childe needs to get a go on.
From Westonia: —
Edna May Deeps’ new manager is faced
with a very severe battle indeed, as when
he starts chasing* that £90,000 profit which
Stuckey says exists above the bottom level, he will
come to the conclusion it is not a mine manager
but a Hercules that is required for that job. Any-
how, it will probably be found that there are not
more than 1000 tons payable ore available above the
No. 3 level; not more than 3000 tons (not including the
block of ore which the Mines Department insists
must remain intact for safety purposes) above No. 4
level; and 10,000 tons above No. 5 or bottom level—
lOdwt. ore and over considered payable grade. The
rich ore shoot at No. 5 level does not exceed 50ft. in
length. In that case the total profit in prospect will not
approach anything near £90,000. The new manager’s
first detailed report to directors may be taken as a
basis to decide which are the more correct esti-
mates —Stuckey’s or those above.
By the way, it is not generally known that the
Deeps cementation process was tried and failed
utterly in the Central workings, the joint Edna and
Central deluge washing it out.
Mararoa directors are inviting share-
holders to enlarge the co. from 100.000
shares to 120,000, paid to Bs. Half the
new issue is ito go to the Light of Asia
syndicate for their Cue mine, besides £BOOO
in cash. The Itae party in the last four
years has sent 19,009 tons of lodwt. stone
out of the Asia to the mill, leaving the new
comers the job of proving whether the shoots
live down. Still the purchase looks well
on bottom prospects. Out of the Mararoa
possibly another 2s. may be scraped, but
w r ith £lO.OOO cash in reserve the co. ought to
be able to keep going at its own claim with-
out calls and put through a fair develop-
ment programme at the Asia. Off the winze
80ft. below the bottom 310 ft. level at the
Light of Asia, 500 tons of 30dwt. stone
have been beaten out, so it looks as though
Llenderson has good reason for advising the
Mararoa shareholders to try their luck in
the Cue mine. About £7500 will see develop-
ment work done and prove if the 600 ft. of
payable reefs worked in the upper levels of
the Light of Asia live down.

“Oof B’rd”: Strong exception is being
takeh in the West to a statement contained
in the Governor’s speech at the recent open-
ing of Parliament that the mining industry
is in a satisfactory condition. After steadily
dwindling for some years, the gold produc-
tion has since 1913 shown an alarming
decline, until now it is not half of what it
used to be, and the number of men employed
in the industry has decreased by over 50 per
cent. To state that mining in Westralia is
in a satisfactory condition when many of the
o’d-time flourishing camps have relapsed into
almost a wilderness and others are stagnant,
and not one is turning out the quantity of
gold it did a few years ago, is too serious to
be considered as a joke.
* , * * *
Floated with a whoop of optimism, New
Boddington (W.A.) now admits a loss on
last year’s operations of £632, arid adds that
one lode, which was spoken of so well in
the prospectus, has proved unpayable. The
co. has about £230 of its original capital
left, and in summing up the prospects the
manager says that so far as can be "t
present there is a shoot of about 200 ft. in
length at depth, but being some distance
fiom the share it is expensive to get at. He
lias no doubts about it going down, but offers
no opinion as to whether it is ever likely to
benefit shareholders.
Things are just so-so at the Ajax, Dayles-
ford (Vic.), for while 33000 z. have been got
from 5359 tons and Is. 6d. has been paid in
divs., it is clear that the big hole in the reef
above No. S and No. 7 levels means that a
new attraction is badly needed in the old
mine. To provide this, Lewis is crosscutting
to try for the North Ajax shoot. Some 100 ft.
of driving will show whether this punch is
to be effective. Values of the stone coming
from the reef at No. 6 south keep tantalis-
ingly close to the pay-line, but that is the
way with so many of the Daylesford forma-
tions. They prospect well, but in the battery
won’t tally out properly. The worst thing
for Ajax is that Larsen at the Ajax North,
next door, has practically shut down his
mine because the men won’t knuckle to
Arbitration Court award conditions. Pro-
specting in that quarter aids the Ajax. With
the drift of miners to other fields no North
developments are likely to happen along to
hearten up the Ajax. The Nuggetty couple
also are on duffer stone, so that generally
mining isn’t rosy on the Ajax line.
“Good Australian” : So T. I. Greenway is
out “to shake up oil problems in Australia”
and “incidentally to divert the local oil-flow
to the Anglo-Persian Co.” (B. 22/8/T8).
Isn’t Australia entitled to its own oil, and
should not the Australian navy have the
first call on Papua’s oil? When will this
business end of “diverting” all the best of
our products to London and Liverpool? The
Anglo-Persian Co. made huge profits out of
the. British Admiralty last year, and the
whole of its crude petroleum was* shipped
and used as oil fuel by the navy. There was
none available for refining as kerosene,
petrol and lubricants, the three articles Aus-
tralia must have. The likelihood of the
Anglo-Persian Co. erecting a refinery in
Sydney to treat local petroleum for kero-
sene and petrol, etc., is remote. Anyhow, it is
not a question of allowing outsiders into the
Papuan fields, but of allowing Australian
mining men to develop the known oilfields
there for the benefit of the Commonwealth,
its people and its Government and navy,
first, last and all the time.
Lateral deposits extending into the walls
are becoming quite a feature of lode forma-
tions in the Cloncurry district. Quite re- ,
cently a lateral deposit was discovered at
the 80ft. level from No. 6 shaft in the
Trekelano mine, and this new make is now
officially stated to measure 47ft., and
worth 14 p.c. copper. A similar deposit has
also _ been discovered at the Answer mine,
bulking 16 p.c. and extending 28ft. into the
footwall. It is mainly owing to deposits of
this nature that little St. Mungo has paid so
many dividends. Gougers frequently drop
upon deposits of ore bulking 20 p.c. copper,
and because these peter out quickly the
general impression is that all the Cloncurry
mines are shallow. The Hampden’s 600 ft.
north level is in 420 ft., and the end shows
5 p.c. ore over a width of 30in.
Bonnie Dundee, Charters Towers (Q.),
recently crushed 366 tons for an average
yield of £3 9s. per. ton, and the latest report
from the mine is more cheerful than usual.
No. S level, which is the deepest, has been
driven 24ft. east of the shaft and is facing
20in. of good stone, the quality having im-
proved. The western drive is also in 24ft.,
and the face shows 9in. of fair stone. Clark’s
realised £4912 for its crushing of 501 tons,
and although this is over £9 pe'r ton, it isn’t
quite so good as some previous returns.
Mount Malvern silver-lead (S.A.) has had
a good development at the 400 ft. level. The
main drive is in 172 ft., and the make of rich
ore assaying up to 77 p.q. lead and 16oz.
silver was met in crosscutting west. After the
workings have been timbered and secured it
is intended to open up the new make. Since
the mill started 775 tons of concentrates have
been sent to the smelter for a net return of
£9300. Charges are heavy, for metal con-
tents figure at £15,500.
* .* * *
The Speck’s carbide of calcium factory at
Electrona, near Hobart, had produced over
70 tons of carbide when operations had to
stop for want of suitable electrodes. Those
imported from Britain proved defective. The
Hun, with his capacity for taking pains,
made a special study of electrodes, and while
the easy-going British Freetrader could
secure the articles from Germany, even the
technical side of the business was neglected.
Now both the Britisher and the Australian
are studying the apparently simple art of
making sticks of hard carbon that will stand
a very high temperature. ’

LOHENGRIN’S FAREWELL TO THE SWAN.


TO CURE COUGHS USE PINE TAR
AND SUGAR.
A SPECIALIST’S SUGGESTION.
Obstinate tickling coughs, the kind that
rack you to pieces and keep you awake at
night, can be quickly relieved by 10 or 15
drops of bitrate of tar on a lump, of sugar,
and letting it slowly dissolve in the mouth.
The specialist who recommends this plan
says it is superior to anything he has ever
tried, and may be used with perfect safety
and success on children. To make an excel-
lent inexpensive cough syrup which children
like, he says there is nothing better at any
price than half a pound of granulated sugar
dissolved in half a pint of hot water, and
then stir in 2oz. of bitrate of tar. When
cool pour into a pint bottle, and it is ready
for use. Prom half a teaspoonful to a tea-
spoonful every hour or two will quickly
relieve coughs and colds, and if regularly
used for a few days will give remarkable
benefit in cases of catarrhal and bronchial
affections.— Advt.

Rockhampton (Q.) lost a strenuous citi-
zen when John Willis crossed into the
shadows a few days ago. He. was an
engineer, a Manchester man, who arrived in
Queensland in 1866 and did a lot of work
in his line. He also tried his hand at gold
fossicking, ran the tin-cutting machine at
Lake’s Creek, helped build bridges on the
Emu Park line, and did a bit of railway
construction and wharf-building. For some
years Willis was a vigorous Labor man and
a most painfully frequent and fluent inter-
jeetor at public meetings. He was 52 years
when the sand ran out.
Adelaide has borrowed Ambrose Roberts,
the handsome Welshman, who
was lately president of the
Council of Churches in Vic-
toria. Roberts looks like a
matinee idol made up for the
part, but is, as a matter of fact,
a fiery and effective preacher,
like his father before him. He
starts after the Devil in the
Manthorpe Memorial (Congre-
gational) pulpit in Adelaide
with the beginning of Septem-
ber.
Lieut. Harry Liddell Mack,
R.N.R., D.S.C., first saw the
light in Warrnambool (Vic.).
Before the war he was helping
to drag dredges across the
oceans to people who were too
tired or too something to build
them themselves, and it speaks
a lot for his confidence in these
unwieldly fliers that he took
his wife out in one for the
Launceston Harbor Board. He
navigated, al«o, the Cleveland
Bay for the Townsville Harbor
Board in 1911. When the war-
gong sounded Mack was on his
way back to the Cold Country,
after one of these pilgrimages,
and he sent word from New
York that he was ready to bear
a hand. He want to the Ork-
neys patrol in October, 1914,
and stayed there till December,
1915, when he was sent to the
Mediterranean, and has been there ever
since. His job is “escorting.”
Recentiv dead in N.-W. Tasmania. John
Newbolt Wilson, formerly a major-general in
the Indian army. He had lived in Tas. for
over 30 years, coming there on the flood of
immigration of retired Indian service men,
which also brought Edward Braddon. Wil-
son’s favorite hobby was the study of Orien-
tal languages, and amongst those who have
time for such light recreations he was some-
thing of an authority. He just missed the
81st milestone.
Warrant-Officer A. R. Falconer, who put
in three strenuous years on active service in
Egypt and France, recently stepped ashore
at Sydney wearing the M.S.M. decoration.
His son, Warrant-Officer L. Falconer, has
achieved the same distinction. Dad and two
sons met on many battlefields in France.
Two of the boys are still carrying on, and
dad is doing important military service with
the home garrison artillery.

“ Jermyn Street,” about a cobber :
Passed out, “Dolly” Brunton (full title Captain
Laurence Brunton, M.C., 4th Divisional Artillery),
one of the wildest—and last—of the reckless old
First Division originals. At Messines rumor got to
him that there were refreshments in a dugout over
towards the Germans. The day was at about noon,
blazing hot and dusty. Accompanied by his batman,
who always followed him on his insane expeditions,
the bar was reached, and beside it was a brand-new
German field gun, fresh from the factory, undamaged
and all complete. Also, there were masses of
ammunition flying around, so Dolly and the bat-
man went into action straight away. When the
spoils of the battle were later assembled in Bailleul"
Square for inspection by George V., this capture was
the most complete and undamaged of the many pieces
of ordnance on view, and it was Certainly the only
piece that had been captured by a single Australian
hand. I believe it goes to Parramatta. On another occa-
sion one of Brunton’s pals had been killed early in the
battle of Bullecourt, at a point right in the front

The appointment of Rev. W. Hayden
to the new R.C. bishopric of Wil-
cannia-Forbes (N.S.W.) recalls the many
occasions that half-brother Tom has
missed the ’piscopal chariot. Irish traditions
invariably point to a College Professor —
Moran, Carr, Mannix, are examples. Hay-
den has been in the running more than once.
Of late he was badly beaten for Christchurch
(M.L.) by old pupil Matt Brodie, son of an
Auckland publican, and he was only a neck
ahead of Terence Maguire, the son of a
Macleay River (N.S.W.) dairy-farmer, for
Lismore. Tom’s ill fortune is due to the publi-
cation of such advanced views that lie was
duly reported to Rome after incurring the
displeasure of Moran. However, though his
episcopal prospects were dimmed, the highly
intellectual priest retained his post as presi-
dent of the Manly College.
“Z.” : When one hears the occasional glib
tipping of a “new Cardinal,” the re-
minder comes of how a card, ever got
here. When Moran was called back
from Australia to be Archbishop of Dublin,
such an almighty howl went up that
Leo XIII. heard it in Rome and squashed
the appointment. Stormy interviews resulted
in Moran being solaced with the Red
Hat and a return to his old job in Australia
—just as present Arch. Kelly, when lie set out
a few years back to see about a bishopric in
India or elsewhere, was glad to return. There
are confused ideas regarding the importance
of the Cardinalate. A man need only be a
deacon to be one, provided he’s ordained
priest within 12 months. Ceretti’s mission
here was to spy out the land and contrast
the two countries in their importance —
America and Australia. Evidently he was
satisfied a Roman candle wasn’t necessary
here, as he floated out and we got a smaller
man. Gazing at the present unimpressive
hierarchy I can see only one runner for the
Australian Hat —Mannix.
H. Y. Hodson, the latest chaplain to win
the M.C., is one of twin brothers who are
priests in the Anglican Bush Brotherhood
at Cloncurry (N.Q.). The decoration was
earned last May, near Salonica, where Hod-
son is chaplain with an English regiment. He
went alone 300 yards across “no man’s
land” under fire, and brought back two
wounded soldiers from the Bulgar’s wire.
On the third trip he found a man so badly
hurt that he could not be moved without
assistance. The fire was terriffic, but Hodson
refused to leave his wounded mate, and
stayed with him all day under rifle and
artillery hail. One shell buried itself under
the stretcher, but luckily proved a “dud.”
After -dark, Hodson went for and led a
rescue party out; but in the meantime the
Bulgars had carried the wounded man into
their lines as a prisoner.
W. B. Rounsevell (mentioned in The
Bulletin's list of September birthdays as
76 on 23rd), was four times Treasurer of
S. Aus. There were at one time three Ben-
jamins in the Adelaide House of Assembly,
and W.B. was distinguished as “Big Ben.”
He rarely misses his daily outing in his
carriage and pair.
Cuthbert Maclean, a Devonport (M.L.)
parson, reckoned that the Anglican sky-
pilot’s license which he held wasn’t enough
for war purposes, so he went in for more
practical training, and is now a squadron-
commander of the Royal Flying Corps in
France. He has just received the Cross of
the French Legion of Honor. Previously, as
lieutenant of the Fusiliers, he won the M.C.
Maclean was born and schooled at Wanganui
(M.L.), where his father was Anglican vicar
in the long ago.
Charles Goode, merchant and philanthro-
pist, of Adelaide, has decided to take a rest,
and has resigned his seat on the local Board
of the A.M.P. Society. He is becoming sadly
deaf, and is less able to attend to business
than he was when he pluekily accepted a re-
nomination two years ago, at the age of 90.
This leaves as the oldest member of the
Board Edwin Smith, a comparative juvenile
of 88, rather touched in the eyesight, but
otherwise almost as keen and regular a man
of business as ever.
Captain A. T. Pringle, V.D., who died
lately, aged 55, was an enthusiast in the old
N. S. Wales volunteer army. For a long
time he was in charge of the clerical branch
of the railway divisional engineer’s office at
Bathurst, where he joined the service 34
years ago. He started the cadet shooting
competitions, commanded the 42nd Regiment
at the last militia camp, got the V.D. when
lan Hamilton was here some years back,
and was a vastly disgusted veteran when
red tape, or something equally foolish, would
not let him go to the war.
Thomas Murdoch, master warden, Hobart
Marine Board, is conspicuous mostly in the
Speck by the galvanic shocks he administers
on behalf of progress. He inherits this trait
from his grandfather, Dr. Murdoch, who
arrived in Van Diemen’s Land nearly a cen-
tury ago, and was associated with Bishop
Wilson in easing the convicts’ lot. Mur-
doch has skimmed
over the principal
continents in one
capacity or another.
As well as being
master warden, he
is Consul for Bel-
gium, ex-M.L.C..
and ex-President of
the Chamber of
Commerce. lie w T as
organiser of the
Tourist Depart-
ment and has so
many schemes up
his sleeve that he
can hardly get his
arm in. For a crust
he bosses the firm
of Murdoch Bros.,
merchants.

HE AND L
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rich man has his soft ease,
His gilt cars
And choice of many wines,
But I thank God for the big seas,
The bright stars
And the night-wind in the pines.
Though, being not of the one plan,
The same mould,
I sink where he may swim—-
A fig, I say, for the rich man,
His heaped gold
And the joy it brings to him.
Though into his mansion scents stray,
And friends throng,
To make his ease-hours bright.
Yet I’m content with the wind's way,
The sea’s song
And the pine-trees in the night.
Though he may have,what his gold buys,
And Joy find
In what his gold has bought,
lie may not think with the night-skies,
The cool wind,
And the dark seas lost in thought.
Yet, being not of the same plan,
The one mould —
As one at a glanqe may see —
’Tis little I care for the rich man,
His heaped g01d....
And ’tis, little he cares for me.
N.S.W.

HER: “Why don’t you enlist instead of get-

seas. Which is only commonsense, seeing
that in these times of submarines and pro-
miscuous mines a vessel’s safety may at any
moment hinge on her look-out’s promptness
of vision. But there’s another way in
which the department might do some useful
work. It might insist on periodic medical
overhauls of ships’ officers. The man on the
bridge might be medically fit for the job, or
he might not. There was the case of the
Makambo, awhile-back piled up on Lord
Howe Island while her skipper, who was in
charge on the bridge, was unconscious in a
fit, which the N. S. Wales Marine Inquiry
Court held was not due to any fault of his
own. Quite unsuspected by himself an after-
guard might be liable to collapse in a
moment of stress and a previous hint from
a doctor might save a big manifest of trouble.
Melbourne’s latest murder is mostly a
story of exceeding casualness. In ’l4 Roma
Thomas, tailoress, met and married Samuel
Smith, sailor, and became a happy bride for
24 hours. Then Samuel went to sea, and.
leaving his ship at Sydney, disappeared in
an offhand and unobtrusive way. Mrs. Smith
worked on at her trade and acquired sundry
casual friends, including one Barleycorn,
alias Tanglefoot. On the evening of August
20 she was noticed inquiring for Barley foot
or Tanglecorn in company with an unknown
stranger. They acquired four bottles, and
adjourned to the lady’s abode, in a Carlton
slum, where, a post-mortem, showed, she was
slain two hours later. That is all that is
known at time of writing. The only con-
crete, unchallengeable fact ascertained so far
is that Roma Smith is quite dead.
Half-way between Thursday Island
and the western Papuan port of Darn,
a dot on the chart indicates the posi-
tion of Yam Island, Torres Strait. It is
mostly hill, beaches, coeoanuts and niggers.
Lying, as it does, out of the track of the
pearling and beche-de-mer vessels, visitors
are infrequent and communications are not
overdone, yet on this lonely place lives a
white woman —Mrs. Smallwood, Government
teacher. She is delicate and elderly, but she
feels no fear at living surrounded by semi-
civilised blacks and with no other person of
her own color upon the island. She has been
there for years, and the natives treat her
with the greatest respect, and look upon her
as a mother. Which, suggests that despite
civilising influences some virtues remain to
the niggers. Mrs. Smallwood has had some
unique experiences. At Madras (India) she
witnessed the festival of Ram and Setha —
the wedding of Justice and Mercy—-and was
the first European so privileged for 50 years,
and the first white woman ever.
A ripple on the surface of events hap-
pened in London when George Heyl and
Jacob Stoner were accused of trying, under
suspicious circumstances, to dispose of cer-
tain goods, including 500,000 rifles, a Dread-
nought, and immense quantities of artillery,
etc. One theory is that they intended to
steal the Dreadnought from Brazil if they
could find a “fence” to take it. Stoner’s
real name was Stein, but a
detective said he was above
suspicion, so that suspicion
couldn’t fall on him unless
it flew up above him and
then dropped. It is a large
business. There have been
few larger since an Austrian
Minister of Finance retired
early last century, leaving a
deficit of four tons of gold
in his accounts.

South Australia’s vintage
for the last season is easily
the best on record, going
well over five million gal-
lons, nearly doubling last
year’s. The previous record,
in 1913, was under four mil-
lions. The value of the pre-
sent lot may be something
over half a million sterling.
About half of it was made
in the Barossa-Tanunda dis-
trict. The famous show-
vineyards on the slopes be-
hind Adelaide had unexpect-
edly little to do with the
result (only 400,000 gal-
lons). There are officially
stated to be 73 wine-makers
in S.A., and 15 of them
turned out less than 1000
gallons each. Methods of
production are so much
improved nowadays that it is not likely that
any of the small growers will die through
drinking their own wine, as used to be said
of them in the early days.
What is believed to be the first Australian
will made by word of mouth has been
admitted to probate by S. Aus. Supreme
Court. The law seems to be quite clear that
a soldier on active service need not trouble
about the formalities required from other
people in making a will. In this case the
soldier had actually signed and dated a
printed form, in the presence of witnesses;
but as it did not contain anything else the
Court held it quite valueless. A definite
statement of his intentions, however, made
at the same time, was recalled in some strong
BUSINESS AND PLEASURE.
“Where did y' get the pin, Bill?"
“Swapped it with the wife for a washtub
affidavits, and admitted as a proper will.
Chief Justice Murray remarked that the in-
tention to make a will was clear, the wishes
for disposition of deceased’s property were
clear and the mere fact of his having taken
the oath and gone into camp made him tech-
nically on active, service. Such a will would
not apply to land, but in this case there was
none. The matter was piloted through the
Supreme Court by Talbot Smith, an Adel-
aide lawyer who has during the war made
the wills of nearly 4000 S.A. soldiers as a
friendly act.
* * * *
The recognition of the Papal flag as one
of the friendly banners that may be flown in
Australia has aroused much controversy of
an illogical kind. The Pope is still the elec-
tive monarch —perhaps the only elective
monarch left in the world—of a very small
State. He has his own army, but not his
own navy. (It was declared in the South
African war time that an inland country
couldn’t possess a navy, and that its flag on
the sea was a pirate’s ensign, so the Boer
fleet never sailed.) Above everything, the
Pope is the only monarch who is sole head of
a church, and a world-wide church at that.
The early Caliphs ruled all Islam spiritually
and temporarily, but after them, church and
State broke apart in many directions. Brig-
ham Young professed at least to be both
spiritual and temporal, but he was very
temporary, and he is dead. The Mahdi and
the Khalifa were not cases in point. George
V. doesn’t head the Anglican Church in
America, and anyhow his creed never set
up a flag. A Baptist flag is impossible, for
there is neither a Baptist king nor a king-
dom. Whether the Pope is a friendly sove-
reign has been disputed, but anyhow he has
never said anything to the contrary. The
really or nominally Roman Catholic peoples
who are in, or belong to, our fighting line—
counting Belgium. France, Portugal. Italy,
Brazil, Cuba, Poland, the British Empire
(8,000,0001, the United States (12.000,000!
are far more than those on the other side.
So we might as well recognise the unique
and very ancient banner which is, in a
sense, our banner, and get out of it any
benefit that is possible. It may not always
be unique, however. If conquered Palestine
is re-established as a neutralised or protected
Hebrew State, then the Jewish flag will be
on the same plane with that of St. Peter, for
the king or president at Jerusalem will be
the head of Jewry in a religious as well as
a political sense. And he will be a ruler
with a sea-coast, which the Pope isn’t, and
a Stock Exchange which the Pope hasn’t.

KNOCKED.
(N.Y.D. Pulmonary Tuberculosis.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Deeper than wounds the war has struck in me;
Harsher than wounds the smart that I must feel,
Bearing, no proud scars that my mates may sec
And yet a wearing hurt that may not heal!
There is a soothing balm in warm blood let
To serve one’s country. But like this to fall
From the brave ranks of manhood strongly set
In Freedom's service, yields no ease at all.
How often in a weariness of hours
Of dust, suspense and dread, within my breast
Have visions stirred of wounds—scent pure as
flowers,
Washed limbs, clean sheets and placid seas of
rest!
And .then, when dread from dreariness was dead,
The war’s worst rage would leave me unalarmed.
Only a strange, strange hope from out me lied
Each time it passed, and left me still unharmed.
I, like a ship to proud adventure sent,
Unscarred by conflict, battered not by storm,
Drop from the line of battle imminent
A humbled victim to a boring worm.
Washed limbs, clean sheets —and yet no sense of
rest!
Record of service incompletely writ,
With no wound periods ; yet in my breast
A gnawing ill—-the bitterness of it!
Yet have I done at least as well as those
Who, safe at home, have bravely talked of death;
I, for my country, in the path I chose,
Have made a willing sacrifice of breath !
IN HOSPITAL.

THE PHONOGRAPH HORN.
HE horn of the phonograph is used to amplify the
tones produced in its sound-box. It does this by
increasing the energy and size of the sound-waves.
It should be understood that in the process of record-
ing the original sound-waves are taken from the air, as it
were, and translated into a microscopic line engraved in
wax, and that undoubtedly they are reduced both in
energy and size during the process. Also that when the
sound-box retranslates this line back into waves they still
lack both their original energy and dimensions.
The horn to perform its function must restore these,
and just to the extent that it is capable of delivering a
faithful counterpart of the original wave does it serve
its purpose.
THE SYMPHONETIC HORN.
HB Symphonetic Horn used in the Aeolian-Vocalion
is a clever and scientifically constructed device
made of ’cello wood sprung into position under
tension and amplified with metal.
The Symphonetic Horn is acclaimed by musicians
and experts to represent a notable advance in the develop-
ment of the Phonograph and Gramophone, because it faith-
fully reproduces the exact tone color of singer and instrument ,
and so perfectly does it accomplish this that it makes

“The Leader of Perfect Tone Reproduction.”

“L.L.W.” : “The Pariah” asserts re learn-
ing Japanese (B. 22/8/’18), “I know men
who wrote and spoke it in two years.” I
now introduce “Pariah” to Tsuchya, a Jap
student of English and Esperanto. “We
Japanese ourselves require about eight years’
school education (of which six years is com-
pulsory) to write a presentable letter or
read a newspaper, another four or five years
are not too excessive to understand such a
speech as in the Diet. Even tbfe Japanese
language transcribed into Roman alphabet is
not properly pronounced by foreigners,
except by an Esperantist. Therefore should
anybody intend to learn the Japanese lan-
guage, I would advise him to learn Esper-
anto first; the grammatical construction of
the latter will serve as a stepping-stone be-
tween the former and the English.” “The
Pariah” alleges that 3000 characters are
“all” you want in Japanese. Tsuchya says
10,000 out of a total of 80,000 must be
learnt before one can read an ordinary
newspaper. In Esperanto there are only
27 characters! And let the educational fad-
dists who introduced Japanese chew over
this. The roll of Esperantists in both Japan
and Australia is increasing by hundreds
yearly, while at the Sydney University
“there are many defaulters” from the 70
or so enthusiasts who last year began to
learn Japanese. “The Pariah” naively says
there must be some reason for this. For
his sake let me state the obvious reason.
Learning Japanese is an impractical task,
commercially worthless except for communi-
cation in a doubtful future with one nation ;
learning Esperanto is a practical and easy
task now with every non-savage nation on
earth.

A 14-year-old boy who deserved to be
tanned for nefarious improbability has tran-
spired in New England (N.S.W.). Nobody
could complain much of him laying a sleeper
on the railway. It was an old sleeper and
il: achieved no damage, and anybody might feel
called upon to do something with a sleeper.
After all a sleeper must be put somewhere.
