“Centurion”: The Germans punish desert-
ers by sending their womenfolk to tasks in
the front-line zone, but we do things more
humanely in Australia. Here the offender
may be shot or he is gaoled where lie is in-
capable of earning anything for wife and
children’s support, so they are faced with
nothing worse than prospective starvation,
his allotment being stopped forthwith and
his deferred pay forfeited in addition.
“Mac N.” : They were a dreadfully awk-
ward squad, and the wild sergeant-major
needed a breather, so he marched them into
the shade, ordered them to stand easy and
told them a story. “Look here, you boys;
when I was a tiny chap my dear old
mother gave me a little box of
soldiers. I liked them better than
any of my other toys and used to
play with them all day. But one
day we had to move, and in the
confusion they got lost. Well, I
was pretty near heart-broken about
it, and my mother did everything
she could to comfort me, saying:
‘Never mind, Sonny, some day you’ll get
your little wooden soldiers back again.’
And, by II — l, so I have! Squad— ’siiun!”
Business for the Twofold Bay (Eden,
N.S.W.) whalers has been slack for
some time, but it livened up a few days ago.
The men were bewailing their luck at miss-
ing one mammoth, reckoned by experts to
have been worth £9OO, when along sauntered
another “bumper” and calf. It didn't take
those well-known expert whalemen, the
Davidsons and mates, long to get busy and
they soon had the calf; but the mother was
another proposition. As soon as the har-
poon was fast, the monster promptly rang
up full steam and, with the boat in tow, set
off like greased lightning close in along the
shore. Suddenly the boat’s bows were
staved on a rock, one of the crew went over-
board and by the time he was picked up the
enraged lady was streaking into the southern
bay, still cuddling the shore. Another boat
was got out in pursuit. Agaiij the harpooner
made fast and again the nightmare tow pro-
ceeded, “humpy” tearing head-on for the
wharf. To dodge collision with the piles the
line was cast off for the second time. The
whale missed the wharf by a few feet, and
turned for the open sea, the whalers, again
having made fast, vainly trying to finish the
furious proceedings with the lance. Just
here the “killers” came into action with fine
dash and soon had the leviathan in a mad
frenzy that threatened to swamp the boat. In
the turmoil one of the chocks came away and
the line, running out with terrific speed,
jerked two of the crew overboard. They
were lugged aboard by good luck, and the
grand parade continued round the northern
bay and out for the rough open sea. Then
the whalers finally cut loose and watched
tlieir sturdy antagonist streak in a smother
of crimsoned foam for the horizon. The
fight had lasted over tea hours.

“That's the first time I have heard strong
drink called a necessity of life,” said Judge
Powers in the Arbitration Court, Melb., the
other day. Necessity knows no law, and
law, it appears, recognises few necessities.
“Equal pay for women,” is going to be a
live subject. Till recently every woman
had a sporting chance of matrimony, for the
surplus female wasn’t very numerous.
Therefore woman was looked upon as a
dilettante worker, filling in time till a
husband came along, with a baby to come
along later —a person who wouldn’t wait
to learn her business thoroughly because it
wasn’t her permanent business, and whom
it wasn’t worth while promoting. But the
war has made men so scarce, and will yet
make them so much scarcer, that the un-
married woman must be viewed in a differ-
ent light. A very large proportion of the sex
is going to work for life, and is to be treated
as a permanency toiling for permanent
wages, and with her old age to provide for
-—not as a temporary bit of fluff earning
money to buy a trousseau with. It must be
equal pay for equal work —or else polygamy.
“Dial-Sight”: I hasten to back up
The Bulletin’s support (22/8/T8) of the
Y.M.C.A. abroad. In the huts as close to
line as the “exigencies of the service” would
permit I have had warm shelter, seats to sit on,
writing material ad lib., groceries, etc., at a
lower price than from private vendors in
Blighty or France (canteen goods are free
of excise and other duties, hence cheaper),
lectures and concerts, and always a warm
welcome, to which I look back with grati-
tude. Against this, all I’ve paid for at the
Recreation Huts was tea at 10 centimes (Id.)
a cup. and biscuits 2d. a packet. In the
front lines or thereabouts I have had warm
cocoa, biscuits and cigarettes gratis. Seems
to me some blokes wouldn’t be satisfied if
they had this planet brought to them on a
silver platter. A part of the adverse criti-
cism emanates from the persons who suffer
from the peculiar mental kink which causes
them to imagine they are always being got
at. A simple summary of their debit and
credit transactions with the Y.M.C.A. ought
to remove that idea.
* * * *
A serious interference with the birth-rate
is foreshadowed by Melb. Argus, in
the event of Chief Sec. Bowser bring-
ing about his proposed reform providing
for the exclusion of infants under a certain
age from pictures-shows. “There are few
young married women,” declares Grandmam-
ma, “who do not sometimes sigh for the
unrestricted freedom and the jollity of the
earlier days; and occasional pleasures and
outings are necessary for their physical and
mental health”—these being provided by
picture-show managements, cheaply and in
ample quantities. In future, with the penal-
ties of wedlock increased by the deprivation
involved by staying at home to rock the baby
to sleep, forethoughtful Gertie will naturally
pause and ponder before che rushes into
marriage with impatient Ernest. The birth-
rate will slump in consequence, and Bowser
will presently be forced to permit a return
to the old, jolly state of affairs, and all will
be well once more—all, that is, except for
the infant, coming out at 11 p.m. into the
cold night-air after a warm, stuffy, comfort-
less evening among the whoop-and-wheeze
germs.

“Silas”: Saw a returned soldier near St.
Kilda (Vic.) railway station the other day.
He had a blue band about each arm, one
round each thigh, knee and ankle, another
round his hat, and the material of four more
floating steamer-wise from his shoulder-
blades. Need I add that the warrior
was so drunk that he asked : “Shay, cobber,
where have the tramsh gone that go to
Brighton?”

THE PRICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Did I give
Part of my youth, part of my soul,
That the song might live?
Did I part
With a fragment of joy for the beggar’s bowl,
With a beat of the heart ?
Have I known *
Pain that would rend, grief that would sear
From seed I have sown ?
Have I felt
Passion to kill, blindness of fear,
Till torn I have knelt
To my God
Agonised? Blood and sweat
Piercing the sod ?
I must give
That my song through the ages’ rust and fret
Shall blossom and live!
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFEE.

WHAT DOCTORS PRESCRIBE FOR
INDIGESTION.
Physicians who have specialised in the
treatment of stomach troubles are naturally
in a position to judge intelligently as to the
best thing to use. It is therefore a matter
of interest to those who suffer from indiges-
tion. gastritis, dyspepsia, pain after eating,
heartburn, etc., to learn that physicians who
have devoted their lives to the alleviation of
human suffering are more and more instruct-
ing these sufferers to get a package of Bisu-
rated Magnesia from the chemist and take
a half-tea spoonful in a little hot water imme-
diately after eating or whenever pain is felt.
Doctors who prescribe Bisurated Magnesia
do so because they know it gives quick relief
in nearly every instance—because it is not a
secret remedy, and because no ill-effect fol-
lows its use. For these reasons sufferers
from dyspepsia, indigestion and stomach
troubles generally are strongly advised to get
a package of Bisurated Magnesia from their
nearest chemist and begin its use without
delay.— Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A Government with a sense of dignity
wouldn’t allow its leg to be pulled by all and
sundry. Recently an Oppositionist asked
Watt if it was a fact that two London
papers (copies not produced) had said that
Hughes had said that “conscription” would,
under certain conditions, come about in
Australia? Whereupon Watt had Melbourne
raked for copies of the alleged papers, and
not finding them he wasteu public money
cabling to Hughes, and Hughes wasted pub-
lic money cabling back that it was a lie.
A man with a less telescopic leg would
simply have told the Oppositionist to
produce the papers. Similarly Laborite
McGarry, in N. S. Wales Legislature,
asked Premier Holman if he was aware
that a harmless nuisance called the
Millions Club, was in the habit of giv-
ing costly dinners to the great aggravation
of the poor who had “neither breakfast nor
dinner”? Of course McGarry didn’t expect
to be believed, but the obvious thing was
to ask him for the names and addresses of
the people who were reduced to one meal per
day, and promise them relief if they were
genuine, and say that anybody who knew of
such people and didn’t get down to plain facts
about them was a deplorable citizen. Instead
Holman said lie would have inquiry made.
The inquiry will cost somebody’s time and
some stationery and perhaps stamps, and
the information, if any, will only evoke the
remark : “Fooled again !”
The spectacle of finance going up a ladder
was witnessed lately in the N. S. Wales
Assembly. Simon Hickey urged that the
savings bank system of lending to people
who wanted to build houses should be
extended. Fitzpatrick (Assistant-Treasurer)
said that £300,000 of the depositors’ money
was now authorised to be advanced each
year, and he was prepared to make it
£500,000, and to increase the maximum
loan from £5OO to £7OO. Bagnall wanted
£1.01)0,000 advanced each year, and that the
bank should lend the depositors’ money up to
90 per cent, of the paper value of the
security. Also it should “take up second
mortgages.” McGirr proposed that where
“workers” were concerned the State should
take the whole risk and have no margin of
security at all. Someone might have gone
one better and proposed that the worker
should have £llOO lent him on a £lOOO house,
but nobody thought of that.
Bananaland is sending two M’s.L.A.
to the Front—. James (Labor) and Elphin-
stone (National). As these are the only
two at present volunteering for active
service, the Assembly might do something to
honor their patriotism. Winston Churchill
suggested, in the case of British M’s.P. at
the Front, a name-plate should be placed on
the back of the seats they held in the
House. If this had been done, nearly half
the seats in the Commons would have been
so labelled. In the Australian Parliaments
the plumbers employed to screw on honor-
plates would have died of the tired feeling.
The Hun Reichstag will probably pass an-
other resolution in favor of peace without
annexations or indemnities. It did that a
while ago, explaining in an aside that as it
had already made peace with Russia and
Roumania it would of course keep all the
lands and indemnities it had got for them,
but it was prepared to give up Belgium and
Northern France and pay nothing for dam-
ages. and take back all its lost colonies.
Then affairs looked up, and the Reichstag
passed a resolution to the effect
that it was only lying on the
previous occasion. Now affairs
are looking down again, and it
will probably announce that it
was lying the second time when
it said it way lying the first
time. In his enormous con-
tempt for people who lack kul-
fur the Hun can’t realise that
everybody takes it for granted
now that he is lying, and no-
body cares much whether he is or not. Eveu
if he swore he was dead people would rather
drown him for a week or so to make sure.
Treasurer Gardiner, of W. Australia -
The situation is full of worries. The States will
have to finance themselves during the remainder of
the war. The Federal Treasurer does not think we
will be able to get any loan money after 1918.
The principal worry is that W.A. had got
into the habit of regarding Cohen as a per-
manent institution and living on the pro-
ceeds of the delusion. Like the other States.
W. A. built up an ornate superstructure of legis-
lation and administration on the crazy foun-
dation of the frequently cadged million and
having developed the great natural resources
by heavily mortgaging these commodities.
An end had to come to the beautiful scheme
some time or other, and whatever may be
the value of the p.n. at the moment the loan
fiends can make up their minds that the
chance of resuming the old jag by pawning
the Australian kid some more are not a
thousand to one.
“H.M.” : British Labor is wondering where
it is, how it got thei’e, and where it is going
next. Something of the same sort is hap-
pening to it as occurred in Australia when
democracy awoke to the fact that the Labor
party organisation gave it -the means of
political expression,
and, rushing in, took
charge of the machinery.
As in Australia, the
stalwarts of the party
in England are puzzled
and suspicious. At a re-
cent conference an at-
tempt was made to get
a list of the names of
the 300-odd candidates
selected by the executive
for the next elections,
whenever they occur.
One delegate who
snorted that he was
particular of the com-
pany he kept, suggested
that Lord Haldane was
on the list. Stranger
things may happen. The
political organisations of
women, seeing in Labor
a champion of their
ideals, have wedded
themselves to the party,
and even conservative
people and organisations
that have an honest
patriotism which sees
the necessity of making
Britain a better, health-
ier and fiscally-saner
country are looking
hopefully at Labor. Pa-
pers of aristocratic tone
now speak seriously and
with approval of the
party as a factor in
national life: it has
ardent press support
from quarters that
would have seemed im-
possible a few years
back. The dulness of
the British Labor
party's past is over and it has a very potent
future before it,
“0.L.” : McPherson, Vic. Treasurer, is an
economical soul, to judge by his frequent re-
marks on State finance ; but in some matters
he seems to take a narrow view as to the
real meaning of economy. For instance, he
doesn’t mind paying out a few pounds each
year to supplement the handsome sum
voluntarily given by private citizens for the
treatment of patients at the Children's Hos-
pital. Yet these moneys only till the main
purpose of attempting to cure. Prevention
is quite another matter, as the research-
committee of the hospital has found, having
been refused State aid in its attempts to
discover the origin of infantile paralysis.
Merritt, M.L.C., brought the matter up in
the Council, the other day, and showed that

The statement made the other day by
Treasurer Theodore about the Bananaland
cashbox is such a very unilluminating effort
that it might be mistaken for an experiment
in dullness. The financial year ended up
with a gap of £409,452 between revenue and
expenditure. During the current year Theo-
dore expects to get. increased revenue
amounting to £627,181 and to spend £210,347
more than before. Keeping strictly to this
basis he looks forward to having a credit of
£7382 at the end of next June and still have
tlu hiatus represented by £409,452 on the
premises. Under retrospective clauses of
new taxation bills £334,000 is hoped for
from land and income taxes. If this hope
is realised the idea is to apply the proceeds
to liquidating last year’s debt. If it is not
—well, there will be nothing for it. but the
reliable old p.n. The impression given by
contemplation of Theodore's budget is of a
citizen scraping along from day to day in
hope that the greengrocer may forget to call
round for a settlement. Of course as long
as they can go on spending a couple of bor-
rowed millions a year and helping themselves
from the boom,- Theodore and co. will be
able to continue talking about “buoyancy”
and all the rest of it, but they are building
on the same foundation as every other State
Government in the land.
WORRY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Perth. —In the Legislative Assembly on Wednes-
day, the Colonial Treasurer, Mr. Gardiner, in intro-
ducing a Supply Bill for £1,451,000, which, he
said, would suffice to the end of September, stated
that the financial position was full of worries.
—Press item.
The job of making four-and-six
Perform the work of five-and-nine
Is now the pith of politics
And was since suns began to shine.
The surplus comes, the surplus goes
When days are fine and talk is bright;
Small figures set in lengthy rows
To boom and squander-lust invite.
Cash is forever in a hurry ;
The situation’s full of worry.
Our obligations must be met,
The thing we pawned we must redeem;
The court of fate is grimly set,
Our creditors for interest scream.
Cash wanted here, cash wanted there;
Ai*l still the figures swell and mount,
But never comes the chance to square
With one good cheque the whole account.
Time, that is gold, is in a hurry,
The situation’s full of worry.
We have had dreams of clean finance
With nothing left upon the slate.
The system never gives a chance
To any statesman to be great.
New debts for old is still the play;
And fast and fast the figures run.
THE DIFFERENCE.
N AVVY: “ 'Ow much did yer pay me last
year? I want ter make out me income
tax.”
PAYMASTER: “Tiro hundred and fifty
pounds.”
NAVVY: “I never earned as much as that.”
PAYMASTER: “I know you didn't; hut that’s
what ire paid you.”
The shadow of a reckoning day
Looks blackest in the brightest sun.
Get cash ! We need it in a hurry !
The situation’s full of worry.
Well, thank the stars that rise and set
For chance and change and all the rest.
The difficulties must be met—
Some other crowd will do their be t.
We can get out. That saving clause
Helps us to tread the well-worn way.
We wink a cheerful eye because
We know the public has to pay.
Yes, we can sidestep in a lihrry
And leave the other chap to worry.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMON WEALTH.

BARNES LINSEED OIL
“Scott T.”: “Jobber” (B. 22/8/US) makes
me hilarious. He says that the way Billy Doyle
piloted his team of five in a coach from Bourke
(N.S.W.) to Wanaaring, “through the scrub on an
almost invisible track was a revelation to us.”
I had some experience with the sportive William,
Once when I was on the box-seat with him, just
after we left North Bourke, he remarked, “You
can drive,” and handed me 175 pairs of reins—-at
least there seemed to be that many. I lacked the
courage to tell him that I couldn’t drive a perambu-
lator, and hooked on. He immediately composed
himself for sleep, and did sleep. After eight miles
those reins weighed 7 tons 3'cwt. So I woke William
and told him to drive the condemned horses himself.
“Oh, that’s all right,” said he, and he tied them (the
reins) to the side of the coach —and went to sleep
again, remarking first, “You couldn’t pull those
blanks off the track if you tried to.”
“The Bagman”: Mount Elephant, in the
Western District of Victoria, was a white elephant
to its squatter owners until the Railway Department
bought a lot of Jumbo’s stone innards for ballast.
On one occasion the heap could have been purchased
for about a boh. Smoke was seen issuing from the
top (it’s an old volcano, of course), and Derrinallum,
the ancient village hanging on to its base, thought
it was going to be blotted out like Pompeii. But as
nothing happened for a while, a venturesome spirit
risked his life and discovered that a couple of sun-
downers (to whom the Mount is known as “The Light-
house,” as they always keep it in sight when circling
round the district) had selected it as a quiet spot
to camp. Derrinallum then unharnessed the horses
and resumed housekeeping.
“Pingin” : I nominate George Walters, of
Tamworth (N.S.W.), as the champion long-distance
Australian job-holder. Here is his record: Collar-
making at Denning Bros., saddlers, for 51 years; has
swept the Methodist Church out weekly for 50 years;
been in choir 50 years and in town band 40 years.
He is 70, and looks good for another 25 years.
“Rags”: Where does the Australian bit-
tern hang out these days? Years ago when the Reed-
beds (S.A.) were covered in reeds and flags the
birds were frequently seen. From a distance they
look like sharp painted stakes, owing to their
peculiar habit of sitting motionless on the ground
with bill pointed straight upwards, their dry-wood
color plumage adding to the illusion, but they are
never seen now that the thick growth has dis-
appeared. At nesting-time they send forth, noises
which are the limit in bird calls, being more like
Hie bellow of a bull than anything else.
* * * *-
“X. or M.”: The story of Holiepa, tbe
Maori chief buried at Maria Island (“D.F.,”
B. 25/7/’18), goes back to the Maori rising
of ’4O. Trouble arose near -Wellington from the eter-
nal land question, and one of the tribes lifted arms
against the pakeha. Rangitoto, who afterwards, as
“William King,” headed the Taranaki rising, was
friendly' at the time and lent a hand to settle tlie
squabble. Five of the two militant patriots captured
by him were sent to Hobart, with a request from
Governor Gray that they should be put to hard labor
as a corrective. Latrobe, the Tas. Lt.-Governor, refused
to do this or to herd them with criminal prisoners.
He sent them to Maria Island to be treated as poli-
tical exiles, whilst ho appealed to the Colonial Office

on their behalf. To the homesick Maoris, however,
the fact of their exile was more than they could bear.
They wandered about desolately or sat, gazing out
across Hie water and pining for their own land. The
Rev. G. Clarke says he thinks that either two or

in the little wind-swept cemetery, is the only one
of whom any record remains.

“Camelero”: .Tack Dempsey, champion
buckjump rider, who is doing his bit somewhere;
near the Suez Canal, does not like camels. Still, the
average Billjim in the Camel Corps secured more
control over the animal than the Gyppos or Soudan-
ese. During the first month of the Corps existence
the camel had Billjim well bluffed, but later on
the animal became more afraid of Bill than the
Turks are. I’ve seen a mad camel chase half a dozen
fear-stricken Gyppos for a mile, yet Billjim, with
half a date-palm, would face the brute and bluff ii
in a qpuple of minutes. And he could ride a longer
distance on the beast than the average Gyppo. Take
the long 15-hour ride from El Arish to llafa; the
day’s fighting and the return ride the following
night without any sleep. The Gyppos gave in and
had to be conveyed back in ambulances, whilst Bill-
jim (those that were left of him) finished smiling.
In. all camel races that I witnessed the Australian
jockey defeated the Gyppo every time. At Mersa
Matruli, during the Senussi rising, one of our lot
and a Soudanese rider started out on camels on a
hurried dasli for an inland post with despatches.
The pace was furious, and Bill arrived six hours
in front. Our men extend much of the same ldndrtess
to a camel that they do to a horse, and rarely use
a rhino whip on its hide, as the Gyppos do. The
animal appreciates the kindness. We once captured
a fine big camel from the Senussi, and judging from
the sores and markings on its skin it must have
received an.overdose of cruelty. For a time we could
do nothing with it; but it learned what kindness
was and it took notice of the extra feeds of dhurra
that were issued to it, and particularly it noticed
the absence of the rhino whip and stick. When it
was blown to pieces with an aerial bomb at llafa
it was one of the best-tempered mounts that we
had in our company.
* * * *
“Sarto”: Can any Abo. find a use for the
leather-head? If so, 1 can give him the address of
millions of the partly-feathered raucous-voiced fiends.
They are bee-eaters, honey-eaters and fruit-destroyers.
In the vineyard they slash the grapes about until tbe
bunches are left bleeding to death, but nothing, not
even a starving cat, will eat them. They have a
special taste for the flower of the grevillea tree, and
I’ve seen them in the towns in hordes on the street
trees. They are black and grey, about the size of a
pigeon, and the head is quite bald and of a leathery
appearance —hence their name.,
“Spade” : Here’s something cheaper, safer
and just as efficacious as cyanide and sulphuric acid
for Indian wax-scale: 2-5 lb. common soda, loz, caustic-
soda, four gallons of hot water. Stir well and spray
it on the offensive. Fumes from this mixture will
riot injure plant or human life.

GOD’S ANSWER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bannister, who lived for gain,
Counting love and mateship weak,
Bannister of Coolah Creek
Once, and once alone, ’tis said,
Bent his knees and bowed his head,
Praying God to send him rain.
Sheep and cattle were to him
Pounds and pence in wool and hide —
That, and nothing more beside ;
Gain, and gain alone, he sought—
Bought and sold, and sold and bought—
Bannister, the shrewd and grim!
Drought might slay ids neighbor’s sheep-
Leive his friends with stricken lands,
Starving stock and empty' hands,
Driving them to ruin’s brink—
Not by so much as a wink
Did it cause him loss of sleep.
Loving neither man nor maid,
Man and maid no pity showed
When the Drought, red-handed, strode
Through the land, and spared him not;
Then it was, by all forgot,
Bannister knelt down and prayed.
Hands entrcatingly out-thrown,
Jle,ad and shoulders bowed with care,.
Bannister sent up his prayer;
Did that prayer include a friend?
Nay; hut selfish to the end
For himself he prayed alone.
Scarce a stone’s-throw from his door,
Coolah Creek, dry-bedded, lay—
Day' and night, and night and day
Staring skyward, stark and dumb;
In its single river-gum
Sang tbe shepherd-bird no more.
Praying long and low, there ran
Through his mind a vision sweet—
Waters singing at his feet;
And his words a torrent poured ;
“Open Thou Thy floodgates, Lord,
Lest I be a ruined man!”
THE WARRIOR’S SPADE.
“I’m, thankful that there’s no tear in these
parts.”
“So am I. This ground round here would be
terrible hard diggin’.”
Then he rose and sought his bed,
Sighing as he sank to sleep,
While, Without, his famished sheep
In the darkness moaned their woe,
And his cattle, lowing low,
Sagged with droop of eye and head.
Dreams were his with splendor lit,
Golden dreams of days to be;
While his prayer limped Jeadenly
Through cold spaces, drear and lone,
Till, at last, it reached God’s throne,
And God, bending, answered it.
Hot and still the darkness was,
Hushed and hot the midnight air,
Drought and death were everywhere,
Thirst and hunger, pain and grief:
Stirless hung the wilted leaf,
Motionless the brittle, grass.
Mercy', pity seemed aloof,
God remote and cold to man,
When the whisper rose and ran,
■ And the sleeper woke to hear,
Low at first, then loud and clear,
Raindrops drumming on his roof.
Little things began to stir,
Little voices filled the night—
Whispers, murmuring* of delight,
Till the torrent drowned them all
In the thunder of its fa 11....
God had answered Bannister!
Thinking thus his troubles o’er,
Pleased with God, he slept again;
Bannister, unloved of men,
Loved of God most surely seemed:
Coolah Creek, awakened, streamed
Scarce a stone’s-throw from his door!
Dreaming dreams of gain anew,
Bannister reposed at ease,
Hearing but sweet melodies,
Free of loss and free of pain:
Gorged and swollen by the rain,
Coolah Creek a giant grew.
Foam upon its torrent swept,
Leaf and limb went down its flood ;
Like a beast athirst for blood
Through the dark it ramped and raged;
Like a lion long encaged,
Free at last, it roared and leapt.
Then it burst its banks and broke
Bar and barrier in its path
Shouting, foaming in its wrath....
Dreaming dreams of golden gain.
Hides and tallow, wool and grain,
Bannister too late awoke.
Never more to speak and stir,
Never more to strive and hoard—
Self, and self alone, his lord—
Veins no more with life aglow,
Body washing to and fro-
God had answered Bannister!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

FOSTER’S LAGER
“L.”: “Pick’s” argument (B. 25/7/’IS),
favoring the display of crimson drapery under the
jaws of horses as a discourager of the bot-fly, does
not bear out the experience of vets, and the more
observant of bush-folk. The fly will deposit its eggs
on the neck, breast, shoulders, forearms and even the
tail of a horse when operations under the chin-
whiskers are interfered with. The fly does not sting,
and it is only its querulous buzzing, resembling W.
M. Hughes settling the metal question, that causes
the consternation of the most staid prad. A horse
taken from a locality where the bot-fly is unknown
to an area where it abounds will exhibit absolute
terror when he first hears the drone of the winged
nuisance, even though the insect may be yards away.
The rag Under the neck certaihly prevents any
depositing of eggs in that particular spot, from which
they might possibly drop into the feed and be subse-
quently taken into the stomach ; but as they hatch in
two or three days, and as the larvae are vivacious and
begin to crawl from the operculum to the skin on
the other parts mentioned, the itching so caused
impels the horse to lick and bite the parts that want
scratching, and in this way they gain the mouth. As
one fly deposits hundreds of eggs the neddy gets a
considerable number at each lick. After they have
passed into the interior, the larvae attach themselves
by means of little booklets arranged about their head,
and cause a small circular sore or depression in the
mucous membrane. I have seen horses hidebound
and emaciated from the acute affection of what a
subsequent post-mortem disclosed to be the larvae
of the bot-fly, and 1 once lost a good old stock pony
from peritonitis that was undoubtedly caused by the
perforation of . the membranes by the pests, although
the animal whs in normal health an hour before
death. Kerosene on the points of attack will keep
them at bay, and an occasional drench shifts the
larvae from his entrenchments and keeps the horse
well.
“Yannatherrg.”: Father O’Gracious was
making his periodical buggy pilgrimage between
Bogglewallah and Eulalia when his horse took fright
at a motor and bolted. Bill Redsoil, the groom,
promptly forgot the language restrictions and blas-
phemed and steered with great success for quite
two miles. The turnout finally hit a closed road
gate when doing even time. His Reverence spoilt
two anthills in his descent, and came to hear
Bill’s lurid address to his incompetent guardian
angel. “William! Stop that cursing! Why, it’s
praying we both ought to be.’’ “Praying be damned !”
snarled Bill, and, wildly contemplating the dis-
appearing wreckage in the dust haze, he added,
“Are you any blanky good at coo-ecing?”
* * * *
“Fitzbattleaxe”: Has any Abo. had the
good luck to witness a platypus fight? In a big
waterhole at Burke’s Crossing, on the Shoalhaven
River (N.S.W.), a fine specimen of the duck-billed
tribe had taken up his abode. During the recent
heavy rains the river became flooded, and another
big plat., probably on a tour of exploration, found
his way into the same hole. The original occupier
resented the intrusion, and dealt out stoush to the
prowler. The battle took place on the surface, there
being no diving. The water was lashed to foam,
and the slaps of the combatants’ bodies could be
heard a hundred yards away. Eor a quarter of an
hour the conflict raged, neither party showing any
weariness. The water and foam then had a bloody
tinge, showing to what effect the fighters had used
the terrible spurs on the hind legs. At last one of
the scrappers must have got a vital thrust,
for he dived. Presently he rose 50 yards away
near the bank and made for the edge, leaving a
bloody trail allthe way. Meanwhile the victor
cruised around where the vanquished had dived, look-
ing for him. Presently he got on the scent, and,
following the streak of blood relentlessly, located
the dying gladiator under the ti-trees. With Ilun-
nish savagery he hurled himself on to the defence-
less victim, and in a few seconds killed him. When
I overhauled the dead ’pus he was torn almost to
pieces. Have heard it said that a dog ripped by
the spur of a platypus can never get the wound to
heal. Any truth in this?

“Terry Hy Hie”: Railway navvying
doesn’t kill off the worker in his youth. Rolled up
for the final pay at Casino (N.S.AV.) last week,
Thomas Cosgrove, aged 82, who twirled a “banjo”
on the Sydney-Parramatta railway in 1854, and who,
for the best part of half a century afterwards, pur-
sued the same occupation all over the Common-
wealth. The old warrior reckoned that Australian
railways would be all right if they hadn’t had a
lunatic asylum to fix the gauges.

“Lagani-Namo”: One of the old brigade
used to blow into Port- Moresby about twice a year,
and have a blow-out on liquors. The beak used to view
the case leniently when it came before him, and send
the culprit to the gaol to sleep himself sober. On one
occasion the wrong-doer’s pal Bobbie saw hirn about
to be led to quod by the native court-orderlies, and
rushed up to the magistrate to protest. “I say,
Mister, strike me pink, y’r not goin’ to let
niggers put their hands on a white man, are
yer?’ “Mr. I fine you 10s. for contempt of
Court. “But strike me blue, this’s over
the - odds.” “Mr. , you’re fined two
pounds additional for contempt.” “By
7 > your W orship, ’ave a bit of *—— common-
sense about yer.” “Mr. you’re fined £5 for
contempt of Court.” Said Bobbie, turning to his
befuddled mate, “Hey! ’Ow much —— r money ’ave
y’ got?” “You cannot talk to the prisoner like
that, Mr. ,” interrupted the man on top. “I’ll
have to fine you again.” “But, strike me
sideways, I only arst him ’ow much ——- money
’e’s got so’s I’ll know how far I can go!” They’d
have lumbered Bobbie if he hadn’t leaped out of the
door and flld.
* * * *
“D.F.”: According to some of the early
settlers in W.A., the favorite tipple of the wild
natives was made by steeping the flowers of the
banksia or honeysuckle in water. Black brother-
dug a hole in the ground, lined it with ti-tree bark,
filled it with water, and put the flowers in to soak.
When the brew was ready they sat round it, each
with a small bunch of leaves, and dipped and
sucked till it was gone. The still more primi-
tive Tasmanian blackfellow is alleged to have tapped
the cider-gum for its juice and made a drink out of
it in much the same way. Old-timers talk of seeing
the “cider-holes,” carefully covered up with stones,
on the plateau of the lake country.
“Damper Bill’’: So someone wants to in-
troduce vultures to this importation-afflicted country.
Seven years’ veldt experience in South Africa taught
me a little of the ways of these birds. The vulture
shepherds every sick or wounded animal, and, as soon
as it is down, goes for its eyes, just the same as the
crow. In the sheep districts of the Free State and
the Cape it has long been outlawed for its
destruction of ewes and lambs. There the sheep-
farmer npstands before daybreak at lambing-time,
and, not even waiting for early coffee, takes his rifle
an d ; patrols his flocks. What a prospect for our
pastoral industry to face, though the race of early-
rising sharpshooters it would develop in time might
help to win the war. In the Transvaal tire protection
that vultures had enjoyed was removed about 12
months after peace was declared in the land, as the
evidence of such experts as Ur. Theiler convicted
them of spreading redwater. Of all the birds in
South Africa, with the exception of the ostrich, there
is only one that I would entertain the thought of
importing. That is the secretary-bird, and I would
not even have him unless a clean bill of health and
good conduct was supplied with him by duly-
accredited stock-disease specialists.

“Oscar ’: Australia’s most beautiful trail-
ing flower 'is Sturt’s desert .pea. The bloom is very
showy -bright scarlet and black, and about three
inches long. It flourishes in the West. AH my
efforts to grow this good Australian in the coastal
district of N. S. Wales have failed. I have tried
the seeds in sandy soil and light loam, and have
resorted to soaking them in hot water before plant-
ing, but without success. It will not transplant.
SPRING FIRES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gold torches blazed in the blue above,
The bush was lantern-lit for love,
And all the sweets of earth burned there
In kiss and kiss of the maddening air,
For the wattle wind was blowing.
My Love came lightly running down
The road that leads away from town;
Round dreaming eyes, her hair’s bright floss
Like little flames was all a-toss,
For the wattle wind was blowing.
And my heart was all September fire
As I sought her gaze with dumb desire,
And her smile was kind, but “No!” said she,
“I want no mate to walk with me
When the wattle wind is blowing.
“Those gracious boughs cry down the breeze
A tryst with the forest ecstasies,
And the thrushes, hid in their golden foam,
Flute to my lonely soul, ‘Come home!’
When the wattle wind is blowing.”
victoria.

had married an exceedingly nice girl in spite
of the opposition of her parents, but that her
father had at last relented and taken him
into partnership, and the baby was the image
of its mother, and a bound volume of the
Gazette had been sent them as a souvenir.
With a few human touches of this sort, and
a serial and a literary page our sodden con-
temporary might look up. It needs a more
capable advertising canvasser among other
things, and an up-to-date Government would
enact that every trades-unionist must buy a
copy just as he is made to buy a copy.of the
local Labor paper.
Leaving the most serious matter to the
last, I hold that a royal commission should
be appointed to find out whether our dubious
contemporary is, or is not, in the pay of
Germany. It may not be actually sending
rifles to the Sinn Fein crowd in Ireland or
furnishing the enemy with information about
Foch’s reserves. It may not, but its failure
to condemn the Hun atrocities in any way
looks like tacit approval. If this attitude
is maintained it might be wise to intern
our dubious contemporary.
JAMES EDMOND.
The Inky Way
“Y.Z.” : Upstairs on the literary floor of
the Age office the other day during my tem-
porary presence, two very fine naval officers
appeared, asking to see Lachlan Mackinnon.
They seemed a little suspicious when lie was
not forthcoming, but presently took the
Age’s word for it and went off to seek the
Argus big gun where he really belongs, up
tlie street. In response to subsequent in-
quiries I was told that, the two. officers were
from the Intelligence Department.
“Bill Bowyang” : So Seiior Devila, the able
editor of La' Pbenza, the leading daily pub-
lished in Buenos Ayres, has gone west.
Eight years ago I turned my back on the.
pearling and trepang grounds of N.Q., and
worked my way on a Iloulder meat boat to
the Argentines. When I reached Buenos
Ayres I called at the office of La Prenza.
Editor Devila had a long conversation with
me and displayed much interest in. Aus-
tralia. Ilis paper had the largest circula-
tion and was the most powerful organ, not
only in the Argentine, but in all South
America. At the present time I believe that
Buenos Ayres boasts of two dozen dailies,
of which La It a/.on is the leading evening
paper with a circulation of 150,000. It is
printed in Spanish, as are most of the others.
A leading London bookseller says that the
character and personnel of his customers
have completely changed. The old-time book-
lovers and buyers have disappeared and their
places have been taken by crowds of well-
dressed munition-factory girls and Govern-
ment Department flappers. They are so
flush of money that they think nothing of
spending five or six shillings on new novels
every week. This particular publisher cannot
get sufficient supplies to satisfy their demands
for popular fiction. The buyers specialise
on stories about girls who come from the
country and meet with sundry adventures in
London. The writer who can turn out with
mechanical regularity the sort of tale that s
clamored for is sure of a handsome income.
Soldiers are also good buyers. Jack London
is a particular favorite of theirs.
A Satchel of Books
It is probably within the margin of fact to say
that 50 per cent, of small traders keep no books ot
account, and that half the remainder muddle along
with a collection of figures that are incomprehensible
to themselves and everybody else. It is certainly
true that carrying on business without a systematised
record of the incidental arithmetic is equivalent to
inviting ruin and a guarantee against going ahead.
These are strong arguments in favor of distrusting
memory and jottings on scraps of paper. The neces-
sity for compiling income-tax returns is another
argument. In order to bring an understanding of
simple book-keeping within the grasp of a reason-
ably intelligent citizen cumbered with a business,
Ernest Morris, a Sydney accountant, lias published a
24-page pamphlet, setting out in simple terms how
to keep track of the £ s. d. and identify profit and
loss. Smith and Lane are the printers.
No. 48 of Science Progress, a quarterly review of
scientific thought, work and affairs, is a solemn docu-
ment of over 700 serious pages. With the spread of
the scientific spirit one can assume the possibility of
such a publication taking native root in. Australia.
On the student it has no doubt a refreshing
influence. Yet science is not always dull.
There is an essay in this number on Meat
Production, in which a university investig'ator
discusses the phenomena associated with animal
growth of obesity which any reasonably intelligent
Australian grazier might digest with interest and
profit. “Fattening” by rule of thumb would appear
to be a. very wasteful process,, and the common ideas
about the pig are apparently open to grave suspicion.
Jail from Within, by. Vance Marshall, arrested in
Sydney Domain and sentenced for using words “apt
to- cause disaffection to his Majesty the King,” gives
the writer’s experiences at Long Bay and Goulliurn.
It is an unlovely picture. If there is even a reason-
able measure of truth in the story the gaol system
is a. mighty long way worse than quite a lot of
people suspect it of being. But Marshall heavily
discounts his statements of fact by such commentary
as this: “Of all the prisoners to-day serving sentences
in the gaols of Australia I am absolutely convinced
that at least 30 per cent, are not guilty of the crimes
alleged against , them. They are there simply because
the police.*.. .chose them as their victims.” There is
no evidence to support this sweeping allegation. It
is not one that anybody knowing anything of prisons
or what happens in Australian courts would give any
support to. The number of innocent men sent to
gaol can at the most be expressed only in decimals.
By tony s on Service, by Hector Dinning (Constable
and Go., London, 65.), is a collection of sketches,
written mostly for newspapers and here brought
together. Recovery of such work is always a danger-
ous experiment. Journalism has an interest of its
own, but it seldom deserves to live beyond the day.
Dinning’s book has all the defects that go with haste.
Yet it is in the main so good as to create a hope
that he may find the time later on to write more
consecutively and less discursively. For he can
write and is the proprietor of eyes that see. His
chapters about Egypt and provincial life in France
are close to being A 1 anywhere.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Dale”: Dave Milne, an early shipbuilder,
now in hospital in S. Aus., has samples of
his work chugging up and down the Murray
riverways. He did not go in for imported
experts and industrial troubles, and there-
fore cannot properly be classed as a really
efficient shipbuilder, but he got through a
surprising amount of work with a staff which
rarely ran to more than a dozen men and a
workshop and a slip at Morgan (S.A.). He
was employed by King and Co., then the big
shipping men on the Murray. In 1907 he
launched the passenger and cargo steamer
Ruby after working with seven men on her
for five months. The design was his own,
and the result is graceful and comfortable.
Her length is 128 ft., beam 18ft., and depth
Oft. lOin. Three decks, electric-light, and
an IS-h.p. engine to send the paddles round
are part of the equipment. Another job was
the barge Emerald (150 tons). A visiting
expert who saw her lying at Morgan classi-
fied her as “a masterpiece.” Her length is
131 ft., and she draws Oft. lOin. She was on
the slips for seven months, and for the
greater part of the time only three men
toiled at her. Other barges were the Ukee
(300 tons) and Moorabein (350 tons),
all composite wood and iron craft.
Quite a few other boats also left the slips,
but those will do as samples. The men had
other little jobs to attend to in between
times. On Friday morning the Gem came
in with 35ft. knocked out of her side and
paddle-bbx by a belligerent tree. As good as
new and fully loaded, she left Morgan again
on Saturday night. About seven years ago
Milne moved down to Goolwa on Lake Alex-
andrina and started in business for himself.
He continued his debates upon the shipbuild-
ing problem by turning out the trading
steamer Renmark, the big Carowe barge
(700 tons) and the Loxton barge (120
tons). He also made a mosquito fleet of
motor-boats. The Gem, still the largest
passenger boat on the rivers, was built at
Moarna (N.S.W.) back in ’7O. She has
since been practically re-built and lengthened
by 40ft., making a total of 133 ft. in all. Will
Westegaard was responsible for the original
craft, and the other work was carried out by
W. Gordon and Sons at Goolwa. There are
about 50 steamers of all makes and sizes on
the rivers, and practically all are locally
built. There is not the work in them which
is required for ocean-going vessels, but any-
one who has seen one of them hauled across
a bank in low water, with one end of the
hawser made fast to a tree and the other
end on the winch, will know that they’ve got
to be able to stand something.
“Curlew”: Red tape is still at it! Con-
siderable quantities of wheat having been
damaged and become unfit for human food,
and poultrymen and pig-feeders being unable
to obtain pollard and bran for feeding pur-
poses. a wise Wheat Board decided to make
available supplies of its damaged article,
some of it. by the way, most awful rubbish.
Now, all that is necessary is to make appli-
cation to the Wheat Board and enclose
cheque —for a truck-load. If a man wants
a bag or 10 bags he must buy a truckful or
go without. As a consequence, the small
feeder has to get his supplies off merchants
who are charging ss. a bushel and making
extortionate profits.
“Counter-hand”: The rise in calico hits
the working-man’s family mighty hard. For
instance: in August, 1910, it was retailed
at 4s. 3d. per dozen yards; in August. 1917,
it climbed to ss. lid. .Tnnnary> 1918> saw
it leap to Ss. lid. (50 per cent, in six
months. The present quote is 13s. 9d.
dozen (another jump of over 50 per cent, in
six months') ! Wholesale houses are quoting
it at 16s. dozen for next season, and there’s
no guarantee of supplies even at that price.
An enforced return from all holders of calico,
with the indent prices and present charges,
would let loose the most heartrending howl
of anguish that the parasitic middleman has
ever unloaded in Australia.
“Anti-Bunny’* ; “W." appears to he look-
ing for a scrap (B. S/S/MS). so I’m at
him. He said ; “Once more Australia’s AYin-
the-War Government comes down on the
unfortunate rabbiter. After removing for a
brief space the embargo on the free and
unrestricted sale of skins, the product is
requisitioned at about half the market price.”
He forgets to add that the object of the
“requisitioning” is to enable supplies of
certain needful articles to be kept up to
our boys at the Front. I’ll admit that the
trapper works six times as hard as some of
the city toilers (and by that I’m not admit-
ting very much), but I will not admit that
he does much good in the way of
checking a dangerous pest. “W.” ought
to go and look at some of the country
that is being trapped out now, and then go
and look at the same country in a few
months’ time, and he will find that trapping
is only an ephemeral check, or, rather, a
thinning out just sufficient to reduce bunny
to the right proportions to breed up faster
than ever, and thus perpetuate the scourge.
As for the rabbiter doing much good to the
Backldocks by spending his money there, “W.”
is equally wide of the target. Ask the Out-
back townspeople, the storekeeper, the baker,
butcher or saddler, which he considers the
best mark —the rabbit-trapper or the cocky,
and I’m right out on the end of a wire
fence if he doesn't plump for the latter.
“OX.” : Oman, Vie. Minister for Agricul-
ture, is always ready to turn over an
apparently good idea to sec if it is as smooth
underneath as it is on top. One of the mat-
ters now being considered by him is the
application of the creamery system to the
beet-sugar industry. Maffra, the present
headquarters of the beet-business, is the driest
spot in Gippsland, yet the State outfit there
has turned the corner and is now producing
a lot of sugar and paying its way. Under
the proposed plan, small extraction-plants
would be built in the likeliest parts oh the
country, local farmers being encouraged to
put in a few acres each, and thus supply
themselves with a bit of handy cash during
the slack part of the year, as well as getting
a lot of extra feed for their cows, pro-
vided by the beet offal. It is a rosy scheme,
of course, and if Oman is thereby able to
make 21b. of sugar grow where none grew
before, doing, it profitably for all parties,
be will have good reason to crow about the
matter —particularly as it hits taken nearly
a dozen Ministers to make a success of the
Maffra scheme.

“8.T.” : I went in to buy a ne\v nib for
my fountain-pen the other day, and, was in-
formed that these nibs had gone rip to 6s.
As I can buy a very decent fountain-pen.
gold nib and all, for 65,, I protested with
some warmth. “But there isn’t two grains
of gold in them, and they were only half a
crown,” I said. “Ah, yes,” replied the bland
shophand, “but that was some time ago.
I suppose you’ve heard of the increase of
freight since then?'’

“Hawkesbury”: Why do we import
tobacco, and send millions out of the_ coun-
try, when we might grow the weed in our
back yards? Because of the better flavor.
What is the cause of this flavor? Consult
alleged tobacco experts or text-books and
they are dumb. Yet the scientist has the
answer, and anyone may prove it. Natural
tobacco, grown and cured in a proper way.
has of itself little or no flavor; the flavor
arises, when it does come, from a micro-
organism, as in the case of cheese and wine.
These ferments have evolved in particular
districts, and give a distinctive flavor to
their products. Also, these ferments can
often be transported, and used to give their
flavor in distant countries. Before knowing
that this was an ascertained fact, the writer
discovered it accidentally. Putting some
cured and flavorless local leaf away in a
box for many months, I forgot all about it:
when found it had tbe flavor of a Havana
cigar. Search showed a broken cigar of
that kind in the box. It follows that in
the varied Australian climates, we can grow
any character of tobacco-leaf we like, and
by inoculation when it is cured and spread
loose on a loft floor, give it the desired
flavor. Probably after the floor, or loft, is
once infected no further inoculation would
be required. This ferment for flavor is very
slow, one, two or three years, and quite dis-
tinct from the quick-curing fermentation.
Those who have kept a box of cigars for a
long time may have noticed how, sometimes,
the flavor has greatly improved; this is
owing to the continued action of the ferment.
“Midnight Oil” : It seems easy enough to
run up a bill for motor hire. A recent I ic-
torian insolvent, whose debts were stated at
thousands while his assets were nil or there-
abouts, owed hundreds for being motored here
and there. At Sydney Central Police Court
last week a constant lady petroller pleaded
guilty to procuring three motor-cars by means
of false representation, and asked throusM
her lawyer to be sent to a convent, lhe
magistrate, after looking through a long list
of previous convictions, decided t.iat lie
wouldn’t trouble the convent. It was shown
that the excursionist only had to tell a
motor garage that she was a squatters
daughter, and she was wheeled 1000 miles
with 90 hours’ waiting. Her bill was a
trifle of £ll2 17s. 6d.

BY FORCE OF NATURAL LAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were four men from Stamper Town,
Four ragged men who thirsted.
Our efforts on the long way down
To get the wherewithal to drown
Our fever had been worsted.
A quarry in our fire we lighted,
And tales of beer each man recited.
Oh, hot the nighti was! Far we sat
From embers dim, condoling,
And every liar with his hat
Was fanning briskly, when, biff! bat! —
Came some strange object rolling.
It stopped by Sam. I snatched the quicker.
By Gott! It was a keg of liquor.
A little paunchy keg of rum
Had down the incline bounded,
And none knew whence the thing had come.
We lingered not, but sampled some,
And cries of praise resounded.
It but remained for Bill to mention
A Providential intervention.
I learned next day that Binge that night,
By Johns beset for stilling
Had rolled the kegs in aimless flight—
No miracle had brought one right
To four so fond and willing.
Resistless law impelled the action.
It’s called capillary attraction!
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
“Bill Bowyang”: I'd advise McNorth
who wants 1:o know if something can-
not be done with the dugongs and
turtles of the Bananaland coast, to drop the
idea of making a living out of them. Fifteen
years back I tried to place dugong meat on
the Townsville market. At the start the
tiesh was taken for bacon, and business was
so good that I had visions of retiring at an
early age with a big banking account. Then
someone whispered around town that the
article was not pig, but plain dugong, and
there was a horrible slump in the market.
Others, such as “Yorkie” of Banfield’s
“Confessions of a Beachcomber, - ’ Ned
Flaherty and Jimmy Spooner tried to per-
suade Bananaland to cultivate a' taste for the
sea pig, but there was nothing doing. All
attempts to make cash out of turtles fared
the same way. Merinda (N.Q.) meatwmrks
once purchased several large turtles and sent
them in a frozen state to the Big Smoke, but
it did not encourage orders for further sup-
plies. One man tried canning the creatures,
but after struggling for two years he put
up the shutters.

Queensland State Ironworks.
APPLICATIONS are invited from persons
qualified to fill the position of
GENERAL MANAGER of the above Works
about to be established in the State of
Queensland.
The salary of the position will be
per annum', with travelling expenses on the
same conditions as provided for positions of
similar salary in the Public Service.
The successful applicant will be required
to take control of the organisation and estab-
lishment of Ironworks, coke plant and all
subsidiary works connected with the proposed
undertaking. Applications must reach the
undersigned not later than 30th September,
1918

All perishable goods in these
hampers are put up in airtight
tins. So it is impossible for the
contents to open up in any other
than a perfect condition. They are
then securely packed in specially-
made boxes, stitched in calico, and
clearly addressed.

Melbourne iias been the Paradise of the
understudy of late. The sicknesses of Flor-
ence Young and Phil Smith were other
people’s opportunities at Her Majesty’s.
Then Cyril Mackay acquired laryngitis, and
was out of the cast of “De Luxe Annie” for
the last week of its run at the King’s, thus
making room for young John Fernside to
give a brisk, effective performance of the
“crook” who treats gentle Annie as a part-
ner in blackmail only. All that Fernside
lacked in the part was the breezy suggestion
of suppressed savagery which gave melo-
dramatic color to Mackay’s interpretation.
Two little sketches that had been Fernside’s
jobs were filled in by Charles Bertal.
Reading London notices of “Follow the
Girl,” the show in which Dot Brunton
made her bow in the U.S.A., one
ceases to be sur-
prised that Dorothy
failed to set Uncle
Sam’s towns on
fire. Anent the
London production,
the critic of
Vanity Fair
(Dorothy Parker)
observes: “I don’t
like to say that I
have seen worse
shows and more of
them than any
other living woman
—I do hate to be
boastful. I merely
wish to remark that
I saw ‘Let’s Go’
and ‘Follow the
Girl’ on two suc-
cessive nights.. . .
Onlv the fact that
“Gran’dad Rudd,” at
Sydney Palace.
Bert Bailey as
Gran’dad.
I saw ‘The Love Mill’ last month prevents
me from hailing ‘Follow the Girl’ as the
world’s worst show.”
“Clianco”: News that Stella Power is
booked to enter double harness at the end
of the year is rousing the old theme: Is
marriage the making or marring of an
artist? The veteran Patti, lively and flourish-
ing in her seventies with No. 3 of her reper-
toire of husbands, and Calve, the greatest
of Carmens, as happy as a schoolgirl, tied
to her adored Gasparri. are prominent liv-
ing examples on the affirmative side. On
the other hand two of the happiest matured
mortals and finest artists who have visited
these parts in recent years—tenor Dufault
and pianist Berwick —are bachelors. Of the
great creative music-makers, Bach, Wagner
in his second venture, Schumann and Grieg'
were ideally mated. Bachelors Beethoven,
Chopin, Schubert and Brahms provide many
heartrending biographical pages of loneliness
and neglect.
From London :
Dan Godfrey has been celebrating his 25 years’
condin torship of the municipal concerts at Bourne-
month (Eng.). The town gave him £5OO in war
bonds, Stanford and Edward German attended to do
him honor, Hubert Parry and Alexander Mackenzie
sent letters of admiration. Encouragement of Eng-
lish music has always been Godfrey’s strong suit.
German (who is thoroughly British in spite of his
name) lias had the honor of a special Queen’s Hall
concert. Manager Alfred Butt has added another
American success to “Very Good Eddie” and “Fair
and Warmer” (*doing well respectively at The Palace
and St. James’s), in “Going Up,” a musical play
made out of Montgomery’s novel “The Aviator.”
“Libretto by Otto Harback and music by Ilirsch”
does not sound well, but presumably they are good
Americans. Robert Evett, managing Daly’s for
the estate of the late George Edwardes, mentions that
“The Maid of the Mountains,” now at about 600
performances, has taken more money than even “The
Merry Widow,” Edwardes’s own best-seller.
Biggs left Australia eleven months ago
to exploit his peculiar gifts in Los
Angeles, U.S.A. lie writes that he is doing
very well, having secured a permanent en-
gagement at a salary which in his simple,
clerking days in Melbourne would have sug-
gested the dream of avarice. Biggs is now
a picture actor, tie went to
Los Angeles with the deliberate
intention of acting for the
movies. He never acted in his
life before, he had neither
theatrical inclinations no r
dramatic gifts of any kind, and
his attention was turned to the
films only by a casual tip from
an expert in a Swanston-street
bar. The expert said: “See,
here, Egbert, if I had a face so
like the, seven plagues I’d go to
America and make the picture-
managers feed me.” Apparently
Biggs’s face was the kind of
face there was a special demand
for in movie circles, the first
man who saw it in Los Angeles
jumped at it. It was originally
one of the ten ugliest faces on
earth, and its peculiar attributes
has been amplified by a kick
from a horse. Biggs writes that
his face value is 80 dollars a weejt.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : A Sydney news-
paper recently stated that while William
Creswick, tragedian, was playing King Lear
at the old Victoria Theatre (Sydney), some-
one threw at him from the gallery a bunch
of carrots, which fell at the feet of one of
the ladies. Creswick, so the paper said, took
the insult to himself and “raged” over it.
An incident of this sort did occur, but not
while Creswick was playing. A lady
in Lyster’s Opera Co. was unblush-
ingly familiar with an old Sydney solicitor,
and the lawyer’s son, himself afterwards a
solicitor and M.L.A., gave a lad half-a-crown
to propel the vegetables at the disturber of
the peace. The incident ended in Lyster
“firing” the giddy lady.
“0.K.” : The dark seance drama that Mar-
garet Wycberly plays in (at Melb. Royal
just now) can at least be commended for
its ample exposure of how criminal sus-
picion can be cast on anybody by “spiritual-
istic”. conspirators. And in most seances the
audacious “medium” has one or more con-
spirators in the circle. “The Thir-
teenth Chair” is instructive to the thought-
less, inasmuch as the detective who ulti-
mately arrives at wrong conclusions starts
on the correct assumption that the seance
was arranged by a person in league with
the medium. He takes the presence of * a
conspirator for granted. A better and more
subtle melodrama might have been con-
structed on the idea that the person who
questions the bogus medium re the murder
of Spencer Lee was tlie actual murderer of
that late citizen. The assassination of the
questioner in the dark is not essential to a
drama founded on the killing of Lee. It
creates what is practically an impossibility.
If the 13 sitters were all holding hands,
each linked left and right with the person
in the next chair, liow did one of them stick
a knife into lan Maclaren without somebody
knowing that the chain of hands had been
broken? And why doesn’t the detective work
on this question? The writer never sat in
a circle in the dark without being able to
swear that he was all the time holding a
hand on either side of him. They were small,
warm, sympathetic hands as a rule, and the
charm of the seance was in their squeezes.
Had either of them been violently withdrawn
in order to commit a murder the writer
would have protested that lie was being
robbed'-of - half; his pleasure in spiritualism.
Moreover, the person who committed the
murder might have sworn that the writer’s
hand was forcibly withdrawn for homicidal
purposes. Prudence dictates that you must
hang on to hands at a seance, or risk the
consequences of letting go. If all hold
on nobody can commit a murder except the
medium.
Oscar Asche and Walter Ilackett, who
w r rote “The Invisible Foe,” are collaborating
in the libretto of a musical show for Daly’s.
The plot (or words to that effect) is laid in
Egypt. Its present title —which is subject
to alteration —is “The Desert Maid.”
“Melb.”: This week the amateur Vic-
torian Opera Company puts on “Black
Cloaks,” in the Palace. This opera was
first performed in Melb. at the Opera
House in ’B3, and revived in ’SO, at the same
theatre. The original cast included Verui,
Knight Aston (Mrs. Gordon Baillie’s hus-
band), Appleby and Annette Ivanova. In
the revival there appeared Loredan, Edwin
Kelly, Appleby and Annette Ivanova. The
first conductor was Galli and the second was
Simonson, Appleby was producer on both
occasions, with Stoyle for his assistant.
Stoyle is producer for the V.O.C.

Baritone Derek Hudson’s end came while
he was a prisoner at I’aderburn. lie was a

machine was brought down he sustained

“S.” : The once eminent Australian actor
told me how he was converted to spiritual-
ism, and why he broke away. “I was parted
a good deal from Helen, me little wife, sir,”
he said. “The exactions of me art drew me
from her side, and a pestilent neighbor in-
duced her to go to some infernal medium, a
Madame Cleo, a stained hag with a purple
wig, who told Helen everything on earth
about me, sir. I give you me honorable word
there was nothing I had ever done, or
thought, or said that that despicable witch
did not know. Be gad, sir, it gave a man the
creeps. Me little wife was bringing things
back at me that were calculated to blight me
life, sir. I loved that woman, and here she
was being induced to consider me as one of
the most despicable rascals unhung,-backed
by all the available facts. ; She was talking
of divorce. I saw meself the victim of a
blighted life, and, by .Tove. I was so flabber-
gasted with the truths advanced that I could
offer no workable defence, and what’s more,
I was getting superstitious. I began to see
there was more in this confounded spiritual-
istic business than I had imagined possible.
I saw that if it was to be introduced into
the: home in this way there was am end to
happy family life. I became painfully super-
stitious.'and one Sunday night sneaked away
to Madame Cleo’s seance. I came back
cured, and, thank God. Helen came back
cured too. -There should be a law against
former partners of one’s joys and sorrows
using their first-hand information _ against
one. Madame Cleo was my first wife!”

Gertrude Elliott wife of George Robert-
son, who is going to tour Australia, has
taken the St. James’ theatre, London, for a
season by arrangement with the widow of
George Alexander, its late lessee. Gertrude
intends to produce there “The Eyes of
Youth,” a piece that has been a phenomenal
success at the Maxine theatre, New York,
which is run by her sister, Maxine Elliott.
“King’s Lynn”: The writer of a para-
graph in. “Poverty Point” (B. 15/8/T8) is
in error regarding the preaching of C. H.
Spurgeon at tin* Surrey Theatre. That
eminent divine commenced his London career
(being then about 20 years of age) as a
“boy preacher” at the Park-street Chapel
near Southwark Bridge, on the Surrey side
of the water, but the place was altogether
too small for the crowds who wished to hear
him, and he shifted to the large hall in the
Surrey Gardens, known as the “Surrey
Gardens Music Hall,” a very large building
in Walworth. Here he drew enormous
crowds. One Sunday a false alarm of fire
was raised, and in the ensuing stampede
seven women were killed. Just as two or
three years after there was a crush on the
gallery stairs of the Victoria Theatre (also
on the Surrey side of the water) that
resulted in the death of 15 boys and the
injuring of about 30 other people.
UNDYING HOPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Y knew him when a noble lad
Of eighteen years or so.
A heartfelt yearning then he had
i Upon tlie stage to go.
With simple, force he would insist
He was an elocutionist
Whose extraordinary gift
The modern theatre might uplift.
At twenty-five he lusted still
As Hamlet to appear,
And hoped the managerial will
To change within the year.
At thirty lie could stir the age
If giv’n a footing- on tlie stage.
Producers, dubbing him a bore
On fame and fortune slammed the door.
I met him yesterday by chance. -
He now is fifty-three.
His hair is grey,-and dull liis glance,
His boots winked up at me.
* lie scattered bills to advertise
“East Lynne,” a local enterprise.
“This is,” he said, “my humble part.
• It isn’t much, but it’s a start!”

A list of 50 N. S. Wales companies, with
their profits for four war years, was pub-
lished in a recent issue of Sydney Tei.-
egraph. Some concerns were excluded be-
cause they had called up so much new capital
that the earnings couldn’t be compared, or
they had amalgamated with other companies,
or bought up other companies, or sold out to
other companies. The net result was that
50 joint-stock businesses made idoi,4b4
more profit in the year to June, 1918, than
in the year to June, 1915. It was made up
in this way: Trading and finance and mi-
porting concerns did well; manutactuiing
concerns, which work to a great extent
abroad with cheap non-union colored labor
did well; while concerns that manufacture
or produce solely in Australia with white
Australian labor did badly. Thus J w o bhMKs
showed increases of £67,65 1 and £33,-69
respectively. A big shipping co., doing a
great foreign business and having a multi-
tude of side-lines of industry outside the
Commonwealth, improved by £119,94*. xne
great sugar co., with its prosperous 1 ljuin
and other business, was £89,571 better oft
on the year’s work in 1918 than in 1914. A
notable wool-trading firm improved to the
extent of £74,585. These five represented
£376.029 of extra profit, and many other
trading and importing firms showed very
solid but less sensational increases. On the
other side were manufacturing companies
employing Australian union labor. Iheie
weren’t very many of them on the list, and 11
of them showed £214,644 less profit in 1918
than in 1915. Some at least of the reasons
for this may be summarised: —
Militant unionism cultivates hatred of every
emplover as a robber capitalist. It concentrates its
hatred on the capitalist within reach because he is
within reach.
In its crude way it never seems to consider that
if it went in for strenuous Protection and for local
manufactures in place of imports it would have three
times as many capitalists within reach. To avoid
facing this big constructive problem (constructive
problems are not in its line), it consents that Aus-
tralia should be, in proportion to its population, the
biggest importer on earth, and thus one of the big-
gest employers of foreign cheap labor on earth ; for
the importer, with all the world to choose fiom,
naturally buys in the cheapest market.
Besides, the importer and the financier are hard to
hit. No Trades Hall has ever engineered a big strike
in the importing warehouses or the banks. And if
there ivas too much strike the big bank, having no
engines and boilers to remove, might simply shift
its head office to another State or to England and
look for foreign business; and if its shareholders
were hit by extra-heavy taxation here they
would find that their shares would fetch a
higher price in I.ondon or some other place,
and would sell out to foreign holders. The
importer also has no engines and boilers and looms
and ponderous steam-cranes and steam-hammers and
vast smelting appliances to anchor him down to one-
spot, and he is not one-tenth so dependent on skilled
labor as the manufacturer. Also, he deals in perhaps
200 lines where the average manufacturer makes one
or two, it being inconvenient to make hats and ships
and flour in the same mill. So it would take 100 or
200 strikes spread over the world to cut the roots of
the importer’s business. The head of his business the
distributing warehouse —is to some extent shiftable.
But the manufacturer who is wholly Australian can
be squeezed at any time, because he is wholly
dependent on union labor and his apparatus is so
heavy that it would cost more than it is worth to
take it down and remove it to Fiji, and re-erect it
among the niggers, \\ hen the grasping capitalist is
mentioned he (or the mine-owner) is always • the
individual meant, because it isn’t worth meaning the
other fellow. He gets most of the hatred that was
really due to the importer, because the only way to
hate the importer is to raise the tariff, and some-
how the Labor party would rather be poisoned than
“ do that. He might be a better manufacturer if he
worked on a bigger scale with better machinery, but
he can’t work on a very big scale when room must
be made for so much foreign cheap-labor produce,
and he feels too much like a temporary intruder to
take very heavy risks on new plant.
There is no evidence that the Melbourne
engineers who refused a 10 p.c. rise and
demanded 20 p.c. because of the increased
cost of living, were not justified, though it
is a dismal business to be drifting back to
the crude strike method at this late day.
But the Australian manufacturer in the
aggregate is getting nothing—in fact he
gets less than nothing—to make up for his
increased cost of living, however good a
wicket the importer and financier may be on.
And it is noteworthy that while 20 p.c.
may be dumped on to the charges of the
employer of purely Australian labor after a
fortnight’s shindy or a day’s disturbance, it
takes years to get 5 p.c. added to those of
the man who sells the same kind of goods,
but has them made by any kind of labor that
isn’t Australian. The one proceeding is as
sudden and informal as a counter-lunch.
The other is protracted and solemn and
reverent, like the building of a cathedral.
“Peter Pan”: According to American
technical journals there has been a phenome-
nal development in German banking during
the war. Deposits in the eight leading banks
rose from £250.000.000 in 1914 to
£500.000.000 in 1916. and to £820,000,000
in 1917. Of course, the main causes of this
expansion of credits are the same in Aus-
tralasia as in Germany—an inflated cui-
rency, and the creation of enormous amounts
of new capital by Government bond issues.
In the absence of foreign exchange transac-
tions. through diminished Stock Exchange
business, owing to the falling-off in trading
bills and the general constriction of com-
pany formation, banking profits must have
been seriously affected, yet it is asserted that
the eight leading German banks are all pay-
ing higher dividends than in 1914. Just as
in Britain, so in Germany, amalgamation to
reduce working costs and secure greater
soliditv is in favor amongst financiers,
v * * *
Capital has been found to open up some
gold-bearing formations in the Cosmo New-
berry hills, beyond Laverton (W.A.). Years
ago Carr Boyd declared that gold w-as to be
seen everywhere in this locality, but several

We Have not increased
the price of a solitary diamond in our
enormous stock, notwithstanding that
Diamonds have risen fully 33 per cent,
lately.
When war broke out all jewels slumped
and we bought enormously, and so had
two years’ normal supply.
AVe could, of course, increase our prices
and profits by war conditions, but we
prefer to increase our customers rather
than profiteer. It will pay us better
eventually.

prices are far below those of London.
