More Miles for Yotxr Money.
The Outlook.
“0.K.” : Boiled down to dry fact, the result
of the brilliant strategical operations of the
three Allies on the West Front since the
middle of July means the recovery of one-
tliird of the territory captured by the Huns
since the last- week in March. It may also
mean an impairment of German “moral”
greater than what the British and French
suffered when they were retreating more
hastily, but it deals no death-blow to
the old military doctrine of the enemy
—that the one and only irresistible
manner of attack, on a large scale,
is the advance in massed formation.
Hun. "drives” accomplished three times as
much advance as the counter-attacks of the
Allies, at less than three times the expense.
That’S the dry fact. Military critics jnay
talk round about it, but they can’t alter it.
The rush in massed formation, where every-
body keeps everybody else from turning tail,
where it is a matter of “If we go forward
we die, if we go backward we die : better to
go forward and die,” is the only sure means
of pushing far ahead. It carried the Huns
to the Somme in August, 1914, and they
have remained thereabouts ever since. Our
babbling experts argued that the Huns were
beaten because they couldn’t push on till they
had captured at least a half of France and
the last remaining little bit of Belgium. The
Iluns boasted, and still boast, that they won
the war in three weeks because they were
already beyond the possibility of being driven
from conquered territory. Possibly the Hun
commanders have now had enough of trying
to end the war by further great attacks in
massed formation. It is certain that the
Allies will never adopt the system, for its
success depends on the intensity of discipline
in the massed forces, and the new American
troops are less slavishly disciplined than the
comparative veterans of “Kitchener's
Army,” who are less disciplined on an aver-
age than the French conscript army. The
massed method, the only method by which
either side could continuously rout the other,
is “off.” Whichever side may have the pre-
cious “initiative” that we hear so much
about it is unlikely to sacrifice hundreds of
thousands of men in irresistible advances,
i.e., advances which no amount of destruc-
tive artillery fire can stop. There seems to
be nothing left for the contending armies
but a policy of playing for safety as regards
ground fighting, whilst the rival air forces
are daring to do all the harm they can to
the enemy’s men, communications, munition
dumps and goods of every description. Only
by ruthless, systematic bombing can either
side force the other to throw in its alley.
Destruction of all the largest German muni-
tion factories of importance would inevitably
win the war, without even the assistance of
a steady drizzling rain of bombs on railways,
bridges, etc., within the Hun sphere of occu-
pation. A crushing superiority for the Allies
in air-fighting, if not already established, is
a positive certainty in the near future.
Wait till the Yanks get fairly going ! Its
possibilities have been only hinted at thus-
far. An unimaginative world has yet to be
staggered by the discovery of the fact that
no military strategical position on God’s
earth is as commanding as a dominant posi-
tion in the broad expanse of God’s air, above
the lines, and beyond the lines, and over the
cities, whence armies derive their means of
carrying on. One remaining German hope
there is (apart from the chances of food and
coal famine, and the intrigues of pacifism
and pro-Germanism) of averting the down-
fall of Hun ambition. The annihilation, or
even a considerable destruction, of the
British fleet by Germany’s warships would
do the trick by stopping supplies to the Allies
in France. If the Kaiser cannot win the
war by other means of exhaustion, he must
order out his fleet to face The Day, and do
or die. It seems his last chance of saving
his Hoiienzollern face. He has talked
so much about the necessity for a powerful
German navy, yet the war has lasted four
years and Germany is still waiting for evi-
dence of where, the usefulness of the
Kaiser’s Dreadnoughts comes in. The conflict
surely will include one desperate attempt by
the German navy to end the state of things
at sea whereby the Allies have the means to
continue their efforts ashore. The Channel
has remained an open thoroughfare to the
British, thanks to their navy. The Kaiser is
bound to challenge the all-important sea-
nower before he admits that Germany is
done. Public opinion will compel his navy
to justify the taxes that u«ed to be collected
from house-to-house on behalf of each new
battleship in turn.
The late engineer Catani’s biggest job
whilst in the employ of the Vic. Gov-
ernment was the draining of the Koo-
wee-rup swamp, undertaken about the time
of the boom-burst, the primary object being
to find work for the unemployed. The swamp
loomed largely in Binghi’s history, chiefly
because it was reputed to be the home of the
bunyip, and on that account black brother
gave it a wide berth. To the early settlers
around its margin it maintained its weird
reputation, and this wilderness of 75,000
acres of water and giant ti-tree was allowed
to remain undisturbed until Chaffey Bros.,
of Mildura fame, made the Government a
sporting offer of £7 per acre for it as it
lay for closer-settlement purposes. This
caused the Government to sit up and take
notice, and finally decide on a drainage
scheme. The recently-demised William
Bloomfield was the first contractor, but
whether the job was too big for him or the
price too small is unwritten history. Any-
how the undertaking was cancelled and it
became a State enterprise under the manage-
ment of Catani. That was back in the
’nineties, and the job is not finished yet;
but, all the same, the Koo-wee-rup is one of
the best-paying propositions the State has
to-day and a standing advertisement for
closer settlement.
Stated confidently in South Aus. Assembly
that during the present year from 7000 to
8000 seals .have been slaughtered on the
islands of that State’s coast, and that the
last lot of Skins fetched half a crown each.
The Government looked shocked, and re-
marked in a faint voice that it would inquire
into the matter.' : . "

After reading, will you please send this paper to
the Red Cross Book, Magazine and Newspaper Depot,
5 Wynyard Street, Sydney, or place it in one pf the
receptacles provided for the purpose. All copies thus
received are divided equally between the Red Cross
Society and the War Chest Fund, and are posted
weekly to our soldiers abroad.

Sydney, September 2. 1918.
My Dear Akini, —
Tiie French Mission will give local society
every opportunity to air its irregular verbs
at the reception in its honor on September
13. This is a Government House function.
-V garden party in the same premises on
October 1 will be a bigger gathering, and
sheaves of paste-board -will flutter through
the post in a week or two.
In brilliant sunshine, with the warmth of
a summer day, Governor-General Ferguson
inspected a fine body of men at Liverpool.
The Camp parade-ground is a fairly level
patch of well-ti ampod-down grass, with
undulating hillocks, and while infantry and
Light Horse (the nags were mostly of the
heavy gun-carriage species, lent for the
occasion) went through their evolutions, a
line of recruitecs on the horizon added effect
to a brisk picture of soldiering in a first-
rate A.I.F. camp. Unfortunately lack of
publicity was responsible for a very small
attendance. His Ex., mounted on a sedate
chestnut, expressed mild wonder, at this
aspect of the occasion. Captain Barton
ambled along , with the Fairguson.
Red Cross dames have offered their con-
dolences t 0( Mrs. Shepheard Laidley, one of
their original members, whose eldest son, a
captain in the A.F.A., was killed in France
during the recent pursuit of Fritz.
Pastoralist J. R. Robertson, of Togan-
main, Hay, lias put his affairs in order, set a
manager to look after his flocks, and enlisted
for active service. Mrs. Robertson, after a
long bout of ill-health, is, in company with
her child, going for a short trip to the
Pacific islands to recuperate. She’ll return
in time to see her husband off to the war.
That done, the Toganmain missus will use
up her spare time in Sydney driving wounded
soldiers from Randwiek around the suburbs
in a motor-car which the head of the family
is leaving her for the 'purpose. Meanwhile
convalescent Soldiers will be welcomed as
usual at the up-country homestead.
The Davidsons are settling down to city
life again after a visit to the Riverimi. The
Otway Falkiners’ homestead, Boonooke. was
the sub-regal camp during spell-oh. Boon-
ooke has been rebuilt on the site of the old
home. Very little is left of the first dwell-
ing. Its surrounding country is hard to
beat in any part of pastoral N. S. Wales. A
kangaroo hunt was put on for the people
who represent George V. The Davidsons
bought two horses in the Riverimi. Her
Ex. is a capable horsewoman and can talk
horse with the best of them.
There’s a rumor of the probable departure
for U.S. of W. 11. Wood. ex-M.L.A. and
Chief Secretary in pre-war days. It’s a
Government mission of some kind. William,
who married the charming Mrs. Young, a
widowed daughter of General French, for-
merly N. S. Wales State Commandant, pays
his own expenses. Lately the Woods have
been living quietly at Wentworth Falls.
Sergeant Maurice Pear.se. killed lately in
France, was the youngest son of A. W.
Pearse, editor of the Pastokai/ikts' Review.
Dad lately returned from Egypt and there-
abouts, where be was a commissioner for the
Australian Comforts Fund. Young Pearse
was an “old boy” of the Arrnidale School
(N.S.W.), and was a clerk in Birt and Co.’s
Sydney office when lie enlisted at 20 in 3935.
He was in time for several smoky months at
Gallipoli, and afterwards went with the :53rd
Battalion to France, where, he was wounded
at Pozieres.
The Sydney presswomen’s stall on Homes’
Day will be at Waters’ corner (King and
George streets). Mrs. Fotheringhaine is
rounding up helpers, and Mrs. (Ethel
Turner) Cnrlewis is pushing ahead with the
purple packet scheme. The p.p. “stunt” (as
the flying boys would say) is on hidden-
treasure lines. The promoters, after naming
the patriotic infant, found that they were lip
against a famine in purple paper. So the
name remains, but violet ribbons will be
substituted for the missing wrappers.
Miss Soubeiran, one of the founders of
the French-Australian League, is expected
home within the next week. She was a
toddler in Paris when the German army
entered the city after it had fallen in 1871.
An orphan of the Franco-Prussian war, it
was her strange lot to once more hear the
same enemy shelling her beloved native city
not so many weeks ago. Since then the
Allied army has been tickling the Hun’s
heels while he flees towards the Rhine. Over
the good news the League will presently
mingle its congratulations, with the French
Mission as its guests. The party is to be
held on September 32 at the Australia.
The French Mission will have as inter-
preter on its country tour in N. S. "Wales
Mr. Ramsay, of Hansard staff, an accom-
plished spokesman in the language of Foch.
The new French Consul-General, M. Cam-
pana, expects his wife to unpack her Sara-
toga in Sydney in about eight weeks. The
lady will give a Slip to the Consulate which
has been without a madame since the war.
The late Patrick O’Brien, during the last
year or two of his life, lent to the Red Cross,
on one condition, his beautiful water-side
home, Reinga, Woolwich, as a convalescent
home for soldiers. The condition was that
Mrs. A. J. Hare, head of the Lane Cove
V.A.D.. should be lion, matron. How well
this strenuous honorary worker justified
this faith in her only the nerve* and shell-
shock patients, who found Reinga a haven
of peace, can tell; but the executive of the
Red Cross and the military made certain
drastic changes. Mrs. Hare, partly as the
reaction of nearly four years devotion toiler
self-imposed duties, became seriously ill, and
when she was “no longer required.” the Lane
Cove V.A.I). took off their pinnies and
downed brooms. Benefactor O’Brien’s
daughter, Mrs. Coberoft, now the owner of
Reinga. kept to the spirit of her father’s
wish and will use the place herself. The
end of the upheaval is that Russell Lea,
near Abbotsford, has been purchased by the
military. The late' Russell Barton made his
home there for many years. Standing on a
green knoll above the sloping banks of the
Parramatta River, it is an ideal spot for
sick men. Nevertheless the village of
Woolwich is feeling very sore over the whole
business.
Killed in the big push, Major Norman L.
Dreyer, D. 5.0., tt partner in the well-known
firm of Sydney solicitors, Stuart, Thom and
Co. He was one of a dozen or so who quali-
fied for their A.I.F. stars through the local
4th Field Artillery Brigade, and first saw
service in the sands of Sinai, where he
earned his captaincy. Later he went on to
France as adjutant, and after about a year
took over the command of a battery which
worked over-time in the great 1917 advance.
Dreyer got his majority towards the end of
1917, and the D.S.O. followed. A fine sol-
dier and a good fellow, Major Dreyer is a
big loss to the Australian gunners. He was
an old Sydney High School boy, served his
articles with Piggott and Stinson, and was
for some time associate to Arbitration Court
Judge Edmunds.
The Lady of the
Lamp, whether she
serves in the battle
zone or tends the
wounded in military,
hospitals, is estab-
lishing a magnificent
record of devotion to
duty. And so the
British War Office
very properly has
recognised the splen-
did work done by the
women who are giv-
ing their helping-
hand to the fighting
men. Matron Mar-
garita Veenman, of
Randwiek Military
Hospital, is one who
has been decorated.
No one was more astonished than she was
when the news came from London. Her
assiduous care and love for Billjim, and her
self-abnegation as matron—and therefore
always on dutv when there is a rush of cot
cases —has long been noted. At the cere-
mony in Martin Place the other day the
Governor-General handed over the Royal
Red Cross decoration to the original of this
little picture by May Moore —a handsome
silver cross inset with red, enamel. In the
centre is George V.’s head in relief. Above
are the words Faith, Hope and Charity.
Matron Veenman belongs to N. S. Wales and
was trained in Newcastle Hospital, where
she became, sub-matron. She has been in
charge of Randwiek Hospital since July,
1915. controlling a staff of 87 trained nurses,
including 46 massage nurses. The Rand-
wiek haven for our badly wounded houses
about 700 soldiers at present, but the new
building operations will provide beds for
1000 men —a large family of sick for any
woman to mother.
The Millions of Meals Club’s dinner in
the Voluntary Workers’ Cafe on Saturday
evening brought forth a good array of ora-
tors to advertise Homes’ Day—homes for
soldiers being the V.W.s’ specialty. The best
evidence of the work being done came from
Sergeant Buckley, a slender returned man
who was almost “given up” by the doctors.
A few weeks ago I saw his cottage on a
loamy lower patch of French's Forest. There
are, first, a capable young wife, six acres
(mostly under cultivation), between three
and four hundred good fowls and their cava-
liers, a steam apparatus to keep an even
temperature in the long brooders’ house, a
garden full of flowers and a cow or two. All
these good things (the Capable GirL ex-
cepted) came through the Voluntary Work-
ers’ Association.
Italy’s Day, which is still moving on to a
finish in the country, will add about
£60,000 to the Red Cross funds of our Ally.
That sum is in sight; £50,000 is in hand and
will be the amount of a cheque which Consul-
General Eles, who comes to Sydney from
Melbourne headquarters, will be asked to
forward. The remainder will follow later.
To keep the petrol tank full and for other
exes, in connection with the convalescent
soldiers’ car at Rose Hall. Grace Stafford
organised a smart show in St. James’s Hall.
One little comedy was - a brisk skit on the
brand-new English V.A.D., on her native
soil, who gives up being a duchess to boil
eggs, and make the cocoa (of knife powder)
for soldiers home in Blighty.
During the past
two years a Sydney
contralto wi t h a
luscious richness in
her voice has com*' to
the fore. As a ballad
singer, notably in
songs of sentiment
with crooning re-
frains. Lilian Gibson
is rapidly becoming
one of our most pop-
ular concert artists.
She began her studies
in the Goulburn Con-
vent, where, some
years ago. Ella Cas-
per’s fresh young-
voice attracted the
notice of the nuns.
Later, the Sydney
girl put in a long course of study under
Andrew Black, a teacher with a gift for
bringing out the organ tones in a genuine
contralto. But the Harbor tow T n thinks most
of Lilian Gibson for her unselfish efforts to
help every patriotic cause. So far as her
professional warbles permit she is always
ready to give her voice to the service of
any fund that is connected with the man in
the firing-line. Few concerts to collect com-
forts for the Aussies are complete without
her song. The photo is by Judith Fletcher.
You can, if you like, give yotlr Rembrandt,
your Gainsborough, or even your little
guinea hat to Jack’s Day. Beard, Watson,
Ltd., notify the generous that they’ll store
anything from oil paintings to a china
candlestick till October. Then the commit-
tee in charge (the Alex. McCormick lady is
president) means to sell the lot by auction.
Mayor Joynton Smith took charge of the
Town Hall meeting held to consider the best
way to raise money for the Crown-street
Women’s Hospital. A new Nurses’ Home is
the main objective of the present struggle.
Judge Backhouse, a persistent barracker for
this Cinderella amongst sister charities, ex-
plained that about £BOOO is urgently needed.
Have you seen the new Spring capes?
They’re rather smaller than the historic
coat over which the famous Cor'sican,
Napoleon, folds his arms in the prints of
the retreat from Moscow.
The Sydney Day Nursery Association
which cares, so far as its means permit, for
babies whose mothers have to go out to
work, in accordance with our villainous
social system, checked its annual debit and
credit the other afternoon. Her Ex. presided.
1 nlies Noad, M.L.C., outlined some schemes
for the future whereby the hope of Australia
might get a fair deal.
Coining home on four months’ furlough
Flight-Commander T. E. Gorman, of Nan-
gunia, Berrigan. The N. S. Wales man has
been peppering Fritz for two years from
the Western' Front. He is going to wed
Frances Haines, a Berrigan girl, during his
brief holiday.
Mrs. Rosalind Brown, who taught the
young vocalist how to reach top notes for
years in this town, has been packing up for
a new home. Goulburn will be her address
in future.
On September 2S there’s to be a rally on
the Cecil Purser’s green lawns at Wahroonga
for the benefit of the Edith Cavell Home
fund. The sub-regal lady is lending her
cherubs to help the other juveniles. En-
trance charge, one shilling.
The _ shady walks in old Hyde Park are
again in danger. Temporary chief gardener
Cooper (on loan from Melbourne) leans
towards dahlias. He hasn’t explained how
these handsome decorative plants are to pro-
vide us on a blazing summer day with the
shade we have been accustomed to in the
park.
On September IS the New South Wales
Voluntary Aids will bid formal farewell to
Mrs. Chris Bennett, who has done such
excellent work as hon. co-director with the
military head. Tea at Rose Hall and the
gift of an album containing groups of the
various squads will be included in the pro-
gramme of the leave-taking.
Brisbane notes: —
The Y.M.C.A. held its annual meeting the other
day, and all the speakers, including Lady Goold-
Adams, urged it to keep on passing the hat for
the £25,000 required for its new building. But
surely Billjim, his missus and kids have a stronger
claim on any spare £25,000 which happens to be
available for bricks and mortar. Appeals of this
kind should be postponed until Mrs. Billjim has
her roof.
Killed in action, in France, Lieut. A. S. West, soil
of the Rev. A. E. West, who is now anchored at
Dulwich Hill. An old Brisbane Grammar boy, he
had been twice mentioned in despatches before his
call came.
. Barrister-aviator Tom Macleod is a major now.
He managed to get back to France at the end of
June, and is in charge of an aviation park some-
where.
Our city fathers are clawing the air over thd
question of mixed bathing in the Municipal Baths.
Two or three of the more progressive fail to see any
harm in it. but the old-timers, led by Mayor McMas-
ter, tremble at the thought. Anyhow, the city
solicitor says the Council has no power to pass a
by-law allowing mixed washing, and the governing
Act will have to be amended before the dreadful
proceedings can take place.
Nurse Mauritz and her sister looked in front
Sydney this week on their way to the Northern
Territory. They have been sent North by the
Presbyterian Inland Mission to. take charge of the
little bush hospital at Marauboy, 160 miles front
Darwin, which was established and equipped some
time ago by the Federal Government.
Gunner W. S. Jeffrey, son of the Jeffrey who
manages the Blair Athol Coal Mines, is back home
in Rockhampton, after 10 months in a German prison
camp at Dulmen, Westphalia. He was reported
killed in action, but German stretcher-bearers picked
him up in the Bulleeourt wire entanglements. lie
declares that had it not been for the Australian
Red Cross parcels he must have starved to death.
Cyril White, Government Botanist, is in New
Guinea indulging his pet science, and is staying with
Judge Murray. Young White is a grandson ol
J. Manson . Bailey, the wonderful old man who was
Government Botanist here for so many years.
Died unexpectedly, this week, Percy Weston, lec-
turer in electrical engineering at the Bananaland
University, lie was a Sydney Uni. graduate, and
had held his position here since March, 1914. A
tiny scatch on the note resulted in blood-poisoning.
His wife was Miss Ivy Shields, a. clever Bananaland
girl.
This week is the appointed time to pass
in any small odds and ends for the IVss-
women’s Purple Packets. David Jones, Ltd-
have a receptacle to drop them into till Mrs.
Cnrlewis carries them off.
Yours affectionately.

Melbourne, September 2,191 S.
My Dear Yakkie.—
Of Melbourne’s shows on Saturday night
the most exciting was at the Palace, where
“The Black Cloaks” was put on by the
Victorian Comic Opera amateurs. The opera
itself has the scintillating qualities of dough,
and; this combined with the fact that the per-
formers were seen, but indifferently heard,
proved too much for that portion of the audi-
ence that had not paid to see its mother in a
short skirt, or applaud its brother or best girl.
Since those on the stag ■ failed to provide
entertainment, the crowd supplied its own.
Half-way through the first act the gallery
began jeering; in the second, things got
worse, and the noise was not confined to the
“gods” ; while during the third, anyone stroll-
ing in casually would have thought that an
election meeting or a wool-sale w*s in pro-
gress. The final curtain descent* d to an
accompaniment of groans and insults, where-
upon the producer rashjy took the stage and
announced that the house had been “packed.”
Voices from the auditorium assured him
that he was mistaken. Anyhow, no producer
qf a misfire should ever produce himself.
Leila Doubleday gave her first concert on
.Saturday night in the Auditorium. The
management used the Continental trick of
suddenly plunging the body of the hall into
darkness, leaving only the platform brightly
lit like a theatre stage. Leila was in white
under an amber-shaded light. A black piano,
a scarlet strip of carpet and a potted orange-
tree completed the picture. Young Leila of
years ago appeared again as a self-possessed
young artiste in a gown built like a white
surplice. There was a lace surplice over
white satin and a white silk stole hanging
sheer from her shoulders at the back. Her
brown hair was coiled close and massed
thickly over her left eyebrow. She soon got
the big house’s sympathy, so that after every
item ushers advanced and delivered bouquets
at her feet. An adoring family party sat in
the stalls. The Ballieux were in full strength.
(Leila has been staying with one branch of
the clan in Toorak since her return.)
The front-view of Leila’s quaint white
satin frock, with its apology for a waist
tending towards her armpits, was less satis-
factory than the side-view when she had at
last nestled her fiddle under her chin. Viewed
from the rear, as she walked off, the dr,ess
was mainly a mere length of material. Leila
might have been garbed in something more
adapted to her healthy Australian style.
Leila’s style indicates indifference to pictur-
esque platform effect. And in her treatment
of floral tributes she has a lot to learn from
Dolores or Ada Reeve. The supply was
bounteous at Leila’s first concert. A small
cart-load of baskets of blossoms and bouquets
was handed up after her first recall. She
got away hastily with as many as she could
carry, but there was nothing of the artist in
her selection of what to take and what to
leave in the foreground. A later consign-
ment of tributes was awkwardly placed at
the side of the platform, and finally the
usher came along with a plain cardboard box
which he deposited in the centre foreground
as though to invite the lady’s attention next
time she took up her position. A casual
glance at this mysterious offering was all
that Leila deigned to give it.
Melba’s pet singing girls lately gave a con-
cert _ in the Richmond Town Ilall, where
Nellie sang when she was a small girl. Rich-
mond is also the birthplace of Stella Power,
who was the star of the other night’s pro-
gramme. Melba played some of the accom-
paniments after receiving Lady Stanley. Her
Ex. wore a string of diamonds round her
hair, brought her lorgnette and muffled her-
self in a sable cloak. Melba’s own magnifi-
cent wrap had gold and large pastel pink
roses embroidered on it. When she peeled
this off for the stage, she was in an ex-
pensively simple frock of white chiffon satin,
with a rope of pearls about her neck and
dangling emeralds set in diamonds. An
emerald clasp held some f”lds of the white
gown. When she finished the last accompani-
ment and rejoined Lady Stanley, the house
began to clamor for her to sing; but she had
to refuse. She has had influenza, and her
doctor has ordered a rest for her vocal
chords. However her admirers didn’t know
that, and kept up such a clamor that the
diva finally had to get up and leave the hall.
Bourguinon, the Belgian who went to
America in Melba’s train, played some con-
cert music. He lias put on weight. Stella
Power billowed on in sails of white satin.
Pc-ggy Center showed very slender ankles
from under a crisp frilled white net skirt and
butterfly frills crisping out from shoulder
and waist. Miss Murdoch, a budding con-
tralto, wore a demure white frock with
ginger hair, and sang with a slight Scotch
accent. The Mitchell clan was thick in the
hall. The pretty Conservatorium girls who
were not singing acted as ushers and pro-
gramme-sellers. These girls have to dress as
smartly as a regiment of soldiers under the
eye of Melba.
Melbourne lost one of its identities last
week. Thomas Welton Stanford, of East
Melbourne, passed away to spirit-land at B>-
and if he is ever again to join the engloomed
circle in his rooms at Russell-street his his-
toric form will have to be impalpable.
Sixty-one years ago, T.W.S. Cs«re to Mel-
bourne from America, and the agency for
Singer’s sewing-machines made him very
wealthy ere lie retired from business' to take
up Spiritualism. Perhaps he didnff die as
rich as he was said to be, but he certainly
was pretty “warm” in a financial way of
speaking. Many years ago Ins home was a
house of call for anyone who could print an
admiring notice of the Stanford collection of
oil-paintings, some of them good, and mostly
old-fashioned. As a collector he was not a
man of up-to-date ideas. One could guess as
much from his extraordinary attachment to
his patriarchal beard, which had been jet-
black like his hair for almost as long as the
oldest inhabitant could remember. Stan-
ford’s impossibly black trimmings in contrast
with his pale face, together with his notori-
ous passion for spiritualism and his 60 con-
secutive years in Melbourne, made him a
conspicuous figure, aud the curious were
never tired of asking why he never revisited
America, what time his millionaire brother,
the late Leland Stanford, was a famous
philanthropist. But o’d Tom always grew
restless under any mention of his visi ing the
land of his birth. , He was a childless
w’dower for the last half-century, or there-
abouts.
Melba drew the raffle for the Kitchener
Flag on Thursday afternoon at the Town
Hall. The bunting went to ticket 162,882,
owned by C. 11. Lear, of Brunswick, an old
sailor of between 60 and 70. He gladly takes
delivery of the 2000 guineas offered for the
flag by Edward Solomon, of Westralia. A
diamond ring was awarded to Mrs. Ryan for
selling the largest amount of tickets. She
generously gave the circlet back to be auc-
tioned “for the boys.”
Edward Solomon, who made possible the
success of the Kitchener Flag Art Un’on,
by offering to buy the prize for 2000 guineas,
makes a hobby of collecting flags sold or
raffled for patriotic purposes. Four years
ago he acquired for 66 guineas a Belgian
flag auctioned at the Oriental Hotel by
Daisy Jerome. Since then he has attended
practically ..every patriotic function in Mel-
bourne at which autographed bunting was
on sale, and has bought flags by deputy in
other capitals. All the Allies are repre-
sented in his collection.
This is Rem-
brandt’s view of
Bombardier Waller,
the one-armed sol-
dier-artist who has
been recently show-
ing some of bis
battle pictures in
Melbourne. Waller
is a Penshurst boy
who originally
whetted his appetite
for art by drawing
illustrations to
Tennyson and Wil-
liam Morris. Coming
to Melbourne, he
entered the National
Gallery and wrung
from Bernard Hall
and the late Fred
McCubbin the mysteries of color and line
and the art of combining the same with
the painter’s creative fancy. In 1915,
when he was pushing along to big achieve-
ments. the war intervened and threw him
into France, -where a high explosive caught
him at Builecourt and smashed his right
arm. Though he now works well with his
arduously-educated left, he has gained
another right hand in Christian Yandell, a
young painter whom he married before he
went to meet the Hun.
George Allan’s sister is acting as secretary,
and Mrs. Tallis, Mrs, Robins, Mrs. Willis
and Mrs. George Fairbairn are with
her on a committee, to help The Girls, Who
Stayed at Home. These entertainers have
raised over £7OOO for the soldiers by giving
town and country concerts. They have been
at it since the beginning of the war, and
their “props” and costumes w’ant renewing.
So the above-mentioned Melbourne hostesses
(the Girls have helped them often) are
giving a concert in the St. Kilda Town Hall
on Wednesday, September 11. Part of the
proceeds will be used to equip the patriotic
entertainers; the balance will provide and
endow some cots in Caulfield Military Hos-
pital.
Captain Geoff. Grice was deprived of his
well-won M.C. in this column a couple of
weeks ago. and credited instead with the
Order of the British Empire. The battle
decoration is hereby pinned on again.
Chilian Consul Barros, is just back from
a convalescent trip to his country.
The venerable Henry Gyles Turner
dropped into the Lyceum Club the other
evening and lectured on “A Day in Mel-
bourne in 1539.” He mentioned that the
smart-dressers of that time banded their hair
like Queen Victoria and wore cottage bonnets
and Paisley shawls. We wouldn’t do that
now—that is, unless it -was the fashion.
Dolly Nicholson, one of Melbourne’s top-
notch tennis players, has been visible on the
Rose Bay (Sydney) courts this week. She
is staying with Mrs. Neville Dangar.
The Bland Holts have packed up and gone
to Sorrento for a long spell.

James, lately from
France, presents
almost the ideal of
militarism in petti-
coats. She has the
f o l* c i b 1 y feminine
face of the master-
ful womanly man-
ner, which may be
partly due to her
baptismal name of
Britomarte. M r s.
James went to Eng-
land two years ago
to be as near as pos-
sible to two sons
then learning to fly,
and she soon became
an active organiser
of entertainments for
the Australians. The Brass-hats must have
had the little lady from Australia marked
as an exceptionally capable woman, other-
wise she wouldn’t have been allowed to
enlist for the Women’s Auxiliary Army
Corps on her own special terms. She was
appointed a Unit Administrator with march-
ing orders. She led her small force of 69
or .so girl soldiers aboard a boat at Folke-
stone, and debarked them at Boulogne.
Oddly enough the very first man who inter-
viewed her on W.A.A.C. business was a link
with home. She had to discuss the fixing-
up of her headquarters and he introduced
himself as- Captain Geach. “You don’t hap-
pen to know a Portia Geach, formerly of
Melbourne, and now of Sydney?” asked the
lady. “She’s my cousin.” replied the cap-
tain. After that the conference progressed
swimmingly.
Adelaide’s budget:—
Capt. C. E. Howell, whose M.C. was chronicled
last week, has added a Distinguished Flying Cross
to that decoration. He and his patrol encountered
nine enemy planes, finally accounted for seven of ,
them, and put an eighth out of control, Capt.
I-lowell being responsible for three of tile Huns. The
performance was staged at 18,000 feet. An M.C. has
also come to Lieut. Shaw, son of an ex-mayor of
this village.
Capt. Pollard, who has been A.D.C. to successive
local commandants for some time, has gone to Mel-
bourne and taken his smart wife.
The Returned Soldiers’ Association’s “indignation
meeting” was not regarded in official circles with
quite the lofty disdain that some people pretended.
The men have got one thing they demanded, i.e.,
representation on the board- of the S. Aus. Soldiers’
Fund. Mr. Blundell resigned his seat and nominated
the R.S.A.’s secret&ry as his successor, and the
Premier announced in the House that he (the see.)
will sit as a representative of the Association and
not of the Government.
An obstinate motor crunk cost William Morrow,
M.L.0., a double fracture of the wrist the qther
afternoon.
Private A. G. Crouch, one. of a well-known south-
eastern family has been killed in action. He enlisted
in Victoria and had. been only four months at the
front.
Lieut. Colin Duncan, who was put out of action by
Hun gas, has gone over to Melbourne to be initiated
into his duties as A.D.C. to the C.-G.
The Royal Red Cross (second class) has gone ,to
Matron Hancock, who has put in a long spell of
service as principal matron of the 4th Military
District and the No. 7 A.G.H.
The Brown dame’s appeal for male spinners of
wool for feminine knitters has’ had its result, and
the Tourist Bureau windows show photographs of the
spinners and some skeins of their handiwork. * One
of them is a south-eastern squatter who learnt his
job in two lessons and spins from wool grown on
his own premises.
The will of the late Dr. Bevan has been proved at
£7200 in this State. The money' goes to his widow
and family, with a legacy to his adopted daughter,
Mrs. Paul Joseph.
The Regimental Clubs gathered the next-of-kin of
the men at the front at the Town Hall on Thursday
evening to exchange news about their fighters.
The 9th and 11th Light Horse Regimental Club, at
its second annual meeting a few days previously,
elected as its president Mrs. Carew Reynell, whose
husband was the 9th’s colonel when he was killed on
Gallipoli.
The Mayoress has called the women’s patriotic
bodies together and urged them to do their bit
toward raising South Australia’s £3,500,000 slice
of the War Loan. The campaign is to be opened 1 \
a meeting in the Exhibition Building on September 10.
Major H. W. H. Seager, M.C., who was badly
wounded at Messines last autumn, has left to take
command of a transport.
Gladys Moncrieff, daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. R. G. Moncrieff, of Double Bay, Syd-
ney, is engaged to Ellis Davies, of Mel-
bourne. Gladys is Katinka in that popular
comic opera; fiance Ellis is chief engineer
for Rmvolt’s, “engaged on work of national
importance.”
Fernie Aitken, daughter of the Mayor of
Rutherglen, has promised to marry Lieut.
Percy Fullerton, a flying officer now busy
over the retreating Huns. Percy also
belongs to Rutherglen.
Alfred Weston-Pett is prouder than ever,
for the engagement has just been announced
of daughter Beatrice, who is still in her
teens, to Flight-Lieutenant Victor Holyman.
R.N.A.S., a Launceston boy who has just
returned after two years of Hun strafing.
Several theatricals have lately committed
matrimony or are threatened with the indis-
cretion. Among the most recent to hire a
parson are T. W. Lloyd and Maisie Carte,
who are both well known in Melbourne and
Sydney. Lloyd is the useful small-part man
who years ago thrilled all feminine hearts
with his white-faced version of .Tacky in
“Sunday.” Maisie Carte is a promising
Maoriland actress who exercises a literary
gift in' secret and has an artistic nature
generally.
Mavis MacMillan was a pale pink bride
last week when she married Western Dis-
trict stockman Edgar. Miss Langridge was
a picturesque bridesmaid in a Pauline pastel
filmy gown. The rejoicings were at Scott's
Hotel.
Ray Bennett and bridegroom Leslie Ivos-
minsky are away honeymooning. The
’groom is the sou of Austral Hat Mills Ivos-
minsky, and has a sheep-run at Cootamundra
(N.S.W.). The wedding was as quiet as
that of two very popular young people can
be. Bert Monasli, daughter of the General,
•and Rewa Marks (Moss Mark’s daughters)
helped the bride with her gloves and bouquet.
Madge Kosminsky, Leslie’s sister, was the
third bridesmaid. Bride. Ray was dressed
in a short Dresden-China sort of frock.
There was great consternation in a Sydney
theatre the other night when the weight and
Influence in the latter half of the show was
found to be missing from her place in the
line. A frantic hunt by the staff failed to
locate the absentee, so after a wait of some
minutes with the orchestra trying to drown
the impatience of the audience, the curtain
was rung up on a substitute hastily com-
mandeered. The runaway turned up some
time later and declared yawnfully that she
had come into town early, and having an
hour or so to spare before going on, she had
turned into a picture-theatre and had
dropped off to sleep ! Some of these Yankee
film dramas would send an insomnia victim
to sleep.

Statutory Notice to Next of Kin.
JDE JAMES HENRY BAKER, late of Brisbane, in
the State of Queensland, deceased, laborer.
Notice is hereby given that all persons claiming
to be next of kin of the abovenamed JAMES HENRY
BAKER, late of Brisbane, in the State of Queens-
land, deceased, who died on or about the second day
of August, 1917, A.n.j at Brisbane, in the said State,
are hereby required to forward proof of their rela-
tionship to The Public Curator, Brisbane, on or
before the first day of November, 1918. And that
after the expiration of the last-mentioned date The
Public Curator will proceed to distribute the assets
of the said deceased amongst the parties entitled
thereto, having- regard only to the claims of Which
he shall then have had notice.
Dated this twentv-fourth day of August, 1918.
R. J. S. BARNETT,
Official Solicitor to the Public Curator,
Brisbane.

SURE CATCH TACKLE
A determined raid on the Coq d’Or (Mel-
oourne) and King Offa (Caulfield) double
was made last week, with the result that
these two nags stand at a mighty short
price straight out, and there’s nothing much more
available on the combination. The unusual circum-
stance about this happening is that the pair are in
the same stable and both are imported horses. There
is the further coincidence that the same owners
supplied Lanins as a red-hot favorite lor last Mel-
bourne Cup, and what Lanius did is a pity to
remember. They also managed to just rnis3 the
Caulfield with Shadowland. Coq d’Or had a fair
vecord in England. Ilis best performance was in the
Column Produce Stakes at Newmarket, 1916, when
he ended up the mile in front of Gay Crusader, who
was giving him 111 b. In the same year he was
third in a mile and a quarter event anil unplaced
over a mile and a half. King Offa’s best to date is
a third in the Doona Trial at Caulfield a year ago.
Since betting on the Epsom started, odds-
merchants have kept Laddie Blue, a country nag
which invaded Randwiek in June and won a novice
mile in “37f,” on the safe side. Whether it will be
necessary to keep on doing this is doubtful. Any-
way Laddie Blue, starting in a mile at Maitland on
Saturday, with his Epsom weight aboard, only just
caught in the last stride a nag that wouldn’t be
given a chance in an Epsom under any weight, and
was giving him 101 b., finishing in “431.” In the
Epsom, Laddie Blue has Bst., and is Handicapped
to be a 41b. more'expeditious animal than Satin Bird,
winner of the race last year and winner of the
Exeter Handicap at Randwiek in June, when he ran
the mile with 9st. in “39.”

Not long ago there was only one horse seri-
ously spoken of for the A.J.C. Derby—Outlook, Cer-
tainly at the end of the two-year-old proceedings
the outlook seemed to be that if this money-spinning
juvenile went on in the way he should he had a
mortgage over the stake. But it is not the first
time that bright outlooks of that kind have later
become dull and sadly disappointing. At present the
Derby is just about as open as could have been
looked for under any circumstances. Almoner, winner
of last Breeders’ Plate at Randwiek, and Finmark,
one of Lowry’s that was speedy enough to win sortie
good races over yonder, are coming from Maoriland.
Tragic Star, from Victoria, Red Fox and Sweet
Lady, and not impossibly, Woorawa, are open to
suspicion of being hard to beat. Also there are others.
And against Outlook is the circumstance of being
one of a race that takes unkindly to going very far.
Anyway there is certain to be a better field than the
one Biplane made an unholy exhibition of.
Desert Gold landed in Sydney on Tuesday
front Maoriland. Interviewed subsequently in her
boudoir at Randwiek the lady said she was quite
well, thank you, and expected to move her legs with
as much rapidity as usual on an early occasion.
* * * *
The 60, 20, 20 scheme with the tote seems
to be peculiarly suitable for pony courses and the
sportsmen who patronise these establishments. At
Ascot (Sydney) on Saturday there was a record
turnover for courses in N. S. Wales other than
Randwiek —-£18,264. There were a few things inci-
dental. One was that not a single stake-winner got
off with £lOO. Most of them got less than £5O.
Another was that the Government appropriated
£1275 from the day’s sport through the piratical
7 per cent, raid on the machine, and the club £450
—tasty additions to the ticket tax and bookmakers’
fees. Twelve races were run for £720, divided
among 36 place-getters. Now, what sort of game
is that to play, and the State to give its blessing
to besides taking, one way and anothe'r, over £l5OO
from it? Meanwhile tlie attempt to “improve sport”
and “encourage the thoroughbred” by Government
annexation of double the sum appropriated by' the
prads seems an interesting experiment.

“M.” : It is quite true the totalisator can-
not lie, and that, given competent clerks, it is
practically imnossible to make a mistake in the
division. But in this particular instance the tote
did lie—hard. At a Maoriland race-meeting when
the machine opened for business on next event, a
punter walked up and asked at £1 window for 11
tickets on No. 16. The ticket man had a few
words with the punter, and with No. 16 in his
mind handed out 16 HI tickets to the investor, who
waived off with his scraps of paper. Almost
immediately the clerk discovered his mistake, and
reported the matter to the totalisator proprietor,
who decided to “wait and see.” No. 16 just
long- enough to win, and paid a div. of over £2O.
The next incident was when an irate punter
demanded to interview the proprietor and wanted
to know why, when he had asked for 11 £1 tickets,
he had been handed 16 10s. tickets. The tote
prop, had a brain-wave on the spot. “Where are
your tickets ?” he asked, and tr - 16 were promptly
produced. “These are pound tickets all right,” he
went on. “Here are your 11 pound tickets,” and
he handed them over to the surprised punter. The
final incident was when the tots proprietor himself
put in the odd £5 worth of tickets for his dividend
—that was after he had paid £5 into the neglectful
clerk s total —and coolly pocketed over £lOO. Only
one or two of the clerks knew what had happened,
and they knew their jobs. The rest of the punters,
therefore, who backed No. 16 lost £lOO out of their
total dividend. That tote man evidently had many
of the qualities required to get rich quickly.
“Spreader”: Fietition, who will stand
during the coming season at Boggrawoggra, has
such a mass of Eclipse at the top of his lines, that
no authority on pedigrees could* fail to note the
marked influence this circumstance must naturally
have on his offspring. Besides being entered on
the English stud-book, Fietition, is entitled,'owing
to his superior breeding, to have his name on the
stud-books of Germany, France, America, and Aus-
tralia. Though never raced he can hardly fail to
transmit the wonderful vitality inherited from his
ancestors as the approved running lines 1,2, 3,4, 5
are beautifully blended with the premier sire figures
3,8, 11, 12, 14 right through a pedigree that is
dotted with the names of the world’s greatest tuff
wonders. Though the inbreeding to Blackloek may
at first sight appear to overcrowd an otherwise
faultless table, with a blood that is not generally
approved of by modern stud-masters, a second glance
reveals that the counter influence of Whalebone and
Whisker appears at the right place (and requisite
distance) for a perfect set-off. The splendid strains
of the brothers Selim and Castrel in Miss Kid (dam
of Fietition) shew on top of Birdcatcher at almost
the same remove as the peerless Pochahontas blood
is marked in Bunkum (sire of Fietition). Thus
a return of the Birdcatcher-Stockwell blood in
a sound mare is about the only thing necessary to
assure getting a foal which at the yearling sales
would have the right mixture in his veins to take
rank with the pick of the lots on offer.

The old-timers’ run-out at the Rugby
Union carnival at Sydney on Saturday had a certain
novelty about it, but it was the kind of sideshow
which calls for the limelight about once a century.
The majority of the players were too old and too
tallowy. The excuse for the match was that it
would draw cash for patriotic funds; but the R.U.
has bid for gates long enough to know that, nine
times out of ten, nothing attracts a crowd except
something worth paying to see. Thp Union billed
three matches for the day. All it got to yawn at
them was about 3500 patrons.
“Post” : Though the finest effort in recent
years, A.. McPherson's (Western Suburbs) 50 goals
in N. S. Wales Rugby League this season is only
a good second to Dally Messenger’s great record in
1910. Messenger was then hooting at his best. He
scored 18 tries and 108 goals (270 points out of
518) during the season.
*.* * *
Young Jim McManamey (North Sydney),
who put some sparkle into N. S. Wales High School
football this season by scoring 14 tries anil a similar
tally of goals, is a son of the late Jim McManamey,
the old-time Rugby Union stalwart, and in his time
the finest umpire in Sydney.
* '* * *
W: G. Tasker, killed lately in France, was
a stout-hearted trier in peace and war. Wounded
at Gallipoli he returned home and, getting well, set
off towards Bei’lin again. At one time Tasker
played a brilliant eame as five-eighth for N. S. Wales
Rugby Union. He wore the blue jersey against
Maorilacd and Bananaland and tripped through
America with the team of 1912.
*• * * . *
Les Mclntosh, of Brookvale (N.S.W.),
gave a taste of his usefulness at the woodheap last
week. Jack Leahy (Tas.) had arrived to tackle
Mclntosh for the N. S. Wales 15-inch log champion-
ship ; but the local giant carried too much skill.
Mclntosh was also busy among other events. During
the evening he won the 15-inch axe handicap from
scratch; and with partner, Ned Higgins (Hornsby),
severed a log three times with the saw before two
other competitors could bite through the same thick-
ness once with the axe. Mclntosh and Higgins hold
the Australian sawing record for 18-inch logs
(23sec.), put up at Sydney in 1915.
Fred Jennings, who has the walking
mania, is again on the track. On Saturday he will
stroll it from Hill End goldfield to Bathurst, over 51
miles of mountain country, the time limit being 10
hours. This will be his most solid effort to date, as
the figures represent an average of nearly 51 miles
an hour. On the following Saturday Jennings moves
per boot from Bathurst ■ towards Sydney, 125 miles.
He starts at 5 a.m., and hopes to pull up at the
G.P.O. at 11 next morning.
From his 18th to his 21st year Ronald
Opie, killed the other day in a motor-cycle accident at
Christchurch (M.L.) stood right out on his own
among the sprinters over yonder. There were pro-
bably few peds of his time anywhere in the world
who could give him a start on the gun. He was
something in the way of a freak. Opie first came
into the limelight by annexing the 100, 220 and
440 yards at the University championship' meeting in
1909. At the same meeting in the following year
lie did the same thing again, setting up new records
for the two shorter distances. In 1911 at the M.L.
championship gathering held at Christchurch Opie
appropriated the three distahces some more, running
the 220yds. in 22sec. Later in the year he took
the 100yds., 220yds, and 440yds. at the Australasian
championship meeting (100yds. in 9 4-osec.). Sent
to England to compete at the Festival of Empire
Sports he had bad luck. Shortly after arrival lie
beat the Polytechnic crack, D’Arcy, in a scratch
100yds. at Crewe —‘but soon after had to throw up
training on. account of damaging one of his thighs,
and at tlic big meeting had only a leg and a half
to run on. Even with this handicap he got a second
and third. After returning home Opie got married
and except for taking an occasional championship
settled down to his job of school teaching. He hit
the tape at 27.
* * * •*
Off to U.S. the other day, “Snowy” Baker.
His fancy lightly turns on doing “stunts” for the
movies nowadays. He takes with him some potted
episodes of Australia, in which himself can be fre-
quently detected and he threatens to keep the
camera man busy over yonder. Meanwhile the
temples of bash in which he is interested are
waiting till peace gives an opening for the resump-
tion of hostilities, and the devastating effect of
the left hook. When that glad time arrives Snowy
hopes to appear at the old address.
C. Walker (Marrickville), winner of the
Cyclists’ Union 40-mile road championship at Enfield
(Sydney) on Saturday, is the fastest Ma State
pedaller out of khaki. A fortnight ago he gave
long starts and won the Marrickville 10-mile handi-
cap (from scratch) in 28min. 40sec. Also, he has
ticked up an array of fastest times during the
season. Walker did the 40 miles rush in 2hr. 3min.
35 2-ssec. Closest to him at the terminus were
J. R. O’Brien (Marrickville), and veteran Dick
Munro (Burwood-Enfield). The Union’s idea in pro-
moting these dust-raising episodes is to infuse life
into a fizzling pastime.

“Machete”: Let me censure “0.K.” (B.
29/8/’18) for stating that curling, Scotland’s own par-
ticular game, is a “Solemn dissipation,” consisting of
“giving a gentle push” to “polished iron weights”
and contains no feature worthy of the name of sport.
The usual curling stone (not a polished iron weight,
please) weighs from 22 to 30 pounds, and many
players use them heavier. If “0.K.” were to put
in an afternoon lifting these trifles from the' ice
and swinging them off from a stooping position, I
think he would alter his mind as to the game’s
athletic possibilities. As to being “solemn”-—well,
a curling match can give golf a long start and beat
it for embittered language. There is danger, too,
of catching cold or from sitting down suddenly.
“Pick”: The rifle performances they write
home about are sometimes mere matters of luck,
but the constable, a former African game-hunter,
and Zulu war veteran, who in the ’nineties had
charge of the Jugiong police station, was willing at
all times to back his bullet. For a wager he once,
with a sporting rifle, split a pipe bowl at 100yds.
That performance led to an argument about long
distance shots, whereupon the John Hop pointed to a
cockatoo 600yds. away and then assassinated the
bird with a Martini-Henry.
“Nimrod”: Recent talk . about long-range
shooting with shot-guns gives me a pain. The
function of the shot-gun is to kill moving
game at short distances. It will do this
cleanly arid certainly up to 40 or 50 yards.
Beyond that it cannot be depended on to do
more that! wound, except by a fluke. Amongst
real shooting men, anyone who starts talking about
long shots is put’down at once as an outsider. If a man
wants to shoot at long ranges, let him get a rifle
and learn to use it. Even then lid ought to know
that the aft of game-stalking consists in the hunter
getting close enough to his game to make sure of it,
not to wound it with pot shots at long range.

The National Coursing Association wound
up its season last week with the annual picnic
meeting—and everything went wrong. It was blazing'
hot the first day till lunch time. Then .heavy rain
fell. On the second day one of the heaviest hail-
storms noticed around Sydney for a long time tran-
spired. The principal stake was the Hart Memorial-
Stakes —a hew name for the old Bookmakers’ Purse.
The field for this event numbered only 20, and after
a number of uneventful ruffs Vera White won from
Donald Dihnie. The last pair left in the Hart
Memorial for the dogs beaten in the first round of
the Stakes were Gold Beauty and Gipsy Prince, a
pair of London Swell-Gipsv Belle puppies. As they
were running in the same ownership thev divided
The Riverstone Stakes, an all-aged event,' was won
by the much-travelled Shy Mary, who came over
from Melbourne. Hope Australia, the winner of this
year’s Oaks, was a runner, but after a beautiful
course in the first round she went to pieces in the
first ties and was easily beaten by Tarcisius, who in
turn was beaten by Shy Mary. The August Stakes
fell to What-a-Wonder, an aptly-named pup bv
Wheatley Wonder(imp.)-Fanny Brown.
“Nor’-West” : The fish of blood-red color,
referred to by “Trist” (B. 22/8/’18), is the golden
carp, and is to be found in all the north-western
rivers, billabongs and creeks. It is a mistake to
attribute the mortality among the other finny resi-
dents of the Barwon and Mclntyre to this fish. True,
the carp laoors under an evil reputation for eating
the spawn of cod and other fish, but it is not car-
nivorous otherwise. Both gold and grey species have
multiplied to a great extent during the last five or
six years, and during a dry spell can be seen in
great shoals in the rivers. They are supposed to
have been put into the headwaters of the Gwydir
and Mclntyre in mistake for trout. The golden
variety is a beautifully-tinted fish—bright seavlei
on the back, shading down to orange and brass-
yellow on the sides. They are utterly worthless as
food. I have seen a few, mostly caught in drum
nets, up to two pounds’ weight,' but in the shoals
they are much smaller. Occasionally I hsye caught
a carp in the Gwydir with worm bait, but they very
rarely bite, and, having a very small mouth, they
are difficult to. catch with the hook. The flesh makes
good bait for night-lines for old-man cod.
“Chev.” : Poor old Hackenschmidt! That
par. from “M.P.A.” (B. 22/8/’18) gives the first
news I’ve had of an old pal since 1916. ( was then in
a military hospital in England, and a bad case. • One
day brought me a big square envelope, stamped all
over, postmarked Berlin and endorsed “passed by
Censor.” The familiar writing was good to see.
“Interned,” T said to myself; but, after telling me
how he had learned from a London Sketch (evidently
they got our illustrated weeklies in Berlin) that
I had been seriously wounded, and expressing sym-
pathy, Hack, told me that the Germans were making
much use of him. They had made him director of
a large sonneh-bad (an open-air sun-bathed enclosure)
on the outskirts of Berlin, where children were given
athletic exercise—Swedish drill, figure marching,
dumb-bells, clubs, and so on. He enclosed a picture
post-card of this sonnen-bad, and, on it, all Sparsely
clad, but virile, healthy and in fine condition, there
appeared hundreds of children exercising in the
full glare of the sun. Hack, hinted that the work
was heavy, but affirmed he was well treated. He
was allowed to live. in a flat in the city, but had
to report himself twice weekly to the police. There
was nobody in the world so thoroughly capable for
the job the Germans found for him. I wrote two
brief and plain letters to Hack, in reply, but botli
were “returned by Censor,” who was very careful
these days, as now. You see, in spite of the German
flavor in his name, Hackenschmidt was Russian,
born at University town of Dorpat. In 1909 i
spent a month’s holiday with him in Germany,
where we met a young medical student. Raffan bv
name, from Sydney. Wonder if he recalls a night
in the ton-halle at. a very military town, when the
very German comedian made disparaging references
to der Englische? And' the “certain liveliness” that
followed. In the years ago Hackenschmidt gave mo
a gold watch, inscribed, “To Dick from George.”
It is one of the few treasures I saved from the
mined Mongolia. I was wearing it when she struck.

Newcastle Surf Club and Life Saving-
Brigade has 183 of its members grappling in the
Big Undertow. It claims this to be a record for any
single - sporting club in Australia. What Manly
surfmen have to say to this will transpire later.
The Newcastle club is 11 years old.

He said he’d do me 3 to 2.
“It’s 6 to 4 except to you.”
So I took the bet.
He’s running yet
The nag I backed. I fear it lacked
Persuasion. Yet, perchance had smacked
It been before the riders of the other mokes
Began to coax
More speed with’whip and spur,
That crossbred, loafing chestnut cur
Might have been noticed at the line
And filled those waiting hands of mine
From off the trusting Hebrew,
Who
Laid me the odds of 3 to 2
When “Thikth tuf’or, I can’t do more,”
Was the ringmen’s yelp on the loother.

To save our soldiers and sailors out of the enemies
claws;
To pray' that we’ll soon see the end of this clause,
So as we’ll see them return in the best of gauze.
Oh! May that our prayers be heard for our boys at
the front,
So as to enable them to carry on to settle the enemies
wont
That we’ll pray for them to stand all the blunt
So as we’ll know the enemies as only a runt.
Oh! May our British peoples all pray to the High
Heaven,
To ease their burdens of sorrow and sufferings.;
So as we will hear that sweet Tintinubulation.
That our prayers have been heard with thank-offerings.

“ Jacko”: Concerning The Bulletin’s
observations upon the preaching of economy
and the practising of waste by State Minis-
ters, let me add the fact that Ball, N. S.
Wales Minister for Works, recently spent a
few days coursing in a Government car
over the territory to be served some day,
perhaps, by th<j Tarana-Oberon railway, a
proposition that lias already been approved
fully, and which years ago was at the stage
of awaiting for the elusive cash for construc-
tion. Yet Ball had to travel all the way
from Sydney, spending good coin to tell the
poor cockies that there wasn’t any coin to
spend. James, Minister for Education, , was
in the same district the same week-end on a
similar jaunt, and all he had to say was
that the badly-needed High School hadn’t a
ghost of a show —in the scramble, by impli-
cation, with Ministerial joy-rides.

Influenza
It is at this time of the year, when the
weather is very changeable, hot one day and
cold the next, that colds are caught and
influenza becomes prevalent.
Influenza invariably leaves the patient
weak and with thin blood, and this anaemia
is~Very stubborn in resisting treatment. It
mast be corrected, however, before any cure
can be considered permanent. As long as
the blood remains thin there will continue
the,relapses with which most sufferers from
influenza are familiar. Warmth and quiet
alone give comfort, and these not for long at
a time. Sleep is restless and does not re-
fresh the nerves which are always at high
tension.
The best way to correct this after-effect
of influenza is to build up the blood, and
there is no better blood-builder than Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.,
As soon;as the revitalised blood courses
through the system you are aware of its
soothing influence. Gradually the color re-
turns to the pale cheek, appetite and diges-
tion improve and you are on the road to
health.
Any reader of this paper may have a
useful booklet bn “Diseases of the Blood” by
writing to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Box 59.4, G.P.0., Sydney.

“Mustard Gold”: Sydney and Melbourne
are congested places, but they are hardly in
it with the two diminutive townships of
Boulder Block and Gwalia Block in AATst-
ralia. Both were slices of surplus land
remaining after the surveyor’s lines had
extracted order from the chaos of leases
hurriedly staked in the prospecting days ol
the fields, and smart men immediately con-
verted them into freeholds.. The Boulder
fraction, known as “The Dirty Acre,” has
six pubs, four barbers’ shops, several grocer-
ies and draperies, and a shooting-gallery
crowded on to it. Gwalia Block failed to get
a pnb, after many efforts in the Leonora
Licensing Court; but the deficiency is made
up by a crowded collection of establishments,
including three draperies—one a large, ramb-
ling emporium with fabrics arranged in wild
profusion about the footpath after the
fashion of an Oriental rag bazaar —halt a
dozen cool-drink joints, two groceries, a
billiard saloon, and a haircutting ranch bear-
ing the proud boast, “Late of Paris and
Boulder Block.”
“Chap” : “Bad Bill” (B. 8/8/T8) is very
bad indeed when he says the AVestminster
dustmen have borrowed the shape of Bill-
jim’s hat. The hat is on the other head, for
the dustmen wore this shape in millinery as
far back as 1851. And it is just as well
known as the shawl and apron of the flower-
girls of Covent Garden.
“Bad Australian,” from Sierra Leone
(AVest Africa) :
This aggregation of crossbreed “comeback” and
straight nigger enjoys so much freedom that there
is nothing left over for the white sojourner within
its picturesque but insanitary gates. Free Town
is an eloquent testimonal to what may happen under
a well-meaning grandmotherly Government acting
in absolute ignorance of the right way to deal
with black brudder, if good results are looked for.
The British Government, out of its exceeding great
love for the poor benighted Ethiopian, started this
nondescript crowd on the road to liberty with a
full civilising outfit, including very decent schools
where the son of Ham is converted from good nigger
(if a nigger ever is good) into d —d poor teacher,
doctor, lawyer and somewhat corrupt magistrate.
Ev;en the common or bush coon has been so pam-
pered and protected in his aggressive insoleiice
toward the ordinary white that he is fully persuaded
that he is quite as good as, and in some cases a great
deal better than, any of the detested breed. Acting on
this notion he shows very little respect to his white
boss, and his language in reply to a peremptory
order is usually painful and frequent and free. If
the white man should deal faithfully with that nigger
and belt seven kinds of slieol out of him, he is
immediately grabbed by five or six black police and
yanked befo e a nigger magistrate, who, after giving
the plaint! (' and his discolored counsel a patient
and friendl) hearing, proceeds to land the mis-
guided while with the heaviest penalty on the
statutes.
The ordinary buck earns from 3s. to 6s. per day
and his “chop,” and when he sheds his working
suit, which usually consists of a gaudy but dilapi-
dated loin rag and a dirty over-ripe clay-pipe worn
bowl downwards, and puts on his glad rags, he is
just the juiciest cockroach on the Coast. He struts
into the best bar in the settlement, and flashing his
brummy “diamond” ring and wrist watch, orders and
consumes his poison on terms of equality with any
whites who happen to be taking their nourishment
at the same stand. The white man’s mana is at a
distinct discount in Sierra Leone.
“Bill Bowyang”: Chewing cordite is
becoming a dangerous habit amongst muni-
tion-workers in England. I met a young
man in Cairo yesterday who had just re-
turned from a small-arms factory near Bir-
mingham. Like many others, he at first
thought chewing the nitro-glycerine and gun-
cotton a harmless pastime; but eventually
it secured such a hold on him that his health
was affected, and he had to return to his
home in Egypt. He informed me that those
who take up the habit stick to it, for they
become so reliant upon the deadly stimulant
that they dare not drop it. It is one of those
drugs which must be used in ever-increasing
quantities if the “inspiring” effects on the
nervous system aye to be maintained; and
this means a decreasing moral and physical
strength.
“Bill Bowyang”: Those Australians (and
they are many) who threaten to settle down
in 'Palestine when the war is over would do
Well to consider the matter seriously before
they take tip land. For centuries the coun-
try has been denuded of its trees, with dis-
astrous consequences, and the heavy rains
at certain seasons, instead of now benefiting
the soil over more than four-fifths of its
area, carry away in rushing torrents down
the wadies and the many streams which tem-
porarily pour down the hillsides and ancient
terraces much of the little soil that the
almost sterile high lands and valley slopes
have left on them. An expert in forestry
and irrigation explained to me yesterday
how the absence of trees tends to reduce such
a country as Palestine to barrenness. If
the ancient forests were restored they would
form a catchment for the. rainfall, and in
time, by the decay of their leaves, form a
humus soil beneath which would be invalu-
able. The Australian eucalyptus is a tree
which might be utilised, and fruit and olive
trees could also be planted, Another impor-
tant measure would be the storage of water,
not by gigantic engineering enterprises, but
by the simple process of erecting small dams
across the higher reaches of wadies. This
work would create a series of small lakes or
reservoirs of from 500,000 to 2,000,000 gal-
lons of water that would last through the
dry season and add needed moisture to the
air. The replanting of the country will be
retarded if goats are not deported or kept in
compounds, for they devour every green
shoot available. All these schemes can-
not be allowed to overgrow the supply
of labor. On this point experience alone
can decide whether requirements will be
met by the Jews and the Arab population
adapting itself to the new conditions. The
country now possesses an advantage it never
had before in railway connection with
Egypt, where all the surplus agricultural
small crops, grapes and vegetables will find
a ready market in the growing Egyptian
cities and the shipping upon the Suez Canal.
For many years to come farming will be in
the hands of the Arabs, and it will be no
place for an Australian unless he opens a
pub in Jerusalem for the benefit of the tour-
ists who are going to flock to the Holy City
when Mars skies the towel.

Another of our Gallant Heroes Praises
REXONA
m
■ ffivHP
i
*3
SA* "*
CORPORAL
R. J. STEVENS
(from a photo).
Corporal R. J. Stevens was the first Inverell
soldier to enlist. He sacrificed an independent
business at the call of duty and enlisted on the 10th
September, 1914, leaving Australia with the First
Division. After three years’ continuous service
he returned severely wounded in foot, wrist and
body. His mother, he says, is his only sweetheart,
and for her sake and the sake of his sisters he
hopes to go back to the Front again.
Dear Sir, —
I have much pleasure in saying that during
my three years’ active service in Egypt, Anzac
and France I constantly used your Rexona
Ointment for cuts and sores of all kinds
with wonderful results. While helping to save
an ammunition dump from burning on Anzac
Beach in May, 1915, I was severely burned
about the hands and face. As I had a supply
of your wonderful Rexona in my kit I was
able to obtain instant relief. You may be
surprised to learn that I always carry your
Rexona Ointment in my pocket. The soap,
too, is perfect.
Wishing you every success,
1 am, yours faithfully,
CPL. R, J. STEVENS,
Ist Anzac Mounted Division.
First at the Front and
First at Home.
The above is only one of the many letters we have received in praise of Rexona
the Rapid Healer from our boys at the Front. They all use Rexona, and find it
unequalled as a soothing- and rapid healing Ointment for the terrible wounds and
skin sores which they so often suffer from.
Rexona is first at the Front and first at home. The ever-ready friend of
all. Sold only in triangular pots at 1/6; or large pots, holding nearly
four times the quantity, for 3/-
