Autumn Lovers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I KNOW the place they went with olden
feet
On crackling leaves below the branches
bare;
The kiss they kissed was very warm and
sweet
In the sharp autumn air.
Old were the trees; the moss about them
clung
A hundred summers ere those lovers came
Lilting old songs, with voices very young,
And whispering name and name.
An echo started from an old grey wall
Beside a broken hedge and twisted gate,
A hoarse old echo with a faltering call
Following the words too late.
Surely the world seemed all too old for love
When those old steps went very lightly by;
Half-hid in mist a cheerless sun above
Hung in a cold, grey sky.
Now is it older by a thousand Springs,
And yet the branch and bough are Autumn
bare,
That strange, cold place deserted of all wings ;
And love can' still come there.
Spring is for nesting, summer for the brood,
Autumn for lonely seeking without song,
And winter for the memory of good
That could not last too long.
But love goes whispering with the Autumn
leaf,
Its happiest dreams of blossoming and
Spring.
Love—and a thousand years of bloom and
sheaf
Leave it a song to sing!
cannot find the little graves they filled
I'nder a coverlet of churchyard moss ;
A drift of yellow leaves is lightly spilled
Where once stood cross and cross.
Their world was old—how old and far
away!—■
Yet as they walked they heard the footsteps
fall
Of other lovers in an older day
Beyond their dreams’ recall.
And now I hear them walk upon my heart—
Dry drift of leaves from autumns of regret—
Those lovers, talking in the grey apart,
That life will not forget.
Still I remember—nay, I never knew
The whispered words those older lovers said ;
Yet as we pass I say them o’er to vou,
Oh, sweet and wise brown head !
Sweet and most wise; for wisdom lives in
this
Through all dead leaves of years trod under-
foot :
The sacramental wonder of a kiss
Is God’s red summer fruit.
X.S.AV.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
His Sixpenny Bed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
little clergyman standing in the
shadow of the mission doorway was
very much annoyed. “You ought to be
thoroughly ashamed of yourself!’’ he chat-
tered in his quaint, old-maidish way, and
shook an admonishing finger at the surly
deadbeat rocking in busted boots out in the
sun. “To go and break the pledge in this
way within two days of taking it! You
ought to be ashamed of yourself!’’
“I’m orright!” gurgled the sinner, and, by
way of proving it, collapsed in a limp heap.
“Dear, dear!” sighed the little clergyman,
and laid helping hands on the feebly-strug-
gling sinner.
“I'm orright—tell -yer ! Leave me ’lone !
I get—up meself!”
The little clergyman, by this time very
red in the face with his exertions, put his
hands under the patient’s arms, and with a
tremendous effort dragged him gradually to
his feet. “There—you—are !” he gasped.
“Now come inside and go to bed ! You are
not fit to be out in the street!”
“ Won’t gorrer bed !” He reeled away a
few steps.
"Williams, if you dare to come back here
any worse than you are, I shall not let you
in.”
“Don’t warrer come in !”
“If you drink any more I shall report you
to the directors, and the committee, and have
you forbidden the Mission under Rule 79.”
The drunk, by way of reply, waved his
hand contemptuously and zig-zagged down
the street.
* ■ . * *• *
The distinguished English visitor stood at
one of the fourth-floor windows of the
famous hotel. The manager was humbly in
attendance to answer any questions the dis-
tinguished visitor or the distinguished visi-
tor’s wife might care to ask him.
“And what is that big building over
there?” murmured her Ladyship.
The manager stroked his moustache doubt-
fully. “That is now T used as a mission-hall
and cheap lodging-house, your Ladyship. I
think it is unfortunate it should have been
placed there. The work, of course, is neces-
sary, but I think it would have been wiser
if the church people had taken a place in a
side street. It brings an undesirable type
about the place.”
“You ought to try to shift them,” yawned
his Lordship, dragging at his moustache. “It
shouldn’t be impossible to move them —these
religious people will do anything for you if
you give them some money.”
“Well, we’ve done our best: but there are
some rich people behind that particular
mission, and one of them owns the building.
I can assure you I would give something to
have them out of it.”
His Lordship yawned as if the discussion
had quite emptied his head of ideas. “Oh,
well, I s’pose it can’t be helped. Damn
nuisance, though!”
A little later the distinguished visitors,
who really weren’t distinguished but were
merely idle strangers with a lot of money
they hadn’t earned, w T ent down in the- nit
and strolled out to inspect the city and see
if it would do. They promenaded down the
steep hill that led to the busy portion, and
there, while waiting for a chance to ge
through the traffic, his Lordship had the
dreadful experience that convinced him the
colonies were seething w. 'li Socialism, l ust
of all he was bumped violently trom behind
bumped so violently that he dropped .his
cane and had his panama hat knocked over
his eyes. Then, immediately after, came
the insulting query, “Why fell earn t yei
gitouter th’ way?” His Lordship turned
like a maddened sheep and discovered a
ruinous and besotted individual swaying on
his feet. “You bounder! I’ve a good mind
to give you in charge!”
“Ah-h, get work, yer immergrant.
sneered the disastrous person, and reeled
away just in time to avoid trouble.
The distinguished visitors proceeded on
their way. But their first morning had been
ruined for them by their unfortunate experi-
ence and they could find no good in any-
thing, #
The distinguished visitors had been gone
half an hour or so, and the manager of the
tall hotel was just about to tackle some
office work after his morning round of in-
spection, when he saw a little, black-coated
figure dive out of the mission building and
make straight for his side of the street. He
thought the little clergyman was hound
down-town as usual and would hurry past;
but he was astonished to see him turn into
the palm-decorated hall and rendered speech-
less when the brawny hall-porter knocked
and asked if he could see the Rev. Mr. Sav-
ing. of the Back Street Mission opposite.
“Yes, show him in,” growled the manager,
and the next minute the Rev. Mr. Saving
was seated before him.
“I’m afraid my business is not very
pleasant, Mr. —er —”
“Billsway’s my name.’’
The little clergyman, twiddled his curious
Hat hat nervously. “I wanted to appeal to
you. Mr. Billsway—to appeal to your better
feelings, sir, on behalf of the poor fellows
we are trying to help.”
“I’m sorry, but I can’t do anything for
you just now. All appeals for donations
have to be referred to our Board, and”—
“It is not a donation I want,. Mr. Bills-
way !”
“No?"
“No: it is this. I want you not to make
drink so attractive. This splendid monu-
ment of drink has a terrible influence on my
poor fellows! What use is it to preach the
wisdom of temperance when here we have a
sort of garish palace of drink luring them
on to destruction?”
That started the trouble fairly, and
presently the two principals were kicking up
such a row, and the staff were so engrossed
trying to overhear something, that the reel-
ing scarecrow, who had been watching tlm
entrance from the other side of the road ever
since the little clergyman dived in, drifted in
without let or hindrance and went upstairs.
If he had hesitated, he would have been
lost for a certainty; but, greatly daring, he
went right in and up without a second’s
pause—which would seem to indicate that his
hazy, alcoholic mind was filled with a definite
idea.
The manager’s room still contained a pas-
sionate duet when the distinguished couple
arrived back from the visit of inspection. They
made thqjr weary way straight to the lift
and were whisked upward to the cool and
quiet regions above. Two minutes later
shrill sounds of rage and panic were heard
aloft, the lift shot down, swallowed the head
waiter and shot up again.
But the panic sounds grew louder, and
the lift, with a horrified minion in charge,
slid down once more, engulfed the brawny hall-
porter and tore up with him. Presently loud
shouts and thumps were heard in addition
to the original shrieks, which had not sub-
sided. Then the flying lift came down again
like a hurried angel, and when the lift per-
son dashed back the iron screen, his Lord-
ship bounded out like a released lion and,
dashing to the manager’s office, beat upon
the closed door with both hands.
“What’s the matter?” shouted the en-
raged Billsway, as the door flew open. Then,
as he noticed who the intruder w T as, “Oh!
your Lordship! Is anything wrong?”
“Wrong!” screamed the nobleman. *“You
come to my room, sir! Just come up and
see what’s in my room ! By Gad, I’ll w T rite
to the Times about this! I’ll show up this
place!”
“Why, your Lordship! What is it? If
there’s anything I can do”— —
“Do!” screamed his Lordship, dancing in
the hall. “By Gad, if you don’t do some-
thing, I’ll —Oh, lieah, damn it all, you just
come and see what it is!”
They grabbed each other by the coat
sleeve and ran to the waiting lift. The
door slammed on them and they shot up
where uproar and bad language awaited
them.
By this time the downstairs portion of
the hotel was buzzing. The little clergyman
hovered nervously in the hall, and presently
the insatiable lift swooped down again and
the white-faced minion frantically becko c’d
him in and rushed him aloft till the white
upturned faces of the hotel employees below
were lost in the gloom. At the fourth floor
the lift stopped with a jerk, the collapsmlo
door clashed back, and the little clergyman
was shoved into the midst of a free fight.
A swaying, roaring figure in an elaborate
white nightgown decked with blue ribbons
was struggling in the grip of a distinguished
military officer of high rank (the hall-porter)
and a nobleman in evening dress (the head
waiter), while his enemy, the hotel manager
was arguing at the top of his voice with a
thin, fair-haired man who wanted to write
to the Times without a moment’s delay. In
the background was a tearful lady who
wanted to know' if something couldn’t be done
about it. She,, it seemed, was the owner of
the nightgown.
As the little clergyman stood surveying this
scene the manager turned on him. shouting:
“You see what you’ve done? He went to
sleep in his Lordship’s bed ! Get the filthy
brute out of that nightgown and take him
away with you.”
‘ Williams!” shrilled Mr. Saving.

Daily Mishaps
ACCIDENTS are always happening in the
kitchen, in the dining-room, in the garden,

But TV llliams, struggling with his captors,
gi unted : “I’ve paid me sixpence, I tell yer.
I won’t be chucked out!”
The Rev. Mr. Saving laid hands on his
sinner and shouted: “You wretched fellow,
this isn’t the Mission !”
But rage and drink had obscured the men-
tal vision of the straggler. “I —tell yer—
paid me sixpence! If it ain’t th’ —Mission,
why you ’ere?”
“The drunken brute thinks lie’s in that den
over the way,” stormed his Lordship. “This
is the last time I’ll stay in this place. I
won’t live with vagrants 1”
Billsway flinched. “I’m sorry, your Lord-
ship, but”
The high soprano of her Ladyship rang
out above the shouting. “Oh, don’t let the
creature split it! Stop him kicking in it!”
“ Williams!” shrieked the little clergyman,
dodging the flying boots of the prisoner.
"\\ illiams, if you don't give up that garment
at once and come away with me quietly, I
shall report you to the directors and the com-
mittee and”
“Oh, don’t waste time forkin’!” growled
the major-general hall-porter. “ ’Old ’is legs
an’ I’ll soon ’ave ’im out of it!”
The Rev. Mr. Saving jumped forward like
a sparrow to obey ; but at that instant the
to.o-eontident porter relaxed his grip and
Williams broke away and dashed with the
bewildering speed of the determined drunk
at the stairway, lie fell with a crash on the
first landing, was pounced on by the porter,
fought him off with his feet and cascaded
down the next flight, where the same disas-
trous performance was gone through, to the
great detriment ,of her Ladyship’s expensive
nightgown. The rest of the company fol-
lowed the running fight down in the lift,
shouting useless advice between floors. But
they were in time to witness the last shameful
scuffle in the splendid hall, when the enraged
and fight-sobered ' Williams threw the hall-
porter heavily for the last time and, decora-
ted with ribbons and strips of fine linen, sped
out the front door, closely followed by the
little clergyman, who, as he ran, threatened
him carefully with Rule 79 and the everlast-
ing displeasure of the directors and the com-
mittee.

Charity.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BESSIE LYND dragged herself down
the sodden road from the tragedy of
her desertion by McNair, numbed by
the wind and sleet, but dead in heart and
brain except for a pitiful reiteration : “How
could he? How could he?”
Her one comfort had been that none in
the Valley knew her secret. Yet, unknown
to her, everybody but her stepmother and her
father had known of her meetings with
McNair, and thought they foresaw the result.
Returning from the scene of her brutal
desertion she felt only cold, for her self-
respect had been lost long before in the
necessity for rehabilitation at the hands of
such a man. The languor of her condition
becajne exaggerated by the utter dreariness
of the day. There seemed to be waiting for
her in the Valley all the gossiping and malevo-
lence of a one-street township—gossiping
not blindly, for McNair had taken a
companion into confidence, and this
savage had publicly laughed over McNair’s
intentions as to marriage as a thing splen-
didly humorous. And the women who had
been called together by the repetition of the
excellent joke gossiped in bunches and said
to each other, “There she is,” with never a
thought of pity for the limping wretchedness
that came to them through the drizzle
searched by the icy fingers of the wind.
When she saw them the last flicker of
pride came to give her courage. She lifted
her head a little higher and limped past them
silently while the whispers grew: “Did you
see? Didn’t I tell you?”
Somehow she reached the home that was
never home, and in momentary terror
gripped the fence before entering the garden.
There was a brugmansia with one late
trumpet upon it. A remorse which is the
fear of punishment came to her. And then
she reflected that nobody knew yet—at least,
none but the uncertain gossips of the one
street, and certainly nobody who could
matter.
Most people who live next a cathedral
never see the cathedral. The hovel crouched
in the shadow of the pyramid knows not the
pyramid. Use has the property of conceal-
ing vital facts longest from the people who
are most interested. Bessie had limped all
her life. A little limping more or less, a
duller languor, said nothing to the nigh-two-
himdred-pounds weight of selfishness which
was the second Mrs. Lynd—drugged with
sweets and the marvellous heroines of “Maria
Corley.”
The awakening of Mrs. Lynd called for a
blow with a sledge-hammer; and she had
received it that afternoon at the hands of
Miss Cloudy, the unpublished poetess, who
had been a virgin for so long that the
memory of: man knoweth not to the contrary.
When Bessie limped into the living-room
of the house she saw that all was known.
The word that drives even the harlot frantic
was spat at her. She did not dare to stop
and defend herself. She tried to run away,
and ended by limping quickly to the street
and up tfi the Higher Valley. She had no
plans, but the gate of the parsonage gave
one.
The wife of the Reverend Malachi Honey-
bee had heard much of the story. The
appearance of the girl confirmed all of it.
“As a -ifibral woman I must condemn her
fault,” said. Mrs. Honeybee. “As a Christian
I must forgive it.”
So Bessie stayed at the parsonage—a
something between a servant and a sinner.
Christian feeling made them give her shelter.
Outraged: virtue made them give her work
which purifies the soul, but no wages which
might tempt the body. So she had drabness
and winter, scrubbing and laundering—work
she had never learned, and now had to learn,
being fage to face with the Four Streets of
Poverty, which are Hunger, Cold, Waiting
and Contumely. She took it gladly, because
it dulled her out of thinking; but it did not
bring her sleep at night, because the future
was uncertain. So for the free horse—more
work though it be ever so lame; more work
and all the High Moralities.
Windy Jimmy Kelly, the publican—-once a
Californian of ’forty-nine, and therefore not
cousin to the snuffle-busters who made up
most of the people of the Valley, had
married McNair’s mother. His romance
had been suffered so late in life that
it had been without fastidiousness. When
the rumors about Bessie crystallised into a
coherent story, Tony Barfoot and Windy
Jimmy were almost too ashamed to talk of it.
“It ain't true,” said Kelly, when at last
somebody else introduced the subject. And
when the man had gone Tony Barfoot leaned
across the fireplace and tapped his friend
on the knee that wasn’t gouty.
“By God, Jimmy,” said he, “ ’tis true —’tis
true enough. It’s nacheral, an’ if it’s all
nacher, she’ll get stripes for breakin’
parsons’ law.”
“It ain’t parsons’ law—it’s right’s law.
It's ’ard an’ it’s ne’ssary. Y’ must make the
women scared, or the kids have got no
chance.”
“Anyway, I’d like to meet the man who
cheated her.”
And McNair’s mother, who knew, went out
very quietly as she heard him. A fortnight
later she died, giving little trouble to any—
after telling Kelly that her son Adam was
the man who deserted the girl. And
Kelly cursed his wife's sons and all their
works.
“Look to him,” said Tony Barfoot to his
wife. “Poor Jimmy Kelly—that was a
world-beater of a man. After he marries he
keeps his wife’s sons, an’ their kids, an’
their dorgs, and they have the run of his
bar, an’, if he tries to keep them away from
the til], they ups and slays him with a pick-
handle, or says they will.”
But a week after his wife’s death Windy
Jimmy Kelly took up all the strings of his
occupation as he had held them before his
marriage, and, being miraculously free of
gout, be made a sudden descent on John
McNair. This gentleman, a highly-disre-
spected cadger, had appointed himself his
mother’s residuary legatee, and translated
the duties of that position into the fattening
of himself on beer, and the eating of the
widower’s substance like some great and
voracious grasshopper on green barley. The
old prospector’s eyes blazed in fury. He fell
on John McNair and beat him so that he
ever after avoided Kelly’s as if it were a
quarantine ground.
This exercise made the avenger feel much
better, and he walked to the parsonage for
Bessie. Mrs. _ Honeybee, however, detained
him to talk in shocked tones of Bessie’s
crime, and of her condition, which, to Mrs.
Honeybee, became more shameful with time
because it became more apparent. She spoke
of it as being an unnatural event, and
one never before heard of, with the shocked
air of a Divorce Court Judge pretending
that he knows naught of human nature. She
told also of her own kindness in the provi-
sion of an asylum for Bessie, judiciously
-stressing the moralities, and keeping
secret the drudgery and the absence of
wages. Finally, and with that deadly
seriousness which is the testimonial of
strong respectability to itself, she said
that she had told Bessie that day to
find another home, as her husband’s position
demanded that she should give no counte-
nance to immorality.
Mr. Kelly said naught to her but “Yes,
mum,” and “No, mum,” but he regarded her
with a stare of wonder, so that she had the
grace to feel a little pity for her sister in
distress and a very little shame for herself.
She even promised to send Bessie’s box to
Mr. Kelly’s next day.
“If the Church turns her down,” said
Windy Jimmy, “the pub is ready to take her
up.”
Bessie, carrying a great bundle, joined Mr.
Kelly on the verandah. He took the bundle
from her, gave her his arm and led the way.
Her lameness was so exaggerated that it
became a disfigurement. She leaned heavily
on his friendly arm. The gaunt old man
looked down on her and said, with the pity
of four-score for the sorrows of twenty
years, “Poor little thing.”
At that rare tone of kindliness she began
to cry softly—not bitterly, but as if betrayed
to it by his sympathy and her own weakness.
“There, there, don’t cry—or do, just’s you
like,” said Mr. Kelly. Then he looked down
again at the limping figure and said'as if he
were alone:
“Poor little thing! It's a damned shame!
Poor little thing!”
That night the gossips had a new
subject. “What do you think? Did y’
ever hear such a thing? A single girl an’'a
widower, an’ she in trouble! Ah ! in my
young days girls were decent. Many a
time my mother give me a slap across the
face”
But up at Windy Jimmy Kelly’s a girl
began to learn to smile again ; awaiting her
harvest almost calmly, because she had
sanctuary.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
To-morrow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
~1 TOW many and how many threatening
I—l things
JL_l_ Lurk as we lightly mount and ride
away
Into the future from the known to-day?
What fate s whir over us on wheeling wings;
To what wild precipice the track-ledee
clings;
What gorge of windy darkness waits its prey •
What sorrow squats and what gaunt hopes
decay,
Below those uplands where to-morrow sings.
And yet we laugh along, or shout a brave
Chorus of challenge to the winds that rave,
The fates that circle and the hordes of
sorrow.
For though we have to rouse old Death and
ride
With that lank rattlebones to serve as guide,
We mean to climb those mountains of to-
morrow.
